B. Stage II: Improving Conveyance Efficiency
1)    Stage II physical works are aimed at improving conveyance
efficiency and water control throughout the system, from the
main canal down to the field channels. The first set of
schemes (Table 1) selected for Stage II in the first year
have begun the process of system improvements and
rehabilitation/ modernization investments under the ongoing
NWMP. Under the WRCP, these systems will receive selective
lining in critical reaches of the main canal, regulators and
some masonry structures. Flow measuring devices (automatic
stage recorders) will be provided at the head of major
distributaries to facilitate an eventual adoption of
volumetric supply of water. Other structures -- aqueducts,
diversion structures, pickup weirs, anicuts -- will be
repaired as per needs determined scheme by scheme. The
average cost of these improvements would be about Rs. 6000
per ha.
2)    The success of the proportionate dividers referred to
earlier depends on effective maintenance. Issues related to
maintenance to be addressed during Stage II include:
effective methods of deweeding; lining to prevent
proliferation of weeds; measures to reduce recurring
expenditures on sedimentation; and protection of completed
works from accidental or deliberate destruction. These
issues will be resolved in discussions between farmers and
the WRO. Where cost effective solutions are not evident,
research and development will be supported.2 In addition
to the civil works in Stage II, flow measuring devices with
automatic stage recorders will be provided to encourage
measurement of water supply and, in the long run, to
facilitate volumetric supply. In cases where lining results
in change in the design geometry of the canal prism and as a
result, water levels, suitable modifications or remodelling
of the corresponding masonry structures (regulators,
bridges, offtake and flumes) will be pursued in the project.
Distributary-level lining will be carried out only where FCs
are willing to contribute 20 per cent of the total cost
prior to the start of works.
2 The WRCP will support research and study programs of the Institute of
Hydraulics and Hydrology, Poondi; the Institute of Water Studies, Taramani; and
the Irrigation Management Training Institute, Trichy; and the Tamil Nadu Water
Resources Research Fund described elsewhere in this report.



-52 -
Annex 4
Page 10 of 22
3)    Five of the seven Stage II schemes will be taken up for
coverage under the FOT program in the first year of the
project (see Attachment B). Some of these schemes have
already begun to see indications of a more reliable and
equitable water services, yet distributary level works
remain to be done and hence are good candidates for the POT
program. The two remaining schemes, Tambiraparani and
Kodayar, will be taken up for FOT in the second year of the
project.
16.   System Tan*s.  Tanks are typically monozone earthen bunds built from
local soil with one or more surplussing arrangements. The water spread is
relatively large, with large fetch causing considerable wave heights. The
maximum height of tank bund is even up to 7 m. Most of the tanks are located
near villages. In case of breach, some tanks located near habitats can create
a potential danger to the population, as for example in the Ramnad Big Tank in
Vaigai System, Kadamba in Tamraparani System. During the cyclonic rainfall of
late 1993, many of the big tanks in Periyar Vaigai and elsewhere were
breached. Reasons were encroachment on the design flood lift to raise the
F.T.L.; inadequacy of the surplussing arrangements causing overtopping; lack
of maintenance, resulting in vertical depression in the tank bunds at
locations where cart tracks were made by villagers; and breach of a tank in
the upper reaches causing a chain reaction of breach in all the tanks located
downstream.
17.   The project will support renovation of some 80 system tanks once
detailed studies on some aspects of breaches and a review of design parameters
are completed. The review will include: assessment of the present approach of
calculating peak floods by empirical methods and its replacement by a more
rational alternative; stability analysis of upstream slopes (sudden drawdown
condition) and downstream slopes (heavy downpour condition, steady seepage
condition); provision of rock toe at downstream slopes; provision of
upstream rivetment; determination of the correct storage capacity of tanks by
accurate surveys; and provision of inclined filters at the downstream hearting
zone.
18.   Link Canal and related vorks in Periyar Vaigai.  In addition, the
project will also support rehabilitation/ modernization works in the Periyar
Vaigai system financed under two previous Bank credits, including completion
of the link canal in the Periyar system; construction of by-pass channels to
existing tanks; and capacity adjustments in offtake and masonry structures;
and repairs to four breached tanks.
On-farm development (OFDI works
19.   OFD provides the last link between the irrigation system and farmers'
fields through the construction of structures below the sluice, as for
example, watercourses and field channels. The project will support the
State's program of OFD works with changes from present practice. Under the



- 53 -
Annex 4
Page 11 of 22
WRCP, farmer organizations will decide on the need for OFD and assistance for
OFD will be extended only in outlets where FCs are organized. The WRO would
undertake topographical surveys, delineating the water course/field channel
alignments and construction of masonry/concrete structures or profile walls at
critical locations, spaced at about 75 to 100 meters. The rest of the field
channel system would be constructed by farmer organizations with their own
funds. Technical assistance, where necessary, will be extended by the WRO.
The cost to WRO would be about Rs. 700 per ha, as against the more than Rs.
3500 per ha as per present practice, where the government bears the full
burden of the OFD works.   With the start of the WRCP, the OFD model described
above will be Statewide and replace other existing approaches. The WRO's
share of the OFD works will be carried out by its own staff or contracted out
to other agencies such as the Agricultural Engineering Department (AED) or the
FCs.
Standardization
20.   Structures will be classified on the basis of their functional
requirements and identified by a specific code number: (i) flow measurement
structures (flumes); (ii) flow control and regulation structures (head
sluices, baffle distributors, undertunnels); (iii) cross drainage and related
structures (eg., aqueducts, syphons, drops); (iv) safety structures (eg.,
escapes, surplussing weirs in tanks); (v) service structures (eg., road
bridges, bathing areas); (vi) water level control structures (long-crested
weirs, cross regulators, composite regulators, and (vii) flow proportional
dividers.
21.   The glossary of terms used in investigations, planning, design and
construction of project works would follow the latest version of standards
published by the Bureau of Indian Standards (BIS) for river valley projects.
Publications of standards or practices of the Central Water Commission,
Central Bureau of Irrigation and Power (CBIP) and of international
organizations applicable to project works would also be consulted. Standards
of the Indian Road Congress of the GOI would be consulted for the construction
of project bridges. Specification of materials and works will be reviewed and
revised where necessary and the new versions will be furnished to the Bank for
comments before undertaking works. The procedures to be adopted for carrying
out repairs of rehabilitation works would be site specific. Nevertheless, the
relevant guidelines contained in well-known publications (e.g., CWC, ICID,USBR
manuals) would be used.
Farmer oraanization and turnover
22.   Three Important features of the WRCP approach to farmer organization
are:
a)    Implementation of organizing efforts will be closely integrated
with physical improvements to be funded through the project.
Existing O&M staff (Assistant Engineers, Work Inspectors, and
Laskars) of the WRD will steer the organizing efforts to ensure



- 54 -
Annex 4
Page 12 of 22
coordination during the implementation phase, and after turnover
is effected.
b)    Organizing activities will cover three levels -- on the tertiary
level (sluice commands); the secondary level (distributaries); and
the scheme level. Turnover of O&M responsibilities at the
distributary level and below will result in cost savings to WRO
over the long term, as well as in improved O&M.
c)    The terms and conditions of turnover will be spelled out in a
written contract -- Nemorandum of Understanding (MOU) -- between
the WRO and the newly created distributary-level FCs.
The turnover program will be applied initially to five schemes, totalling
70,833 ha, which have already completed Stage I physical improvements under
the NWHP.3 In two of the schemes, Amaravathy and Sathanur, pilot organizing
efforts have already been initiated. As soon as the FOT work is well underway
in this first round of schemes, successive rounds of organizing activity will
be initiated in the last two of the seven NWMP locations, in all major schemes
awaiting Stage I improvements, and in those minor schemes and project
completions where there will be infrastructural improvements at the
distributary level or below. The sequence of schemes to be taken up for the
FOT program year by year is shown in Attachment B. The underlying logic in
planning the sequence of coverage is that it is evolutionary, gets
progressively intensive, is linked to the sequence of physical works, and
allows for a "learning approach" for the WRO.
23.   Structure and function of farmer organizations.  A three-tier farmers'
organization will be implemented consisting of local sluice level
organizations, distributary level FCs, and scheme level Apex Committees. The
first tier of sluice level organizations (20 to 40 ha) will be formal or
informal. In many cases, indigenous sluice level organizations already exist
in the field. By tradition, these organizations have assumed responsibility
for water management below the outlet hiring local staff for O&M purposes.
Payment is usually in cash or in kind. Since indigenous sluice level
organizations may already exist and function well, there would be no need to
impose formal structures. Where necessary, though, they can be formalized
through registration.
24.   The focus of WRO's organizing activities would be at the distributary
level (about 500 ha) where FCs will be established. Each sluice level
organization will elect three representatives (including one woman
representative) to form the distributary level FC, a formally registered
organization under the Societies' Registration Act. A federation of all FCs
within a scheme command will form an Apex Committee that will have an advisory
role in main system operations.
3 The  five  schemes  are:    Amararathy,  Sathanur,  Cumbum,  Marudanadi,
Sethiathope.



- 55 -
*                                                       Annex 4
Page 13 of 22
25.   The function of the FCg will be:  (1) to provide an organized forum for
WRO-farmer interaction; (2) to maintain and protect the distributary canals
and structures that have already been improved under NWMP, or that will be
improved under WRCP; (3) to ensure equitable water distribution among the
sluice outlets;' and (4) to assist the WRO in planning system operations.
Following the formal turnover of O&M responsibility to the Councils, all costs
of OEM will be borne by farmers (e.g., for canal desilting or deweeding).
Sluice level organizations, whether informal or formal, will manage water
services below the field outlet. The function of the Apex Committee will be to
participate in scheme level consultations on inter-distributary matters and on
issues related to scheme level water management and FC.
Financina arrangements
26.   irrigated farmers in Tamil Nadu are already paying land taxes to the
Revenue Department which are nominally about the same as the O&M expenditures
of the WRO, including both works and establishment costs. When actual
repayment rates (around 80%) are calculated, farmers' real payments are
roughly equivalent to the agency's recurrent costs. Under the WRCP, farmers
will be asked to share a larger proportion of the total management burden than
is currently the case. Most of the increased burden will be "in kind", that
is, through participation in the activities of management (attending meetings,
keeping records, monitoring water levels, adjusting flows, resolving disputes,
etc.). However, there will also be increased financial costs to farmers. To
ensure a minimal level of 0&M funding below the distributary level, each
farmer will be required to contribute Rs. 250 per hectare (Rs. 100/acre) for
deposit in a fixed-deposit, interest bearing account under FC control. The
annual yield of roughly Rs.30/ha from this account will be supplemented by
additional collections from the members if O&M costs exceed the interest
income. These financial provisions will be a condition for signing a
Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) between the WRO and the FC turning over
distributary O&M to the FCs.
27.   The Government is examining the possibility of an additional tax to be
on all farmers directly benefiting from project improvements, with a rebate
offered to farmers who collectively agree to assume management responsibility
for their respective distributary canals. The tax will likely take the form
of a "betterment levy" for which there is ample local precedent, the logic
being that farmers who receive unusual benefits from new infrastructure should
contribute to the cost. In the context of turnover of O&M to FCs, the
'modernization levy' will bring additional revenues to the government while
providing an incentive to farmers who satisfactorily discharge their O&M
responsibilities.
28.   In addition to bearing the O&M expenses below the distributary (or
structured) level, the farmers will contribute 20 per cent of the costs of
lining works in the distributary or minor in Stage II. The rationale here is
that lining is often the most expensive and desired intervention to increase
conveyance efficiency. However, it is also often poorly implemented and
maintained.   The demand for contributions from farmers is expected to ensure



- 56 -
Annex 4
Page 14 of 22
that the right priority will be accorded to this improvement, that selective
rather than full lining would be undertaken, and that careful attention and
farmer monitoring would follow. In addition, as already noted, farmers will
contribute in-kind to the completion of OFD works.
Lecal basis
29.   The formal registration of FCs and other farmers' organizations in the
WRCP will be as per the Tamil Nadu Societies' Registration Act, 1975. The Act
provides for a democratic framework for farmers' organizations and their
governance, including registration, periodic elections to management
positions, transparent sharing of information among the members and for the
formation of federations. The office of the State Registrar of Societies will
receive annual reports and audited financial statements from the farmers'
organizations.
30.   There is no comprehensive act in Tamil Nadu (as in the case of the State
of Maharashtra, for instance) for irrigation and drainage management.   In its
absence, the Societies Registration Act will provide the framework for
incorporation. A Government Order will be issued to the WRO prior to the
start of the project requiring the establishment of farmer organizations and
declaring the decision of turnover of management below the structured level
from the WRO to these organizations. The legal transfer of management
responsibility (not including the transfer of ownership rights over assets)
will be defined through a Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) to be signed
between the FCs and the WRO. Sample contents of an MOU are shown in Attachment
C. The MOU will specify the obligations and benefits of the transfer
arrangement; the conditions under which the Council will engage in Joint
Management of the distributary during a transition period of 3 to 9 months;
and the conditions for full transfer of O&M responsibility.
31.   The State Water Policy recently approved by GOTN will emphasize the role
of farmers in water resources management and the policy of management turnover
to provide a general policy umbrella for WRO's actions. The Tamil Nadu
Panchayati (local governments) Act, currently under revision, is unlikely to
affect the irrigation sector specifically, but will support broad efforts to
induce local participation.
The Process of oraanizing farmer councils
32.   The process leading to O&M turnover is presented in this section in
three phases: (1) assessment, (2) organizing, and (3) joint management,
leading to turnover. The process of organizing and turning over O&M
responsibility will be basically similar for all schemes coming under the
WRCP, regardless of whether the particular scheme is undergoing Stage II
improvements (the case of the seven systems improved under NWMP) or are
starting fresh, under Stage I improvements. In either situation, the process
of organizing farmers will precede any construction activities at the
distributary level or below. The logic for this approach in to ensure that



-57 -
Annex 4
Page 15 of 22
farmers provide inputs into the design of improvements being carried out and
are committed to maintaining them once under their O&H responsibility.
33.   Plans for Stage II improvements to the distributary canal and below
would be agreed with the FC, following a joint diagnosis of system
deficiencies carried out by means of a walk-through. In cases where the FC is
yet to be formally registered, agreement will be obtained from informal FCs.
Plans for Stage I improvements at the distributary and below will be discussed
with farmers as an integral part of the organizing process. A listing of the
improvements to be implemented under Stage I will form part of the provisional
MOU to be drawn up jointly by WRO and farmers, with the help of the FOT team.
This provisional MOU will serve as a common reference point in discussing the
mutual roles of the Council and the WRO. The MOU will be finalized at the
earliest once the works begin. The WRO will seek and record FCs' opinion on
satisfactory completion of works.
34.   Throughout the period of the project, FOT teams would be deployed for
work with farmers; 15 FOT teams will be deployed in the initial years with
subsequent adjustment in numbers as required. The FOT team will be supervised
by an Assistant Engineer (AE) and will include a Works Inspector (WI), and a
Lascar. Each team will cover about 3000 ha per year. The teams will work
with some starter farmer organizations, some advanced, and some "difficultw
ones. Good FOT team performance and well functioning FCs will be recognized
through annual awards and other incentives.   In any given distributary, the
time from assessment to turnover is anticipated to take between 12 to 18
months, but organizing efforts will not begin in all distributaries
simultaneously. A two-year period is planned for each round of organizing
activities, and once the project is fully underway, two rounds would be under
implementation simultaneously (see Attachment B).
a)    Assessment Phase (about 3 months): The FOT team will be trained at
IMTI prior to taking the field. The team's initial activity will
be to arrange participatory walk-throughs of each distributary,
along with WRO project staff and farmers, to begin a dialogue
about priority improvements. Other tasks during this phase
include compiling a list of actual farmers and the landholders, a
detailed map of the official and actual command area (including
unauthorized water use), and a rough assessment of the
organizational capacity of farmers in each distributary area,
including information on any existing formal or informal
organizations. Each team will be assisted by a trained male and
female social organizer, to assist in the Assessment and
Organizing phases. Additionally, a cadre of experienced
Institutional Advisors will be deployed to guide the FOT teams and
provide advice to senior project staff regarding the
organizational efforts and the turnover process. The social
organizers and institutional advisers will be recruited as
consultants. A major information and education campaign will be
launched in this phase with the assistance of IMTI to provide an



- 58 -
Annex 4
Page 16 of 22
appreciation of the process for senior managers of the WRO and to
offer specific information on costs and benefits to farmers.
b)    OrganizIng Phame. (4-6 months):  The FOT team, with guidance from
the Institutional Advisor, will begin a long series of informal
meetings with farmers throughout the command to discuss issues
related to turnover. The women social organizers will meat with
women farmers and encourage their participation in both the
sluice-level and distributary-level organizations. A provisional
MOU will be drawn up as a reference for discussing the details of
the Council's eventual rights and responsibilities. Pending
formal registration, a provisional FC will be organized by the FOT
team. The WRO will consult with the FC concerning any
modifications to ongoing distributary-level improvements (Stage I
works), and negotiate the detailed terms of the MOU for transfer
of O&M responsibility. Once the O&M fund is collected, the MOU
can be signed.
c)    Joint Management Phase. (3-9 months): During this phase, the FCs
will learn how to manage the distributary. Farmers will work
closely with WRO field staff to understand how to read water
levels, and how to maintain the new improvements. With help from
the AE and other WRO staff, the Council will also develop an
operational plan for water distribution. Prior to formal
turnover, a test of the water flows will be conducted to ensure
that the physical facilities are in order. The WRO Executive
Engineer will then formally turn over responsibility to the FC.
35.   After the official turning over, WRO and the FC will discuss further
improvements to the distributary that can be implemented under the WRCP as
Stage II improvements. The AE will work with FCs to encourage the formation of
a project-level Apex Committee of FC presidents. The WRO will consult the Apex
Committee regarding main system improvements, interdistributary issues and
problems and requests for Stage II improvements. Well functioning Apex
Committees will be rewarded with a modest office-cum-training facility at the
scheme headquarters location.
ODeration and maintenance: clans and practice
36.    Infrastructural improvements and farmer participation described in the
preceding section will address some of the system deficiencies noted earlier.
These changes will be accompanied by an emphasis on O&M. The WRO's O&M program
will be managed by basin managers in four regions. A central O&M office will
provide specialized services to maintain standards and review criteria of
performance. The functions and expectations of the specialized unit and that
of the basin manager are described elsewhere in this report.
37.   To begin with, O&M in the entire system would be the responsibility of
the WRO. Later, as FCs are formed, registered, and funds collected for O&M,
the responsibility for O&M of distributaries/minors and below would be turned



- 59 -
Annex 4
Page 17 of 22
over by the WRO to FCs, while the WRO retains control over the headworks and
main system. operational plans will be developed in consultation with farmers.
This, of course, cannot be a one time exercise and would be reviewed every
year between the-WRO and the apex committee of the FCs.
O&M mechanization
38.   The restricted period available each year for maintenance and scarcity
of labor during the agricultural season point to the need for mechanization.
The WRO's O&M staff will be strengthened in their capacity to improve their
O&M practices by the procurement of selected equipment like excavators, rock
breakers, clam shell, crawler tractors, tipping trucks and back hoes (Table
4). The equipment would be shared for construction works on by-pass channels
or rented out to FCs at a concessional rate to speed up the desiltation works.
At the same time, purchase of vehicles -- jeeps, cars, motor cycles, bicycles
-- would improve the mobility of the staff including those engaged in FOT
(Table 5). Communication would be improved through the use of mobile radios.
39.   Three new regional workshops will be constructed at Madras, Trichy, and
Aliyar. The Madurai workshop will be strengthened. The workshops will be
well equipped; two fabrication workshops will be established at Trichy and
Madurai.
Financina O&M
40.   The GOTN has gradually increased its annual budget for O&M and has
allocated funds separately for works portion and staff at the beginning of
each financial year. The funds are allocated to each executive engineer on a
quarterly basis through letters of credit drawn on a commercial bank.   The
budget for financial year 1994-95 is Rs. 265 per ha on an average of which it
is estimated that Rs. 150 per ha is for works portion.
41.   Under the WRCP, each structure and canal reach will be inspected
annually and budget will be provided to meet the requirements of full funding
of maintenance.   Importantly, the funding requirement will be based on
specific and objective scheme level performance assessments. To assist scheme
staff in preparing the O&M requirements, a maintenance manual indicating
guidelines for carrying out repairs and an asset register for each scheme in
which inventory and state of assets registered annually will be prepared.
Consultants, whose terms of reference are to be agreed upon with the Bank,
will be appointed to assist in the preparation of these manuals and registers.
42.   FCs will take over OhM functions of the part of the system below the
distributary/minor head and maintain it. To this end, they will use the
interest from the fixed deposit collected from each farmer on entry into the
FC. In addition, they will organize special projects for immediate repairs to
the system under their management control if the situation warrants it. The
GOTN will consider whether a portion of any charges levied on farmers for the
rehabilitation/ modernization improvements as a result of the WRCP could be
shared with FCs. Conditions for such sharing would be a well-functioning FC



- 60 -
Annex 4
Page 18 of 22
with a proven record for at least one year of effective O&M in the system
turned over to their control.
43.   Budgetary procedure.  Tamil Nadu has established a satisfactory
arrangement for the preparation of the budget and allocation of O&M funds for
different schemes. The CE (Irrigation) at Madras coordinates all activities,
obtaining government approvals and allocating the funds to SEs and EEs
responsible for O&M in the various schemes.
44.   The budget process starts early, usually in August, in the financial
year (April-March). The SE (O&M) in a scheme reviews the proposals presented
by the EE in August and, in turn, submits his proposal to the CE (Irrigation)
in September. The CE : (i) finalizes the revised budget for the current
financial year after taking note of the progress made on O&M, expenditure
incurred ad the balance amount needed for the coming months; and (ii)
estimates the budget needed for the next financial year. He then submits his
proposal to the Secretary, Finance through the Secretary, PWD every September.
The Secretary, Finance informs the PWD of the final budget allocation for
every scheme in November/December. Information on the budget for the
following financial year is conveyed to the CE after it is voted in the State
legislature in February/March. The CE informs the SE/EEs of their budgets.
The EEs are authorized to incur expenditure on O&M through letters of credit
issued by the Finance Department, on a quarterly basis, supported by a
recommendation of the CE. The early allocation of budget has helped the EEs
in planning their O&M works without difficulty. However, the problem was the
amount of funds provided in the past.
Training
45.   The largest training effort will be the preparation of the WRO staff to
meet the challenges of the new thrust on O&M and farmer participation. In the
case of the latter, a major training effort will be launched with IMTI as the
nodal agency. IMTI will involve other training and research institutions and
NGOs in Tamil Nadu as well as private consultants with experience in farmer
organization.
46.   FOT Training. The FOT training program would include:  appreciation
workshop for senior managers and project staff; specific skill building
workshops for the FOT team and for FCs. Details of the training programs
planned and their proposed schedule is in Attachment D. The FOT program will
also provide IMTI an opportunity to develop a systematic set of instructional
material for farmer organization that could be of great use to Tamil Nadu and
of some interest to other States in India. Resources have been provided to
develop case studies and teaching material as well as for collaborative
initiatives with other institutions involved in farmer organization and
related efforts such as Anna University, Gandhigram, and the Tamil Nadu
Agricultural University.



- 61 -
Annex 4
Page 19 of 22
47.   In view of the importance of the training effort in the success of the
FOT, it has been agreed with GOTN that it is of the utmost importance to
infuse stability and strength to the institution.   In the past, although IMTI
was set up as a training institution, not all staff or directors posted there
were motivated by the training function.  Directors have been posted for short
periods of time. Staff on deputation from other departments have not been
allowed to stay for more than three years if they wished to, and if IMTI found
them to be good. A 'core" group of faculty was chosen some five years ago but
is yet to be so designated. To rectify this situation, it has been agreed
that a "corew faculty group will be designated and that the full complement of
faculty will be recruited. Where it is not possible to get staff on
deputation, contractual and consultancy arrangements will be tried.
48.   Irrigation Management Training. The project would also support training
in the following areas:
a)    Management skills -- IMTI at present offers a series of training
courses for supervisory skills. These course will be reviewed
with consultancy assistance as part of the project and redesigned
an necessary. In addition, IMTI would enter into a collaborative
agreement with a reputed management training institution to offer
a condensed program of management skills for those already at, or
are about to move into, senior management level.
b)    Performance assessment -- Irrigation managers would be trained in
system diagnostic techniques for application to their own field
settings. IMTI would continue its training programs on this
subject, using material from its ongoing collaborative program
with IIMI, Sri Lanka, which could be expanded.
c)    Equipment operator and maintenance training -- as part of the
purchase contract for new equipment and vehicles, suppliers will
be asked to provide operation and maintenance training to
mechanical staff in WRO. As for specialized maintenance of
electronic communication, data processing and computer equipment,
initial training will be provided by suppliers as part of purchase
contracts, to be followed up at IMTI.
d)    Water control, distribution and measurement -- training of staff
in this area will be provided by IHH, Poondi and by consultants
from the Indian Institute of Science, Bangalore and CWPRS, Puns.
0)    Maintenance of drainage channels -- The canal and drainage O&M is
part of the same organizational hierarchy in Tamil Nadu. The CZ
(O&M) and river basin managers will arrange for training in O&M of
drainage channels, including annual workshops with the assistance
of consultants. Annual training budgets and plans will be
provided by each basin manager for this purpose.



- 62 -
Annex 4
Page 20 of 22
f)    The project will support national and international study tours on
selected topics from the above list of subjects.   These tours
will be planned as part of an annual program of training, and
reviewed each year during the formal annual review.
Monitoring
49.   The GOTN will set up a systematic program of monitoring the Systems
Improvement and Farmer Turnover component of the project. Performance
indicators for the component are suggested in Attachment E.
50.   Systems Improvement. Apart from the operational plan, O&M performance
will be evaluated annually, in association with FCs. Annual reports for each
command will be published by basin managers. The report will detail O&M
achievements, actual and budgeted expenditures, and planned performance
targets for the next year.
51.   The project would support the use of modern monitoring tools.  For
instance, sensors at critical locations will be used to monitor levels and
flows in the main canal systems. Remote sensing data will be used to monitor
impact. These data can be processed in near real time to provide estimates of
crop acreage, crop condition, optimum design for crop cutting experiments,
crop yield estimation and overall system performance.   Annual monitoring can
lead to evaluation of system performance through the years to identify areas
requiring intensive follow up action. Concurrent monitoring through the season
could provide early indications of moisture stress or crop damage for
corrective action. Surveys for all WRCP schemes will be carried out during
the first year with the assistance of the National Remote Sensing Agency
(NRSA) at Hyderabad, Anna University, Madras and IWS in WRO. IWS would
gradually develop capacity in remote sensing techniques and undertake
increasing responsibility in the application of such techniques for improving
water resources management in the State.
52.   Farmer Turnover. The GOTN will also establish a program of monitoring
farmer organization and turnover. Indicators include assessment of inputs,
process and output. To complement the quantitative indicators, qualitative
assessments are also necessary. It is too easy to be satisfied with "numbers
of farmer organizations registered" without verifying whether these are
"paper, organizations or are "real" organizations where farmers know they are
members and are aware of rights and obligations. For this purpose, the
following have also been agreed to as monitoring devices : (1) The Basin
Manager and the CE (O&K) will visit a sample set of sluice organizations,
farmer councils and apex committees at least three times a year to get direct
feedback from farmers about progress. These field visits will be in the
nature of rapid assessments;   (2)  meetings of institutional advisors who
will assist the FOT teams will be convened every month at the basin level to
obtain their views on issues and operational problems; (3) IMTI's visits to
the sites to assist FOT teams will also be used as a periodic source of
information for basin managers and the CE (O&M) to gauge progress in FOT.



- 63 -
Annex 4
Page 21 of 22
53.   In addition to these methods, every annual review of the project as a
whole will provide an opportunity to adjust original plans according to
current experience.
Risks
54.   The WRCP represents a significant shift in the government's approach to
water resources in general, and irrigation in particular. Whereas decisions on
system improvements so far in the State have been made in a top down fashion,
under the new arrangement, farmers will work with the WRO to determine the
best course of action. Some aspects of irrigation management, particularly
main system management, will remain within government's exclusive purview, but
even here farmers have legitimate interests in making their voices heard.
Similarly, below the distributary, farmers will have exclusive management
responsibility, with the WRO assisting in and monitoring farmers, performance.
55.   The public-private partnership required to make the new arrangement
function well calls for new kinds of skills and a markedly different attitude
on the part of both government and farmers. There is an inherent risk that
public bureaucracies may not easily undergo the necessary transformation. It
is also important to retain the autonomy of internal management of farmer
organizations so that they are not subject to bureaucratic and political
pressures. Much also depends on the stability and performance of key
institutions such as IMTI that will undertake a major training effort in both
the Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover activities.   The project will
address this risk through: (1) policy commitment to the SIFT program; (2)
specific administrative action to translate the commitment into action,
including decisions in the case of IMTI, to introduce stability and
consistency to its training programs; (3) training programs aimed at
engendering an understanding of the management logic underlying the new
arrangement, and imparting new skills, particularly in farmer organization and
turnover; (4) a transparent process of farmer organization in which existing
departmental staff steer the process with support from NGOs and specialists in
the field; (6) frequent monitoring and evaluation of the new arrangements so
that WRO staff remain well informed of progress and are challenged to propose
solutions to the inevitable difficulties of the SIFT component (See Box 2)



- 64 -
Annex 4

entirely as a means of cost recovery.  This view does not adequately take
incentives for farmers into account. Farmers' responsibilities should be tied
to service improvements and hence a transparent program of infrastructural

inappropriate government subsidies for OhM or for OFD, although the GOTN has
agreed that the WRCP's OFD model would be applicable throughout the State.
Efforts to maintain this model must be made during project implementation so
that competing approaches that  emphasize subsidies are not supported.  The
tnnual Reviews of the project will offer an opportunity to assess progress in
the SIFT component and to take corrective action.

MEMORANDUM OF UNDERSTANDING
MOU between Water Resources Organis.ation and Farmer's Council at
Thip  deed  of  Memorandum  of  TlriHrstanrling  executed
betwer'n the Chairman on behalf of tlie .................... council
at Distribtitory/Branch/Channel .taking off
from .....eft/Right Main Canal
of .................. Project in ..........               Taluk.
District, hereinafter called the "Counril' which expression shall
unless excluded by or repugnant to the subject or context include
his succ,essors in Office and assigns of the one part and the
Executive Engineer ............... Division of the Water Resources
Organisation   on   behalf  of  the  Government   of   Tamilnadu,
hereinafter  ralled .............. which expression shall  unless
excluded  by or repugnant to the subject or conttPxt  include  his
successors or assigns of the other part;
WHEREAS, it has been decided to carry out the Operation
and Maintenance of the irrigation system at the level of distrib-
utory and systems below it in the State of Tamilnadu jointly by
the Council of Farmers and the water Resources Organisation of
Governm-nt of Tamilnadu and, upon satisfactory completion of  the
4a id  ji-nt management phase, to hatd .ve-r the management of  the
distributory  and the systems bel'r; it t, the Cnruncil of  Farmers
by turning over.-
AND  WHEREAS, it has been decided to provide a  written
understdnding between the Council and the Water Resources Organi-
sation on the activities, to be carried out by both the  parties,
during  the phase of joint management and turn over by  means  of
the following Memorandum of Understanding
MEMORANDUM OF UNDERSTANDING
Part I   Preliminary
(1)        In  this Memorandum of Understanding, unless  there  is
anything repugnant in the subject or context-
(a)  "Apex   Council'  means  the  federation  of  all   the
Couincils;
(b)  "Council' means the association of farmers;
(cl  "Joint Management" means the Operation and  Maintenance
of  the irrigation system at the level of  distributory
and systems below it in the State of Tamilnadu carried
out  jointly  by the Council and  the  Water  Resources
Organisation   of  the  Government  of  Tamilnadu,    as
specified in Part II below;
(d)  "Turn  Over' means the point in time when  the  Council
assumes  full  responsibility  for  the  Operation  and
Maintenance  of the irrigation system at the  level  of
distributory  and  systems  below it in  the  State  of
Tamilnadu, as specified ir. Part III below.
(e)  "Distributory' means  a channel or  group  of  sluices
nvFring  an area of aboul 'nn ha. and taking off  from
either main or branch canal ,f an Irrigati ion System.



73                             Annex 4
Attachment C
Page 2 of 5
Part II   Joint Management
2.        The Joint Management phase will begin when
(a)  The  Council  has been formed and  registered,  thereby
implying that informal Sluice Committees are in place;
(b)  Office Bearers of the Council have been elected;
(c)  A  service fund amounting to .... ...... per hectare  of
command area has been established in a bank account.
3.        The   Joint   Managemen't  phase  will   include   close
collobaration between the Water Resources Organisation and the
Council through exchange of information and transfer of skills.
4.        The  Water  Resources  Organisation  will  prepare   an
operation plan for the entire scheme showing the availability of
water  and  allocation  of water made  for  Irrigation,  Drinking
water,    Industrial   and  other  uses.   The   Water   Resources
Organisation  will also prepare the method of distributing  water
to various distributories, namely, opening and closing of canals
and ON!OFF periods for all the distributories and for the
distributory  of the Council concerned. The Council will  prepare
the  Operation Plan for the distributory under its  jurisdiction.
The respective Operation Plans will be discussed and modified as
required.
5.        The Water Resourcos Organisation-will conduct hydraulic
tests  of the distributory to check the capacities  at  different
points with design.
6.         Water Resources Organisation will operate the headworks
and the main system while jointly managing the distributory with
the Council. Ownership of the system and related works will vest
with  the Government. Normally, water supply to the  distributory
will  be  in  accordance with  the  distributory  operation  plan
prepared,  discussed,  and  agreed  to  by  the  Water  Resources
Organisation with the Council. However, -in case of reduced
availability  of supply the supplies to the distributory  may  be
proportionately  adjusted  in proportion to its share.  To  carry
out  its functions the Water Resources Organisation  will  deploy
necessary  field  staff. The Water  Resources  Organisation  will
share with the Council, relevant data in respect of canal  design
or actual capacities and discharge; designed water levels at
different  sluices; water delivered among distributories,  design
details of the Proportional distributors wherever applicable;
seepage and operation losses. The Water Resources Organisation
will also maintain the main canal and branches to ensure designed
discharge  at  the head regulator.  During the  Joint  Management
phase, the Water Resources organisation will-train the Council in
monitoring irrigation schedules and their documentation.
7.         The  Water  Resources  Organisation  will  prepare   an
inventory  of  the  works showing lengths of  lined  and  unlined
sections,  structures,  crossings,  sluices,  outlets  and   land
acquired for the above.
8.         The  works  will  be jointl]y  inspected  by  the  Water
Resources Organisation and the Council to identify deficiencies,
omissions, sub-standard or incomplete works.



74                             Annex 4
Attachment C
Page 3 of 5
9.        On  completion  of  these works,  the Water  Resources
Qrganisation   will  organise  the  hydraulic  testing   of   the
distributory to ensure -that the designed discharge can pass to
the tail outlets. The Water Resources Organisation will prepare a
statempnl    -4    _-                L;d. a- -lUerent
t!~z cpaclty~,: L. iLrLij4uLory a-citrn
sluice offtakes and the designed and actual water depths.
10.       The Office Bearers of the Council, its  representatives
or Staff will accompany the Water Resources Organisation Staff
during the operation of the gate and head regulator, observing
and reading gauges, accounting for water, working out actual duty
and depth of water.
11.       The  Council  will  identify any shortage  or  fall  of
levels in water'supply in the distributory at the outlets and
communicate  to  the Water Resources Organisation for  follow  up
Action.
12.       The Council 'will ensure equitable distribution ofjwater
among  the  outlets. In the Joint Management phaae,  the  Council
will prepare itself for taking over the Operation and Maintenance
function at the distributory level. To this end, it will collect
all  relevant  data, .prepare and  implement  the  Operation  and
Maintenance plans together with the Water Resources Organisation
and   ensure   collection  of  adequate   service   charges   for
maintenance. It will also demonstrate capacity to conduct the
affairs of the Council in an organised and satisfactory manner
with full attention to records and accounts.
13.       An  Automatic  Stage  Level  Recorder  (ALRI  will   be
installed  immediately  downstream of the head regulator  of  the
distributory  for continuous record of water flows.  The  Council
will  be responsible for the safe custody of the Automatic  Stage
Level Recorder.
14.       The distributory will be jointly inspected by the staff
of the Water Resources Organisation and the Council before the
season  to identify the status of maintenance and to  list  items
like,  silt  removal  and weed  clearance  needing  immediate  or
regular maintenance. The list will also include items of repairs
to  earth  work,  raising  of  tank  bund,  and  repairs' to  the
structures. The Water Resources Organisation will prepare an
estimate of such works and discuss the list and related funding
needs with the Council. The Council may suggest items to be
added. If these. items cannot be accommodated in the Water
Resources Organisation budget provision, then the Council may
indicate   its  contribution  before  estimates  are   finalised.
Contributions can beAn cash or in kind.
Part III : Turnover.
15.       The  Management  of the distributory  and  the  system:
below it will be handed over by the Water Resources Organisation
and taken over  by the Council as per the procedures laid down..
16.       The  Water  Resources Organisation  will  also  prepare
completion records of all the works; the land plans; gauge
discharge   curves;  and  a  map; showing  the  command  of   the
distributory and of the sluice or tank in the command.



~ 75           ~             Annex 4
Attachment C
Page 4 of 5
17.       As   part   of  the  turnover,  the   water   Resources
Organisation  will  hand over svstem mananipmont-  A I      :.';
.iuuumencs  mentioned  above to the Council. The  Water  Resources
Organisation  and  the Council will indicate  that  turnover  has
occured by signing this documemt 'at the appropriate place.
18.       The Water Resources Organisation will-operate the  head
regulator of the distributory and be responsible to maintain the
distributory upto 30 metre length from the head regulator. It can
suggest  improvements  for Ooeratinn >an    4-t,4    z &   qcc    tne
distributory. The Water Resources Organisation, as a functional
requirement, would inspect the position of water supplieg,
irrigation works in the command, distributory and field level
structures  under  the  jurisdiction of  the  Council  to  verify
whether   the   Memorandum  of   Understanding   is   implemented
satisfactorily or not.
19.       The  Council will carry out all activities  related  to
Operation and Maintenance from the date of turn-over. The
maintenance and repairs shall include weed removal; silt removal;
earth  work  to  restore  banks;  repairs  to  lining,  pointing,   i
plastering; replacing the damaged portion; repairs to masonry and
other structures; oiling. and painting of gates and shutters. The
Council shall protect the distributory and masonry structures
thereon from any damage. The Council will also undertake or
suggest   measures   for' improved  water  management   in    the
distributory  command.  It can ask for and obtain from the  water
Resources  Organisation,  information on  planned  Operation  and
Maintenance  activities  in  the main  system  including  at  the
concerned  distributory.  It can utilise lands acquired  for  the
distributory  for  growing  trees,  fodder  and  timber  or   for
beautification  and  utilise the products of such  work  for  the
general welfare of the Council with due care for the protection
of the assets and th- environment.
20.  Disputes:
- All  disputes  in respect of  various  provisions  made
under  this  understanding  will  be  resolved  by  a   Committee
comprising   one   nominee  from  the  :Apex   Council   and   the
Superintending  Engineer of the Water Resources Organisation.  If
the  Council  violates  any of the provisions  contained  in  the
Memorandum of Understanding with regard to water management or
maintenance, the Executive Engineer will issue notice to the Apex
Council giving time to take corrective action within a specified
period. In case no improvement is made by the Council, the
Executive Engineer may decide to take back the management and
maintenance functions from the Council.

Registered  land  owners under each sluice command, who
have enrolled as members of the Genaral Body will elect
three representatives for each sluice including a leader  to
constitute the sluice level committee to take care of water
distribution within the sluice command.
Elected Leader of each sluice would become a Member of
the Executive Body of the Farmer Council.
Atleast 51 % of the registered land owners of the
distributary command should be enrolled as Members before
registration of the council.
9.   ADMINISTRATION OF THE COUNCIL
Members  of  the Executive Body will elect  the  office
bearers of the council such as Chairman, Vice-Chairman,
Secretary and Treasurer. This office bearers will manage the
affairs of the Council.
10.  PERSON AUTHORISED TO DEAL WITH THE ASSOCIATION CASES:
The   Chairman,Vice-Chairman  and  the  Secretary   are
empowered to sue or to be sued on behalf of the Council.
11.  PERSON  AUTHORISED  TO  IMPLEMENT  THE  PROCEEDINGS  OF  THE
COUNCIL:
The Chairman of the Executive Body of the Council is
empowered to give direction in regard to the business of the
council.
12.  MEMBERSHIP AND OTHER FEES
A fee of Rs.10/= will be charged to each member of the
General Body at the time of enrollment.
Each   Member  will  have  to  contribute   a   minimum
subscription of Rs.250/Hectare towards Service Fund soon
after the enrollment as Member of the General Body.
13.  RIGHTS, OBLIGATIONS AND PRIVILAGES OF MEMBERS
The Members can compete in the elections by attending
the General Body and Special General Body, Meeting. All
Members have the right to vote. Each one will have only  one
vote.  The  Members  should  co-operate  in  conducting  the
meetings successfully. They have right to see the  Councils,
Membership Register, Minutes of Meeting, Accounts Registers
and other details during the office hours. The Members
should  not act against the Bye-laws and Objectives  of  the
Council.                                                                 A
14.  THE MANNER IN WHICH THE COUNCIL SHALL TRANSACT ITS BUSINESS:
(a) The financial year for the Council shall be from
April 1, of a year to March 31, of the next year.
(b) The General Body will have Annual General Meeting every
year. If necessary, a General Body Meeting will be held
whenever found necessary, with advance intimation of
Ten days time.

Annex 4
Attachment C (Part II)
Page 3 of 4
(c) The Executive Body shall meet atleast once in a month.
They can have Meetings whenever required.
(d) The tenure of Office Bearers of both the Executive Body
and sluice level committee shall be Three years and at
the  end  of Three years a fresh   election  will  take
place.
(e)  The  General  Body  will decide on  policy  matters  on
Operation and Maintenance and the Executive Body will
be the implementing agency.
(f)  To  collect  the Service Fund at a rate of  Rs.250  per
hectare  or more as decided by the General Body and  to
deposit the amount in a bank and to carryout Operation
and Maintenance from the interest gained from the
deposit. The account will be operated by the Chairman,
Secretary and the Treasurer by following the Terms  and
Conditions  laid  down  by the Executive  Body  of  the
Council. The Treasurer along with any one of the two
viz. Chairman or Vice-Chairman can jointly sign and
operate the bank account.
(g) The Executive Body shall decide about maintenance
and repairs of the distributories, field channels and
repairs to structures coming under the area of the
Council. The Operation and Maintenance shall be carried
out from the amount of interest on the share capital
amount deposited in a bank.
(h)  The  Executive  Body may appoint staff to  conduct  the
administration, on terms and conditions which shall  be
decided  by  the  Executive Body.  But  the  person  so
selected shall not be a member of the Council.
(i)  If  there  be any legal disputes, the Chairman  or  his
nominee  will  handle  such  cases  on  behalf  of  the
Council.
(j) The Executive Body will ensure proper auditing of the
Council accounts.
(k)  To  conduct the  General Body Meeting, a quorum of  one
third of members shall be present. If such quorum is
not obtained the Meeting will be postponed for one
hour.  Even  if  on postponed time the  quorum  is  not
obtained, the Meeting shall be conducted by the members
present.
The resolution should be passed in the General Body
Meeting as per the majority (51 %) of the members
present.  Voting  should  be  conducted  to  pass   the
resolution. In case of an equal vote being polled for
and against the particular resolution, the Chairman
shall exercise his casting vote.
15.  ANNUAL REPORT AND ANNUAL ACCOUNT
(a)  To  maintain  the accounts of the management  cost  and
Operation and Maintenance costs separately and get them
audited every year by an Accountant appointed by the
Executive Body of the Council.

TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
SCHEME COMPLETIONS
1.    This annex' deals with the nine schemes to be financed under WRCP which focus on
improving the source of supply of water to existing traditional infrastructure2. PWD (WRO) is
building small to medium sized dams in small rivers to mobilise on-channel storages and to use the
stored waters to provide assured full irrigation supplies, thus stabilizing production in areas under
partial irrigation and bringing gap areas3 which have gone out of irrigated agriculture back into
being high productive irrigated areas. Such schemes would alleviate moisture stress which is a
major constraint in improving agricultural production.  Some of these schemes to create
impoundments and to improve supply channel and tank conditions are under way with several under
planning stages.
2.    After reviewing a number of these schemes, nine schemes have been selected for assistance
under the IDA credit. The selection is based on soil and land suitability for growing a variety of
crops, possibility of increasing cropping intensities, water availability, marketability of the produce,
improvements in the living conditions of the farmers and economic performance of the scheme.
These schemes are: Poigalyar, Nambiyar, Sothuparai, Mordhana, Rajathopekanar, Kodumudiyar,
Vadakku Pachayar, Adavinainarkoil and Gridhammal II. Construction work is well under way for
the first five schemes. In four schemes a small amount of additional area which is under rainfed
dry crops at present would be brought under irrigated agriculture. Altogether 25,824 acres would
be stabilized, 6762 acres of gap and 2158 acres of rainfed uplands would be brought under
irrigation. Schemewise breakdown is given in Table 5.1.
3.    Engineering designs for all the nine schemes have been prepared by PWD (WRO). These
were reviewed by the preparation and appraisal missions and modified as required. Eight of the
nine schemes involve building dams higher than 9m and impounding reservoirs. Consultants were
appointed to carry out flood hydrology and dam safety studies under FAO/CP supervision and their
reports are on file. There is a Dam Safety Review Project established for Tamil Nadu already under
Cr.22410-IN/Ln.33250-IN. This panel has reviewed the upgraded designs and other parameters
developed by PWD (WRO) and the consultants and have approved the construction of these dams.
Adequacy of supply channels and other water control and delivery structures have been ascertained.
These schemes submerge relatively small areas of land and impound small reservoirs. The land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation of project affected persons is dealt with in detail in Annex
9.
4.    The cost estimates prepared by PWD (WRO) have been revised taking into account the
modifications in the designs. The unit rates adopted for various work items were examined and
found to be substantially lower than prevailing market rates. Past contracts were examined and they
I Detailed tables. figures, engineering drawings, and analysis for this annex are in the project files.
2 For background information on traditional irrigation infrastructure refer to appendix I of this annex.
3Stabilization and gap areas vary on a yearly basis depending on climatic conditions.



- 85 -
Annex 5
Page 2 of 23
also revealed the same trend of contracts awarded exceeding the estimates by 30% to 40%. In order
to correct this situation, the unit rates provided by PWD (WRO) have been enhanced by 50% after
detailed discussions with a number of field engineers handling contract awards. The estimates can
therefore be considered as more realistic.
5.    Implementation capabilities of PWD (WRO) were assessed. Past performance indicated that
a division headed by an Executive Engineer with associated lower level staff is able to handle
construction work with a value of upto Rs3O to 50 million per year if there are no cash flow
problems. This has been taken into account in establishing the implementation duration for each
scheme.  Project procurement, contract packaging and contractor supervision and monitoring
arrangements are specifically designed to achieve maximum economies in construction costs while
maintaining good quality standards. Implementation schedules together with assumed contract
packages are given in Figure 5.1(i) through 5.1(viii).
6.    Benefits from implementing the scheme completions component would come from increased
agricultural production. Agricultural extension services in all the scheme commands are organized
following Training & Visits (T & V) principle which was introduced in the early 1980s. IDA credit
would be targeted specifically to source development, irrigation infrastructure development including
improvement of the operation and maintenance of the system under the WRCP. No specific
investments would be provided to the agricultural department in support of crop production in the
scheme commands. Therefore in the future with project situation, only crops already known to the
farmers of the area are included. Yield increases for these crops are restricted to that which can be
achieved by relieving moisture stress. Yields currently obtained under full irrigation conditions in
the scheme commands or adjacent commands have been used to reflect future with project
conditions. Existing agricultural extension services are expected to continue in the future. The nine
schemes when completed would generate incremental production of about 47,000 tons of paddy,
88,832 tons of sugarcane,and 5,234 tons of groundnut. Small increases in cotton and chilies
production would also be obtained. A beneficiary farmer without access to supplementary well
irrigation4 would be able to obtain incremental incomes of Rs1370 to Rs6106 per year per acre
depending on the scheme. Similarly a beneficiary farmer with access to supplementary well
irrigation would be able to obtain incremental incomes of Rs1552 to Rs4884 per year per acre
depending on the scheme.
7.    The estimated cost of this component is Rs3.11 billion (US$100.37 million) including
physical and price contingencies. The base cost excluding these contingencies is Rs2.46 billion
(US$76.8 million). The annual economic value of the incremental production from the nine schemes
is Rs380 million. The economic rate of return for the portfolio of nine schemes is 15%.
Schemewise breakdown of costs, benefits and economnic performance are also given in Table 5.1.
8.    Operation and maintenance of the completed schemes would be carried out with full
beneficiary participation. Water users associations would be formed at each tank or sluice command
4 There are an estimated 4505 wells in the nine scheme commands capable of irrigating on the average about 0.9
ha each.



- 86 -
Annex 5
Page 3 of 23
with an apex farmer organization (registered) for each scheme5. WRO would be responsible for
the maintenance of the dam and reservoir complex and all supply channels delivering water to the
tanks for the indirect ayacuts and upto sluices for direct ayacuts. The sluices and the tanks would
be under the joint responsibility of WRO and WUAs. Below the sluice and tank, operation and
maintenance would be the responsibility of the WUA.
9.    Specific aspects of each scheme are discussed in the following sections.
Poigaiyar Scheme
10.   There are eight system tanks fed by water diverted from the Poigai River and there are
another eight tanks which are rainfed from their own catchments. In addition there are a number
of farms that are cultivated using rainwater alone. By constructing a dam across this river and
impounding flood flows that would otherwise have gone to waste (Poigai river carries heavy flood
flows from the Northeast monsoon), the scheme would bring assured irrigation to the areas
commanded by the sixteen tanks and provide irrigation for a portion of the land currently under
rainfed cultivation. (system schematic, plan and other relevant maps are in project files)
11.   The Poigai Reservoir would be impounded behind an earth dam located on the Poigai River
near Aralvoimozhi village in Thovalai taluk of Kanyakumari District at Longitude 77030'45"E and
Latitude 816'00"N. The reservoir would have a live storage capacity of 2.7 MCM. The average
annual inflow into the reservoir is estimated to be 12.5 MCM. Catchment area contributing to this
reservoir is 2.85 sq.mile (730 ha). Poigai river, a tributary of Pazhayar river, originates at an
altitude of 2500 feet above mean sea level in the eastern slopes of the Western Ghats (Poigai
Mountain). On the other side of Poigai mountains, there are two odais6 namely Chungan Odai and
Erappaiyar Odai originating in the Poigai Mountain Reserved Forest. About 40% of the flows of
Chungan Odai are diverted to Poigai River.
12.   The Poigai River branches into two channels when it enters the plains: Shenbagaramanpudur
Channel and Thovala Channel. The Shenbagaramanpudur Channel is 2750m long and feeds three
tanks commanding a total area of 231.84 acres (93.86 ha). There are two diversion weirs across this
channel which control the waters flowing to these tanks. The Thovala Channel taking off on the
left flank of the Boothayan Anicut is 4780m long and feeds five tanks commanding a total area of
265.78 acres (107.60 ha).
13.   The scheme comprises the following engineering works:
- Earth Dam 1174m long
- Ogee shaped masonry spillway 56m long capable of passing the 1 in 10,000 year flood
safely.
5 Scheme commands vary from 545 acres to 8683 acres. one apex organization would normally cover an area
not greater than 5000 acres. Therefore for Mordhana and Vadakku Pachayar two apex farmer organizations would be
registered. For further details refer to the appropriate scheme description section.
6 Odai is a local name for a small non-perennial stream

Annex 5
Page 4 of 23
- High Level Canal Sluice lno 0.90m x 1.50m Q = 47 cfs
- River Sluice Ino 1.22m x 1.83m Q = 100 cfs
- Excavation of spillway discharge channel 280m long to connect to a natural water course
- Improvements to the natural watercourse which is to handle spill flows ( for a distance of
about 475m from the confluence of the spill discharge channel and the natural watercourse)
- Excavation and lining of left side canal for a length of 7 km (this canal will deliver water
to the new area and the area under rainfed tanks)
- Excavation and lining of Field Bothies upto the 20 acre limit
- Standardising and improving the existing eight system tanks and the eight rainfed tanks
including provision of new inlets to the eight rainfed tanks which would receive regulated water in
the post-project period.
- The system tanks would be fed by regulated flows released through the river sluice located
in the body of the earth dam at 270+00 and the direct command would be fed by the regulated
releases from the canal sluice located at 90+00.
14.   Structures to be constructed on the Canal: 11 sluices, 1 regulator, 4 drops, 4 super passages,
6 aqueducts, 2 undertunnels, 3 culverts, 8 footbridges, 1 Highway Crossing Culvert and 1 Railway
Crossing Culvert. (Total 41 structures). The eleven sluices would feed the direct ayacut of 500
acres.
15.   The scheme command is covered by two agricultural extension centers. Farm inputs supply
and distribution are mainly handled by these two centers and ten village cooperatives. The area has
one farmer's training center as well. The soils of the scheme command are well-drained, moderately
coarse textured soils suitable for growing paddy, groundnut, pulses, gingely, tapioca, coconut and
fruit trees. The existing cropping pattern in the tank irrigation area is paddy (Pishanum) in the
northeast monsoon season followed by groundnut. In areas with supplementary well irrigation7,
banana is usually grown in a three-year rotation with Pishanum Paddy. Groundnut is grown in the
gap and dryland areas. The average annual rainfall in the command area is 716mm. Rain falls
heavily in June, October and November. Details of rain and other climatic parameters including
evapotranspiration computed using modified Penman-Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(i).
Crop calendar is provided in Figure 5.2. Water balance computations are on file. With completion
of the proposed works, paddy would remain the dominant crop and double-cropping of paddy would
be practiced within the limit of available water supply. Groundnut would be grown under irrigated
conditions.
16.   The estimated cost to complete this scheme is Rs 130.78 million (US$4.22 mrillion) including
physical and price contingencies. Detailed quantities and costs are given in Table 5.3(i). At present
work on the earth dam has been completed. It would take another three years to complete this
scheme. Implementation schedule is provided in Figure 5.1(i). One division is sufficient to
complete the scheme construction activities.
17.   The scheme would generate an incremental production of 1586 tons of paddy, 190 tons of
groundnut, 150 tons of pulses, 75 tons of chilies and 4200 bunches of bananas. A beneficiary with
7 There are an estimated 25 wells in the scheme command capable of irrigating 2.5 acres each.



- 88 -
Annex 5
Page 5 of 23
1.0 acre farm would obtain an incremental income of Rs3754 per year whilst a beneficiary with 2.5
acre farm having access to a well would obtain an incremental income of Rs3881 per year. Overall
cropping intensity would increase from 122% to 239%. Full benefits are expected to accrue from
1997 onwards although partial benefits would begin to accrue as early as 1995. The economic
internal rate of return for this scheme is estimated to be 12%.
18.   Sixteen water users' associations will be formed covering the sixteen tank commands and
eleven associations will be formed covering the direct ayacut. An apex farmer organization will be
formed and formally registered comprising representatives from these twentyseven associations.
Operation and maintenance of the irrigation system below the tank outlets and the sluices would be
the responsibility of the associations. The inlets to the tanks and the tanks themselves will be
maintained and operated jointly by the association and officers of the WRO. The dam and canal
system delivering water to the tanks and sluices will be maintained and operated by the WRO.
Nambiyar Scheme
19.   The Nambiyar River is the main source of irrigation water in Nanguneri Taluk of Tirunelveli
District. There are no storage facilities on the main stem of Nambiyar River. There are nine anicuts
from which the flood flows of Nambiyar River are diverted to feed sixty-eight tanks commanding
9073.72 acres. In addition there is one diversion weir on a minor tributary of the Nambiyar River
providing irrigation supplies for 110 acres through three tanks. It is proposed to build a storage
facility on the Nambiyar River between the seventh and eighth anicuts with a live storage capacity
of 2.33 MCM. The Nambiyar river originates on the eastem slopes of the westem ghats at an
altitude of 1650m above sea level in the Kalakad Reserve Forest Area. The river is fed by both
southwest and northeast monsoons.
20.   The scheme would benefit lands located below the seventh anicut and would cover lands
presently irrigated through forty tanks - 13 system tanks and 27 rainfed tanks. Thirteen system
tanks covered by the scheme, command an area of 655 acres and the twentyseven rainfed tanks
command an area of 767 acres (total 1422 acres). Three system tanks are located on the right bank
commanding an area of 110 acres. The combined storage capacity of the three system tanks is
about 1.02 MCM. These tanks were fed from waters diverted through the Kallankal Odai8 Anicut
which would be submerged by the new storage reservoir. A new channel will lead the water from
the reservoir to these three tanks (system schematic, maps and other relevant information can be
found in project files). In addition the dry ayacut to be brought under irrigation also lies on the
right bank. Eleven rainfed tanks located on the right bank would also be supplied from the right
bank main canal. These rainfed tanks have a combined storage capacity of 2.85 MCM and
command a total area of 353 acres. Six system tanks with a total storage capacity of 1.90 MCM
commanding an area of 324 acres and sixteen rainfed tanks with a total storage capacity of 1.07
MCM commanding an area of 414 acres located on the left bank would be supplied from the
reservoir by a left bank main canal. Four additional system tanks located on the left bank with a
total storage capacity of 1.64 MCM and a command area of 221 acres would be supplied through
8 Tributary of Nambiyar river referred to earlier.

channels taking off from the eighth and ninth diversion anicuts namely Tuttikulam and
Pulimankulam Anicuts.
21.   Engineering works include:  an earth dam about 10.40m high and 2370m  long, an
uncontrolled spill weir 240m long (from 1330+00 to 1570+00), two irrigation sluices (one to supply
areas located on the left bank of Nambiyar Iriver and the other to supply areas located on the right
bank), excavation of main supply canals leading from the irrigation sluices to the tanks,
improvements to two diversion anicuts and their supply channels, improvements to 40 tanks,
excavation of distributaries and field bothies in the new dry ayacut of 375 acres to be brought under
the scheme.The irrigation sluices would each have 1.20m x 1.65m opening and would be capable
of discharging upto 3.74 cumecs. The main canals will run for about ten days to fill their respective
tanks. The right bank main canal will have to run throughout the cropping season to supply the
direct ayacut which is situated in the head reaches of this canal. This reach from the dam to the
direct ayacut will be lined to minimize losses since the canal will be operating at far below its
design discharge for most of the time.
22.   The average annual flow entering the reservoir is estimated to be about 70 MCM. The flow
is highly variable as evidenced by the high coefficient of variation. Annual rainfall in the command
area averages about 740mm but is also highly variable as indicated by a C, value of 0.46. Within
the year, rain occurs predominantly in the months of October, November and December (59% of
annual rain falls in these three months and the remaining 41% is fairly uniformly distributed over
the remaining nine months). Rain and other climatic data including evapotranspiration values
computed using modified Penman-Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(ii). Details of rainfall,
flows and corresponding analyses are available in project files.
23.   Farm inputs supply and distribution are covered by two agricultural extension centers located
in the scheme area. A number of village cooperatives and private dealers also handle chemical
fertilizers and pesticides in the area. Typic Haplustalfs, deep calcareous soils with moderately fine
texture and Typic Ustorharts, shallow non-calcareous soils with moderately coarse texture are found
in the scheme command. The former is suitable for paddy-based cropping system and the latter for
non-paddy crops such as groundnut and pulses. The present cropping pattern is paddy (Pishanum)
followed by rainfed pulses in the tank irrigation area. In areas with supplementary well irrigation',
banana in a three-year rotation with paddy, cotton and chilies are grown. The gap area is planted
with rainfed pulses, groundnut and sesame. In the new dry ayacut to be brought under the scheme
groundnut and pulses are grown in the northeast monsoon season. In some of this new area with
access to well irrigation, vegetables (mainly tomato) are cultivated in rotation with groundnut under
irrigated conditions.With completion of proposed scheme works,paddy would remain the dominant
crop and vegetable and banana cultivation would continue without any increase in area cultivated.
The crop calendars are given in Figure 5.3. Water balance computations are on file.
24.   The estimated cost to complete this scheme is Rs173.93 million (US$5.61 million) including
physical and price contingencies. Details of quantities and costs are given in Table 5.3(ii). At
present work on left bank main canal, spillway and energy dissipator and causeway and some

25.   The scheme would generate an incremental production of 1842 tons of paddy, 346 tons of

and the tanks themselves will be maintained and operated jointly by the association and the WRO.
