by the WRO.
Sothuparai Scheme
27.   The Sothuparai Reservoir would be impounded on the river Varahanadhi at Longitude
77028'04" and Latitude 1007'45". Varahanadhi, a tributary of the Vaigai River drains about 323
sq.km up to its confluence with Vaigai far below existing Vaigai Dam and Reservoir Complex.
Berijam Lake located at the originating point of Varahanadhi is used mainly for water supply and
for irrigation. The lake has a storage capacity of 2.2 MCM. There are no man-made storage
facilities in the Varahanadhi system. There are four diversion anicuts commanding a direct ayacut
of 2107 acres and an indirect ayacut of 2519 acres. The Sothuparai scheme would benefit area
commanded by the first anicut namely Thalaiyuthukoil Dam. (Direct ayacut of 587 acres and
indirect ayacut of 1412 acres). Varahanadhi channel, 7980m in length, irrigates about 468 acres
through four open offtakes and one sluice. Thamaraikulam Rajavoikal, 4095m in length, irrigates
about 120 acres through fifteen sluices. Periakulam and Thamaraikulam tanks release waters
through three sluices-each to 722 acres and 371 acres respectively. Pappiampatti tank irrigates 319
acres through four sluices. These three tanks have a combined storage capacity of 3.18 MCM.
Varahanadhi channel takes off from a road culvert with five 1.05m x 1.20m vents. Along the
channel there are four diversion anicuts and at the entrance to Thamaraikulam Rajavoikal there is
a tail dam. In addition to providing improved and assured supplies to this area, the scheme would
also bring in about 1040 acres of upland under assured irrigation.
28.   Varahanadhi drains approximately 49 km2 at the damsite. The reservoir created would have
a storage capacity of 2.8 MCM. The average annual inflow to the reservoir is estimated to be 6.1
MCM. Average annual rainfall in the command area is 813mm. Rain distribution through the year
together with data on other climatic parameters and evapotranspiration values computed using
modified Penman-Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(iii). About sixty percent of the rain falls



- 91 -
Annex 5
Page 8 of 23
in the September-December period with another twenty percent in the April-May period. The
remaining twenty percent is fairly evenly distributed in the remaining six months.
29.   Sothuparai dam which is under construction, is a solid gravity masonry dam 57m high and
345m long. Flood flows would be handled by an uncontrolled 55m long spillway capable of passing
a 10,000-yr flood safely. Irrigation water would be released from the reservoir through a 1.50m x
1.80m river-cum-canal sluice. Irrigation water for the new ayacut will be provided through a
7.23km lined canal taking off from the Varahanadhi Channel downstream of the existing
Thalaiyuthu Dam. The canal is designed to carry 0.29 cumecs. Seven sluices would be provided
along this channel to irrigate the new ayacut (figure 5.6 refers). The masonry dam has been divided
into twelve blocks and a corewall. Work has been taken up in ten blocks leaving Blocks III and
IV for river flow. In Blocks I and II, pegmatite zone was encountered. Considerable delay in
construction work in these blocks was experienced until a decision on the handling of the pegmatite
zone was made. Various geological solutions were suggested by experts but final decision was
made by the PWD to completely remove the pegmatite. Work is currently under way in these
blocks.A construction diversion sluice would be constructed in Block II. After the completion of
this sluice river water would be diverted through this block and work would be taken up in Blocks
III and IV. In the remaining blocks, excavation and foundation concreting has been completed.
Masonry construction is at varying degree of completion in these blocks. Construction of Core wall
is awaiting the release of necessary land from the Forestry Department. Work is progressing in the
stilling basin area. Excavation of 2.23km of main canal has been completed and further work is in
progress. In the canal there would be one pick-up weir cum head sluice, twentyfour superpassages,
one Im canal drop, two undertunnels, sixteen aqueducts, eight canal syphons, three footbridges, five
culverts and a tail dam. Out of these, nine structures have been completed and work is in progress
in the remaining fiftytwo. A head sluice with screw gear shutter would be provided at the entrance
point of the main canal taking off from Varahanadhi channel. Similar shutter arrangements would
be provided at the seven sluice points as well.
30.   The scheme command is covered by three agricultural extension centers. Farm inputs supply
and distribution are mainly handled by the above extension centers, three cooperatives and eight
private dealers. Typic Haplustalfs, deep calcareous soils with moderately fine texture and Typic
Ustorharts, shallow non-calcareous soils with moderately coarse texture are found in the scheme
command. The former is suitable for paddy-based cropping system and the latter for non-paddy
crops. Crops grown at present are paddy, groundnut, sugarcane, cotton, sorghum and sesame. In
the future areas under some of these crops would be increased and dryland crops such as sorghum
and sesame would be dropped from the cropping pattern. Crop calendars are given in Figure 5.4.
Water balance computations have been carried out taking into account the availability of
supplementary irrigation from wells'". Sugarcane cultivation would be increased in order to
supply canes required by a recently established sugar mill in the scheme area. In the new area
brought under irrigation, groundnut would remain the main crop in the northeast monsoon followed
by some cotton and chilies. Areas that do not have supplementary well irrigation, would grow
samba paddy as the main crop in the northeast monsoon season followed by some groundnut within
the limit of the available water supply.

the tank outlets and sluices will be the responsibility of these associations. The inlets to the tanks

Mordhana Scheme
34.   The Mordhana Scheme would be located on the Koundinyanadhi, a tributary of the Palar
river. There are no storage facilities built on the main stem of this river. Fortyfour supply channels
draw water from the river and supply water to a direct ayacut area of 4046 acres and an indirect
ayacut of 4175 acres supplied through 17 tanks. The objective of this scheme is to provide a
storage facility on the main stem of the river to regulate the flash floods and deliver water to the
ayacuts and stabilize an area of 6388 acres and provide irrigation to the gap area of 704 acres.
35.   The Mordhana dam would be located at Longitude 7847'E and Latitude 1303'N. The
catchment area of Koundinyanadhi at Mordhana damsite is 1431 sq.km of which 1069 sq.km lies
in Andhra Pradesh,354 sq.km lies in Karnataka and 10 sq.km lies in Tamil Nadu.The average annual
inflow to the reservoir created at this site is estimated to be 110 MCM. However the flow in the
river is highly variable. During the period 1978-1992, the minimum annual flow at the damsite was
1.6 MCM and the maximum recorded annual flow was 654 MCM. The live storage capacity of the
reservoir would be 6.5 MCM. The reservoir would be impounded behind a masonry dam 470m
long and about 24m high at the maximum section. The size of the resevoir is such that even if
major consumptive use schemes are executed in the territories of Andhra Pradesh and Karnataka,
(which is highly unlikely since the river traverses mostly through forest zone in these States), there
would be sufficient water to fill the reservoir as designed. The mean annual rainfall in the
command area is 886mm. About 90% of the rain falls during May-November period. Rainfall and
other climatic particulars including evapotranspiration values computed by modified Penman-



- 93 -
Annex 5
Page 10 of 23
Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(iv). Inter-annual variation of rain is not very high although
intra-monthly variation of rain is quite high. Flood flows would be handled by a 398m long"
uncontrolled spillway capable of passing the routed 10,000-yr flood safely. The crest of the spillway
will be of ogee type with R.C.C. 1:2:4. The bodywall of the spillway will be in CR masonry in
cement mortar 1:3 with chisel dressed stones for its front and rear faces of 45cm thickness and the
core wall will be in RR masonry in cement mortar 1:4. A 24m long stilling basin with suitable
endsill would be provided to ensure safe dissipation of energy.
36.   A main canal will deliver irrigation releases from the reservoir to the direct ayacuts and the
tanks commanding indirect ayacuts. This arrangement is to avoid leaving the irrigation flows in the
river to be picked up at the off-take points. Since the maximum irrigation release would be in the
order of about 8 cumecs only, the river cannot be used as a delivery mechanism particularly in the
navarai12 cropping season. The main canal would be located on the left bank of Koundinyanadhi.
It would traverse through eighteen villages and terminate in the command area of Kalinjur Village
at 44+650. The canal would be divided into thirteen reaches and in the end of the second reach
would feed a branch canal which would cross the Koudinyanadhi and supply ayacut located on the
right bank of Koundinyanadhi (1480.27 acres). Eleven distributaries would take off from the main
canal to irrigate lands located on the left bank of Koundinyanadhi and Palar (1789.14 acres +
1696.54 acres). The main canal would have a bed slope of 1 ft per mile and would be designed for
a flow velocity of about 2 ft/sec.(see table of areas commanded by distributaries and the canal and
tank system). Unlined earth canal, regulator and head sluice for branch canal, head sluices for
distributaries, cross drainage structures, syphons, bridges and road culverts would be constructed
under the project.
37.   The scheme command is covered by six agricultural extension centers located at each block
involved in the scheme. Farm inputs supply and distribution are mainly handled by the above
extension centers and a number of village coperative societies and private dealers. Typic Ustrepepts,
deep, moderately coarse textured calcareous soils, Fluventic Ustrepepts and Udic Haplustalfs which
are deep, moderately fine textured, non-calcareous soils are found in the scheme command. These
soils are generally suitable for all crops grown in the scheme command. About 85% of the cropped
area is paddy and the remaining is sugarcane and pulses. Paddy yields vary depending on the level
of moisture stress. Main constraint to production increase in the scheme command is lack of
adequate and guaranteed water supply to the crops. In the future, area under sugarcane can be
expected to increase with greater reliability of water. At present, where groundwater facilities are
available to supplement tank irrigation13, several paddy-based cropping systems are practiced:
double cropping of paddy (Samba - Navarai), triple cropping of paddy (Samba - Navarai -
Sornavari) and paddy followed by sugarcane in a three-year rotation. The gap area is usually
planted with rainfed pulses. With completion of proposed works, Paddy area under Navarai would
increase considerably and there would also be a substantial increase in sugarcane area. Paddy would
1 This length is included in the 470m length quoted for the masonry dam.

remain the domninant crop and triple cropping of paddy would be practiced within the limits of
available water supply. Crop calendars are given in Figure 5.5.
38.   The estimated cost to complete the scheme is Rs659.21 million (US$21.26 million) including

39.   The scheme would generate an incremental production of 9383 tons of paddy, 29,250 tons
of sugarcane, 213 tons of pulses and 941 tons of groundnut. It is estimated that a total of 4185
families would benefit from the scheme. A beneficiary with a 1.5 acre farm would obtain an
incremental income of Rs5000 annually whilst a beneficiary with a 2.5 acre farm having access to
supplementary well irrigation would obtain an incremental income of Rs1O,395 per year.
Employment opportunities would be provided by the scheme to about 8000 to 10,000 farm workers.
Overall cropping intensities would increase from 127% to 185%. Full benefits are expected to
accrue from year 2000. Partial benefits are expected to begin to accrue from 1997 based on
expected rate of completion of the main canal and distributaries. The economic internal rate of
return is estimated to be 13%.
40.   Water user associations will be formed at each of the benefitting tank and sluice command.

Rajathopekanar Scheme
41.   The objective of this scheme is to stabilize and improve agricultural production in 545 acres
presently irrigated through five tanks which are fed by waters of the Rajathopekanar river through
an open offtake and an unlined channel delivery system. The Rajathope river originates in Andhra
Pradesh and after flowing for a short distance of 2km enters Tamil Nadu and travels another 5km
upto the proposed reservoir site. An earth dam 273m long with an additional 53m long spillway
and 1 1.87m high at its maximum section is being constructed at Longitude 7904'55"E and Latitude
1301'05"N. This dam on the Rajathopekanar river would impound a reservoir with a live storage
capacity of 0.78 MCM. The river drains 8.6 sq.km upto the damsite. The average annual inflow
to the reservoir is estimated to be 1.90 MCM. The five tanks have a combined storage capacity of
1.6 MCM. The constraint in filling these tanks with the existing arrangement is the low carrying
capacity of the supply channel which makes it difficult to tap the river flows which are quite flashy.
Under this scheme, some of the flash flows would be retained in the reservoir and released through
the canal sluice to the supply channel at the rate of about 20 cusecs. All the five tanks would be
filled in about 30 days.
42.   A river cum canal sluice would be provided at LS 134m of the earth dam. One vent of the
sluice would have a higher sill elevation of +283m to allow water to flow to the tanks through the
existing delivery channel. The other vent would have a sill elevation of +281m to discharge



- 95 -
Annex 5
Page 12 of 23
necessary flows to the river channel during low-flow or no-flow seasons. The canal vent would be
0.90m x 1.00m and would be capable of discharging 24 cusecs. The river vent would be 0.90m x
1.50m. A 53m long spillway would be provided from LS 137m to 190m taking advantage of the
availability of good rock strata at this location at reasonable depth. A connecting channel 10 to 15m
long would be excavated to deliver the spill flows to the Rajathopekanar river.
43.   The scheme command is covered by two agricultural extension centers located at each block
involved in the scheme. Farm inputs supply and distribution are mainly handled by the above
extension centers and a number of village cooperative societies and private dealers. Agricultural
research works for the scheme command are conducted by two research stations at Virinjipuram
(crop research) and Melallathur (sugarcane research). Udic Haplustalfs, deep, moderately fine
textured, non-calcareous soils and Typic Ustochrepts, deep, moderately fine textured, calcareous soils
are found in the scheme command. These soils are moderately suited to paddy-based cropping. The
existing cropping pattern in areas without supplementary irrigation is Samba in the northeast
monsoon season followed by Navarai season paddy limited by water availability in the tanks. In
areas with supplementary well irrigation",double cropping of paddy is the dominant cropping
pattern with triple cropping of paddy and a three-year rotation of paddy with sugarcane or banana
practiced in a small area. In other areas samba paddy is usually followed by pulses grown with the
residual moisture of the previous paddy crop. With the completion of scheme works and the
availability of secure and increased water supply, it is expected that some increase in the sugarcane
and banana areas and triple cropping of paddy would take place. Average annual rainfall in the
command area is 886mm. About 90% of the rain falls during May-November period. Details of
rainfall and other climatic parameters including evapotranspiration values computed using modified
Penman-Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(iv). Crop water requirements have been estimated
for proposed cropping patterns. Crop calendars are provided in Figure 5.6. Water balance
computations have been carried out to establish water availability for the scheme and are on file.
It has been determined that together with the existing wells and storages and yields that can be
mobilized in the system tanks, the scheme would have adequate water to meet the crop water
requirements. In addition water would be available for domestic use for the villagers living in the
vicinity of the command area of the scheme.
44.   The estimated cost to complete the remaining works is Rs4O million (US$1.3 million)
including physical and price contingencies. Detailed quantities and costs are given in Table 5.3(v).
It would take another two years to complete the scheme. Implementation schedule is given in
Figure 5.1(v).
45.   The scheme would generate an incremental production of 2760 tons of sugarcane, 50,400
bunches of banana and 189 tons of paddy. It is estimated that 300 families would benefit from the
scheme. A beneficiary with 1.5 acre farm holding would obtain an incremental income of Rs3,500
per year whilst a beneficiary with a 2.5 acre farm having access to supplementary well irrigation
would obtain an incremental income of Rs 11,968 per annum. In addition agricultural employment
opportunity would be created benefitting about 900 to 1000 farm laborers. Overall cropping
intensities would increase from 161% to 169%. Full benefits would begin to accrue from 1996.

Kodumudiar Scheme
47.   The scheme envisages construction of a 405m long and 28m  high dam  across the
Kodumudiar at Longitude 7732'E and Latitude 0826'N which would impound a 3.6 MCM
reservoir. The average annual inflow to the reservoir is estimated to be about 45 MCM. The dam
would be located at the confluence of Kodumudiar and Kombiar rivers. Below the confluence the
river is called Thamaraiar which is a tributary of Nambiyar. The Kombaiar river originates on the
eastern slopes of the western ghats at an altitude of 1400 masl. The stream travels for about 7km
before it joins the Kodumudiyar. Similarly Kodumudiyar originates in the eastern slopes of the
western ghats at an altitude of 1650 masl, and joins the Kombiar. The combined stream called
Thamaralar travels 2km to join the Nambiyar above the second anicut on the Nambiyar (the
Dalavoipuram anicut)'5. The commands developed under the Thamaraiar and Nambiyar river
systems are fed through a number of channels taking off from a series of anicuts across these rivers.
The Kodumudiyar is flowing on the right side of the Kombaiyar. Kombaiyar divides into two arms
and the right arm joins the Kodumudiyar. At this junction point a high-level contour canal called
the Valliyooran channel takes off and feeds the Valliyoor big tank and a series of tanks after flowing
for a distance of lOkms. There is a masonry anicut across Thamaraiyar which is the only anicut
across Thamaraiyar and is called Thamarai Anicut. An irrigation channel called Padalayar channel
takes off from this anicut on the right side and feeds four tanks besides irrigating some direct
ayacut. There are nine anicuts in the Nambiyar river complex. An irrigation channel takes off from
the second of these anicuts namely Dalavoipuram anicut. This channel is called the Vadamalayan
channel which feeds 36 tanks besides irrigating a direct ayacut of 696 acres. Improvements to
Padalayar, Valliyooran and Vadamalayan channel systems to achieve better water management and
to increase agricultural production and incomes in areas commanded by them would be the scope
of the Kodumudiyar Scheme to be financed under this project. Fifteen villages would benefit from
this scheme. About 1954 acres would be stabilized and another 1925 acres which are currently
classified as gap areas would be provided full assured irrigation through the construction of on-
channel storage on the Kodumudiar.
48.   Formation of a composite dam to a total length of 405m across Thamaraiar with earth section
from 0+00 to 270+00 and masonry section from 270+00 to 405+00 with the following breakdown:
river sluice from 270+00 to 279+00, uncontrolled surplus weir 279+00 to 328+00, canal sluice to
supply Valliyooran channel from 328+00 to 337+00 and a non-overflow section from 337+00 to
405+00. Excavation of surplus course for 175m. Improving and remodelling existing Padalayar,
15 Cross-reference should be made to Nambiyar scheme.



- 97 -
Annex 5
Page 14 of 23
Valliyooran and Vadamalayan channels and the tanks fed by these channels. River sluice would
discharge the flows required for Padalayar and Vadamalayan channels. The river sluice would have
a sill level of +136.00 and would be able to pass 450 cusecs. An R.C.C barrel 1.5m x 1.8m would
form the sluice which would be provided with service and emergency gates. The canal sluice would
have a sill level of +144.00 and would discharge into the Valliyooran channel. The barrel size
would be 2.0m x 2.8m and the sluice would be able to pass 1000 cusecs. The 175m long and 49m
wide surplus course would deliver the spill flows back to Thamaraiyar. Hard rock is exposed at the
surface in the vicinity of the spillway and therefore no additional energy dissipator is necessary.
Spill flows would be delivered to the rock bed.
49.   With the scheme, Valliyooran channel would receive water from the high-level canal sluice.
The channel would be improved to handle rush supplies upto 1000 cusecs. This channel would
deliver water to the four tanks in its system and carry flows to be diverted to 23 tanks of the
Vadamalayan system.  A cross-regulator would be constructed just above the entrance of
Valliyooran channel to the Valliyoor big tank and the existing left bank outlet would be improved
to divert required flows to the Vadamalayan channel to feed 23 tanks out of its 36 tank command.
The Padalayar channel takes off from Thamarai anicut and this channel would be improved to carry
495 cusecs to feed the four tanks and direct ayacut under its command.The Vadamalayan channel
taking off from the Dalavoipuram anicut has already been improved upto 3.1km of its 22.85km
length. Under the scheme a further 5km would be improved and lined to minimize conveyance
losses (this section passes through porous soils). The channel would be modified to handle 450
cusecs. This channel feeds 36 tanks - 13 are fed in the reach 0 to 8km. The remaining 23 would
be fed by flows diverted from the Valliyooran system as described above.
50.   The scheme command is covered by three agricultural extension centers. Farm inputs and
supply distribution are mainly handled by the extension centers. A number of village cooperative
societies and private dealers also handle chemical fertilizers and pesticides in the area. Typic
Ustropepts, moderately deep, medium textured soils and Typic Ustorthents, shallow, non-calcareous
medium textured soils are found in the scheme command. The former soils are suitable for paddy-
based cropping system and the latter for non-paddy crops. Present cropping pattern is paddy
(Pishanum) followed by pulses in the regular tank commands.  In areas having access to
supplementary well irrigation"6, double cropping - Kar paddy followed by Pishanum paddy,
Pishanum paddy followed by cotton or chilies - is practiced. In addition a small area of banana is
also cultivated in a three-year rotation with paddy (2-yr banana, 1-yr paddy). With completion of
the proposed works, it is expected that the existing irrigated command area including the gap area
would receive a more secure water supply. A slight increase in chilies, cotton and banana areas is
expected with the increase in available water under the scheme. Average annual rainfall in the
command area is 740 mm. Inter-annual rain variation is quite high as evidenced by a C, value of
0.46. About 50% of the rain falls in the months of October and November. Details of rainfall and
other climatic parameters including evapotranspiration values computed using modified Penman-
Monteith method are given in Table 5.2(ii). Crop water requirements have been estimated for
proposed cropping patterns. Water balance computations have been carried out to establish water
availability for the scheme and are on file. It has been determined that together with the existing

wells and storages and yields that can be mobilized in the system tanks, the scheme would have

Vadakku Pachayar Scheme
54.   The Pachayar basin drains about 250 sq.km. The Pachayar basin also gets water of the
Keelamanimuthar basin through Karumandiamman anicut situated on the western side of Pachayar
Hills and also from Netterikal anicut situated upstream of Karumandiamman anicut.There are twelve
anicuts across Pachayar and one across Vadakku Pachayar namely the Madathu anicut. Some of
the catchment area contributing to the flow in the river system is influenced by both the southwest
and northeast monsoons (catchment contributing to Keelamanimuthar diversions). Flood flows occur
in the October to December period and the withdrawal capacity of the channels at the various
anicuts being limited, most of the flow goes past the anicuts to the sea. On the average about 25
MCM is surplused at the last anicut on the Pachayar. It is proposed to form a reservoir across
Vadakku Pachayar'7 and constructing a diversion weir across Pachayar below the second anicut
and divert the flood flows through a 800m long feeder canal into the Vadakku Pachayar reservoir.
17 Reservoir across Pachayar was contemplated but dropped due to its submerging some valuable forest lands.
The current proposal to create an impoundment on the Vadakku Pachayar avoids such submergence.



- 99 -
Annex 5
Page 16 of 23
55.   The scheme envisages the formation of an earth dam 3115m long and 20m high at the
maximum section impounding a reservoir across Vadakku Pachayar river"8 with a live storage
capacity of 12.4 MCM (expected to be filled twice a year). The dam would be constructed at
Longitude 7731'45"E and Latitude 08032'30"N. Head sluice with three vents 2.8m x 2.5m each
would be provided with a total withdrawal capacity of 2369 cusecs. A scour sluice with one vent
2.8m x 2.5m would be provided as well.
56.   The average annual inflow to the proposed reservoir on Vadakku Pachayar is 18 MCM. In
order to augment this flow a 60m long diversion weir will be constructed across Pachayar river
below Thalai Anicut and the flood flows of Pachayar river will be diverted through an 800m long
feeder canal. The feeder canal will be lined to minimize cross-section required to handle the
diverted flows when passing through already developed and cultivated areas. Lining would be
75mm thick and made of 1:3:6 precast concrete slabs. The canal bed width would be 10m and full
supply flow depth would be 2.5m. Necessary cross-drainage structures would be constructed.Two
drop structures would be constructed to negotiate the fall in ground level at +500+00 and +800+00.
57.   An uncontrolled weir will be constructed at left flank end 122m long together with an
associated surplus course. The surplus course would be 300m long and would deliver the spill flows
to Pachayar river through a natural watercourse. The weir would be capable of passing 1281
cumecs, which is the routed outflow of the 10,000-yr flood, safely with minor encroachment on the
freeboard provided. The surplus course would have a bed width of 80m and at full flood flows
would flow at depths of around 2.5m. The natural watercourse would be widened wherever
necessary.Excavated earth from this component could be used for dam construction'9. Cross-
drainage structures would be provided as required.
58.   A 3300m long link canal will be excavated to deliver water from the reservoir to the
Nanguneriankal. The following structures will be constructed on the link canal: regulator with two
shutters 1.5m x 1.5m each at LS 50m, aqueducts at LS 250m and 2300m, culverts at LS 1350m and
3350m, and required cross-drainage structures. The canal would be lined with 1:3:6 precast slabs.
Bed width of the link canal would be 4m and the carrying capacity would be 282 cusecs. On the
branch canal the following structures will be built: one shutter 1.5m x 1.5m at the offtake point of
the branch channel together with a drop structure to handle a 2m drop, an aqueduct with five vents
of 6m span each at LS 250m - RCC trough 4m wide over RR masonry piers and abutment, culvert
at LS 1350m 4m span RCC slab on RR masonry abutments, aqueduct at LS 2300m to cross the
existing supply channel to Mudukulam - 2m span RC slab with RR masonry abutments.
59.   A river sluice will be constructed at 1300+00 of the main dam to deliver water to irrigate
existing and proposed ayacuts. The waterway size would be 1.53m x 1.83m and the discharge
capacity would be 298 cusecs.

with supplementary well irrigation , the dominant cropping pattern is double cropping of paddy.
Groundnut and banana are also grown in a small area in rotation with paddy under rainfed
conditions. Groundnut is also grown under rainfed conditions in paddyland with no irrigation water.
Paddy grown in the tank/well area produces relatively higher yields reflecting less water stress than
paddy grown in tank irrigation alone.
64.   With completion of the proposed works, it is expected that the existing irrigated area would
receive a more assured water supply and the gap area would become a more productive irrigated
area. Double cropping of paddy would be practiced to the extent possible within the limit of
available water supply (water balance computations refers). The cultivation of existing non-paddy
crops except pulses would continue under the scheme with an increase in their cropped areas. Crop
water requirements have been computed on the basis of the cropping pattern and calendar given in
Figure 5.8. Water balance computations are on file.

Adavinainarkoil Scheme
68.   Hanumanadhi is a major tributary of Chittar river which in turn is a tributary of the
Thambaraparani River. Hanumanadhi originates on the eastern slopes of the Western ghats at an
altitude of 1570m.a.s.l. The river traverses 6.4km in the hilly region and a distance of 11.20km in
the plains before its confluence with the Chittar river above Veeraman anicut near
Veerakaralampudur village of Tenkasi Taluk. At the damsite the river drains about 16 sq.km. The
average annual inflow into the proposed reservoir is estimated to be 26 MCM21.
69.   At present irrigation water is being supplied through a system of tanks fed by small channels
taking off from the diversion anicuts constructed across Hanumanadhi. There are nine anicuts upto
the confluence point of Hanumanadhi and Karuppanadhi. The Hanumanadhi system comprises these
nine anicuts seving some 38 tanks. At present the ayacut under Hanumanadhi does not get assured
irrigation supply from the existing anicut system even though there is ample water resource potential
in the river. By constructing a dam and creating an impoundment, the irrigation system would be
converted from an anicut-based system to a reservoir-based system and the ayacut would be given
better assured irrigation supply during both cropping seasons.
70.   The scheme envisages the construction of a 680m long masonry dam, 43.70m high at its
maximum section across Hanumanadhi river impounding a reservoir of 4.9 MCM capacity. The
dam would be located at Longitude 7714'06"E and Latitude 0904'00"E. The entire structure
would be of stone masonry with foundation concrete. The structure would be founded on solid rock.

72.   Irrigation deliveries would be made through two sluices: Mettukal sluice and Karisalkal cum

74.   The average annual rainfall in the command area is 1165mm22. The area receives rain both
in the southwest and northeast monsoon seasons. The monthly distribution of rain is given in Table

sunshine hours. Evapotranspiration values computed using modified Penman-Monteith method

75.   The scheme command is covered by four agricultural extension centers each located at block

private dealers. Typic Ustropepts, deep, moderately fine textured, calcareous soils and Udic
Rodustalfs, deep,medium textured, non-calcareous soils are found in the scheme command. These
soils are moderately suitable for paddy and upland crops under irrigated conditions.
76.   The existing cropping pattern in the tank irrigation area is paddy (Pishanum) in the northeast
monsoon season followed by rainfed pulses. Double cropping of paddy is practiced subject to
availability of tank water. A small area of coconut plantation also receives tank water. Areas with
access to supplementary well irrigation23 also have a similar paddy-based cropping pattern.
Groundnut and banana are also grown in a small area and, like other scheme commands in
Tirunelveli District, banana is grown in a three-year rotation with paddy. In the gap area, sorghum
is planted under rainfed conditions and some rainfed Pishanum paddy is grown as well. In the
dryland area which would be brought under the scheme command in the future, the main cropping
pattern in the pre-scheme situation is groundnut followed by pulses under rainfed conditions.
77.   With completion of proposed works, it is expected that the existing rainfed area would
become a more productive irrigated area and the existing irrigated command area would receive a

Gridhammal II Scheme
81.   At present the tanks are filled with excess river flows through supply channels with open
offtakes at various points in the Gridhammal river. In each crop season people of the area build
temporary diversion structures24 made of clay, silt and sand. There are six offtakes and six supply
channels. Irunjirai, Athikulam, Maraiyur and Varisaiyur supply channels take off on the left side
feeding the four tanks of the same name and commanding a total area of 2042 acres. The Ulakkudi
and Manur supply channels take off from the right side of the river. Ulakudi supply channel feeds
Ulakkudi tank which commands 454 acres. The Manur channel feeds Manur, Athithanendal and
Narikudi tanks which command a total area of 602 acres. Under the scheme these open offtakes
would be replaced by one intake on the left bank and one on the right bank at the 1.4m high and
60m long diversion weir to be constructed under this project across the Gridhammal river. These
intakes would be capable of delivering the required flows to fill the respective tanks within a short
flood duration.
24 Locally called mud korambu.

82.   Irrigation water will be delivered to these two intakes via head sluices located on both banks.
Protection against siltation will be provided by scour sluices that would be located adjacent to the
head sluices. The left head sluice would have four vents each having 1.00m x 0.90m opening
capable of passing 5.6 cumecs in total. The right head sluice would have two vents having 1.10m
x 0.90m opening capable of passing 3 cumecs in total. The tanks would be filled in about one week
time. Vaigai-Gridhammal link channel is expected to operate for a period of about one week at the
rate of about 28 cumecs and therefore there would be sufficient water and time to fill the tanks.
The scour sluice on the left would have four vents with 1.65m x 1.70m opening and the scour sluice
on the right would have three vents with 1.1 Om x 1.70m opening with the upstream and downstream
aprons at the anicut apron level. The sill of the head sluices would be kept 0.30m above that of the
scour sluices.

Appendix 1. Background information on traditional irrigation infrastructure
96.   Irrigated agriculture has been practiced in Tamil Nadu for more than a thousand years.
Rainfall is influenced mainly by the northeast monsoon in the cultivable areas. This monsoon rain
is concentrated in the months of October, November and December. The remaining period of the
year is usually dry. Even the monsoon rain can be erratic both in its onset and end and in quantity
as well. The ancient kings who ruled parts of Tamil Nadu had devised a system of small to medium
sized storage reservoirs referred to as tanks to collect the rainwater and to use it for irrigation.
These tanks originally were constructed to receive the flows generated in their own catchments.
With the progress of time, the yield of the tank catchments were supplemented by water diverted
from nearby streams and rivers. The recipient tanks are referred to as system tanks and the other
type being commonly called rainfed tanks. The diversion is achieved by masonry structures called
anicuts built across the streams a few centuries ago. Some areas are irrigated through sluices
provided in the supply channels taking water from the diversion weirs to the tanks. These areas are
called direct ayacuts and the commands under system tanks are called the indirect ayacuts. In
recent years, some more tanks are receiving supplementary water through temporary diversion
structures built each year by the farmers referred to as mud korambu. Below these tanks, a fully
developed traditional irrigation system is in place. However, yields from the catchment of the tanks
have reduced particularly over the last two decades due to changes in land use and abstractions
within the catchment. The supply channels and the open offtakes at the anicuts are limited by
design and are therefore unable to extract additional flows from the rivers. Hence the cultivated
areas are no longer assured of a secure irrigation supply. Most of the area gets partial supply and
upto 25% of the commands on the average does not get any water at all and it is either left as
fallow or cultivated under rainfed conditions with poor yields. Some farmers have constructed
shallow wells to supplement the tank water. Yields of crops in such areas are higher than in regular
tank-fed areas reflecting the effect of moisture stress.

WATER POLICY FOR TAMILNADU
I. PREAMBLE
The National Water Policy enunciated by the Government
of India in 1987 has recognised that water is a prime
natural resource, a basic human need, and a precious
national asset. Water as a resource is one and is
encompassed in the hydrological cycle; river water, surface
storage, and ground water are all part of one system; water
is also part of the larger ecological system. Water is one
of the most crucial elements in developmental planning. As
the State prepares itself to enter the 21st century, it must
develop, conserve, utilise, and manage this important
resource on an integrated and environmentally sound basis to
meet the growing needs.
With a high percentage of the State's surface water
resources already utilised, the scope for constructing new
surface water storage reservoirs is limited. More efficient
water management is the most needed measure to ensure
additional productivity from water in Tamilnadu.
Opportunities for conservation and reuse in irrigation,
industry and municipal water systems together with sector
policies developed to promote conjunctive use of surface and
groundwater and effective conservation measures in all water
use sectors need to be pursued.
Water quality of surface and ground water sources must
be maintained to established standards in order to protect
designated users from deterioration of the resource. Water
legislation and regulations, the design of facilities, and
the management of basin water resources shall be directed to
maintain water quality appropriate for designated uses and
to permit maximum reuse of all water following each
diversion. The government through the appropriate agencies
shall provide' means and measures to manage all aspects of
water development beneficially, including timely control of
groundwater levels, restriction or treatment of wastes;
maintenance of appropr:ate water 2uality as designated for



- 109 -
Annex 6
Page 3 of 7
each point in the river network, forecasting of suppliea,
and direction of individual project operations to meet
developmental goals and policies.
II. GOALS AND OBJECTIVES
Within the framework provided by the National water
policy, the broad objectives of the water policy for
Tamilnadu shall be
a. Establish a ManaBemenit Information System (MIS) for
water resources.
b. Ensure preservation and stabilisation of existing
water resources.
c. Plan for augmentation of utilisable water resources.
d. Promote research and training facilities for water
resources management.
e. Establish allocation priorities ior water use by
different sectors with provision of drinking water
being of highest priority.
f. Maximise multi-purpose benefit from surface and
ground water, land and other resources.
g. Provide adequate water for domestic users.
h. Maximise hydro-power generation within the constrains
imposed by other water users.
i. Provide adequate water for industry.
J. Preserve and enhance the economic fisheries.
k. Maintain water quality to established standards.



- 110 -
Annex 6
Page 4 of 7
1. Promote equity and social justice among users of
water from irrigation and domestic water supplies.
m. Plan for economic and financial sustainability based
upon the principle that those who benefit from
projects and programmes should also pay for them.
n. Provide flood protection and drainage.
o. Promote users' participation in all aspects of water
planning and management.
p. Provide mechanisms for the resolution of conflicts
between users within and between intra-state river
basins.
III. ACTION PLAN
1. This water policy will apply to all the water
resources in'the State. The Water Resources Control and
Review Council (WRCRC) will monitor the implementation of
che policy. The Institute for Water Studies (I.W.S.) will
be the Technical Secretariate for W.R.C.R.C.
'. River basin will be the unit for water planning.
Small river basins can be grouped together for the
convenience of management and administration.
3. The I.W.S. will be the Nodal Agency which will
collect and store all data related to water resources in the
State. The I.W.S. will utilise the modern facilities and
techniques for storing and retrieving data, basin modeliing,
water balance studies for planning new uses, etc. In this,
I.W.S. may also seek the technical expertise from the
Academic Institutions in the country.
4. Necessary information system for the flow of data
from the field to the I.W.S. will be built up.



- 111 -
Annex 6
Page 5 of 7
5. The I.W.S. will prepare water balance studieo and
prepare macro-level basin plans and assist the W.R.C.R.C. in
allocating water for different sectorial users keeping the
objectives in mind.
6. Regulation and control of ground and surface water
will be put on sound legal footing. Legislation may be
enacted dealing with ground and surface water and their
conjunctive use.
7. For maximising the benefits from the available water
resources, the following steps will be taken
a. Efficient management of watershed to minimise
sedimentation.
b. Removal and prevention of encroachment in water
courses and water bodies.
c. Restoration of the capacities of the existing water
bodies.
d. Modernisation of the physical systems.
e. Avoidance of transmission losses in the conveyance
systems by lining, providing pipe lines, etc.
f. Minimisation of evoporation losses.
g. Adoption of modern methods of irrigation
applications.
h. Planning of recycling and reuse of water, wherever
possible.
i. Minimisation of leakages in pipe lines in drinking
water system.



- 112 -
Annex 6
Page 6 of 7
8. For augmentation of water resources the following
will be attempted :
a. Artificial recharging of ground water.
b. Interlinking the river basins within the state.
c. Co-ordinating with the National Water Development
Agency (N.W.D.A.) for implementing the National Water
Grid (N.W.G.).
d. Planning for cloud-seeding discreetly.
e. Encouraging rain water harvesting by the individuals
and organisations through simple techniques.
f. Adopting desalination techniques in coastal belts.
9. For maintaining the quality of water the following
steps will be followed :
a. Monitoring for water quality and ensuring strict
compliance of "The Water (Prevention and Control of
Pollution) Act, 1974" for the designated use.
b. Preventing over extraction of the ground water in the
coastal belt to avoid sea water intrusion.
10. The flood control and drainage component will be
included in the basin plans to be prepared after identifying
the extent and intensity of these problems and necessary
control measures will be detailed. Watershed management,
flood forecasting for reservoir operations, flood plain
zoning, and prevention of flood plain encroachments by human
settlement and obstructions to flow will be considered along
with structural measures such as embankments and flood
channels. Fast communication net-work will be created for

11. A drought management plan will be prepared for each

a. Employment-oriented schemes will be formulated and
kept in shelf for implementation during drought
period.
b. In the irrigated areas, contingency plans will be

(c) Turning over the maintenance and management of
the distribution system to the beneficiaries in
stages will be promoted.
14. The existing water rates for water use in different
sectors will be periodically reviewed and rationalised.

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Sectoral.  Environmental  Assessment  S rya/
introduction
1.    The Tamil Nadu Water Resources Consolidation Project (WRCP) will
introduce a more rational and sustainable system of water use and management
in the State of Tamil Nadu. In preparation for the project, a Sectoral
Environmental Assessment (EA) has been carried out in accordance with the
World Bank's Guidelines. The result of this work was a comprehensive report
covering the environmental issues of the water resource sector for the whole
state, examination of the impacts of the investment components under the
project, and preparation of an Environmental Management Action Plan which has
been incorporated in the WRCP project proposal. The Environmental Assessment
was carried out by HR Wallingford Consultants of UK in association with
environmental consultants from Indial, the Public Works Department and other
relevant state and national bodies. Social impact analysis and preparation of
an economic rehabilitation programme for families affected by land acquisition
under the project has also been carried out with assistance of Operations
Research Group consultants and is described in Part II of this sunmmary.
Assistance and guidance provided by World Bank officials and officials of the
Ministry of Environment is gratefully acknowledged.
PART 1. - ENVIRONMENTAL REPORT AND ACTIONS
2.    The focus of the EA has been the overall environmental isaues facing the
water resources sector in Tamil Nadu. This included a review of the existing
legal and institutional framework as well as study of the physical
environmental issues in the state as a whole and the specific environmental
impacts of the project's investment components. An Environmental Action Plan
(EAP) has been prepared and proposals made for inatitutional changes to ensure
that the present technical requirements, organization and environmental skills
are improved so that they are better able to deal with environmental issues in
the future. This Executive Summary presents the main points arising from the
environmental assessment and social impact studies.
Project Objectivea and Description
3.    The WRCP comprises a seven-year programme to improve planning and
management of the state's water resources and to improve the productivity of
irrigation. Objectives are: (i) introduce water resources planning by river
basins across all uses of water, incorporating environmental management of
water in decision processes; (ii) improve agricultural productivity through
modernization and completion of existing irrigation syatems and farmer
1 Principal consultants: Drs P. Bolton, B. B. Sunderesan, A. Hall
a/ Source: GOTN, August 1994



- 115 -
Annex 7
Page 2 of 17
participation; (iii) assure sustainability of infrastructure and the
environment; and (iv) improve institutional and technical capability for
managing the state's water resources. Proposed project components would be:
(a) Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover ( involving investment in base
costs totalling Rs. 4820 million or USS 153 million; 56% of base costs);
(b) Completion Works on existing schemes (Rs 2070 million or US$ 66 million;
24% of base costs);
(c) Water Planning, Environmental Management and Research (Rs. 1100 million
or USS 35 million; 12% of base costs); and
(d) Institutional Strengthening (Rs 684 million or USS 22 million; 8% of base
costs).
4.    The Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover programme responds to the
state's need to improve water management on existing commands which are old
and in poor repair. As a result water distribution is poor resulting in
unreliable water, shortfalls in tail reaches and waterlogging at headends. It
would involve rehabilitation and modernization on about 650,000 ha comprising
about 50% of the state's surface irrigated area. The programme would be
conducted on 17 major and medium irrigation commands and 40 minor commands.
Works are confined to the existing irrigation distribution network comprising
repair and as needed additional water control structures, and repair and
selective lining of canals, plus measuring devices and a radio communications
network. Farmers would also be provided topographic assistance in their
construction of water channels for irrigating their fields. The programme is
technically similar to the successful National Water Management Project
(Credit 1770-IN) already implemented in Tamil Nadu on 150,000 ha, but involves
a two-stage investment approach and integration of farmers in investment
decisions and part of investment cost, and arrangements with Farmer Councils
for take-over by farmers of operations and maintenance of distributaries plus
association of farmers in overall scheme management. Maintenance would also
be strengthened: government funding has been increased to full levels
required, and will be continued to the required levels for sustainability of
the infrastructure.
5.    Scheme Completions involve completion works on nine existing commands to
improve availability and reliability of irrigation supplies. The works
typically involve improvement and completion of the existing irrigation and
drainage system, including additional canals and bypass channels as needed,
and supplemental storage through small reservoirs (eight reservoirs in total
with an average area of 67 ha). Total land acquisition needs amount to about
600 ha, and the associated land acquisition and economic rehabilitation
programme is described in Part II. All schemes have been subject to
individual environmental assessment and appropriate environmental safeguards
incorporated, and environmental clearance by state and central government
authorities has been obtained for each sub-project.
6.    Other project components do not involve physical works, and comprise
strengthening of water planning and environmental capabilities and associated
policy reforms, consistent with the recently formulated "Tamil Nadu State
Water Policy', and Government's objectives to manage the state's water



- 116 -
Annex 7
Page 3 of 17
resources holistically; catering to both the needs of different sectors and
safeguarding the environmental sustainability of water resources.  To
implement these objectives under the project, a Water Resources Organisation
(WRO) has been created from the former irrigation wing of the Public Works
Department. WRO will be strengthened under the project to emphasise a broader
management role for state water resources and improved capability in all
technical areas; from water planning to water management. A Water Resources
Control and Review Council (WRCRC), comprising all parties involved with water
use and environmental management has also been created, and state water
planning and allocation will be implemented under the WRCRC and its technical
Secretariat, the Institute of Water Studies. Priorities for public
expenditure have also been overhauled, with emphasis now on maintenance and
water management rather than new construction.
Baseline Data
7.    The project area includes commands distributed throughout the State of
Tamil Nadu, and basin planning has state-wide coverage. The EA was based on a
thorough review of the water related environmental conditions prevailing
throughout the State. Conditions in the major river basins were examined and
a detailed assessment was made of the Bhavani river basin to provide a model
for future studies to be carried out by IWS. Comprehensive data was collected
from numerous Government and non-Government sources and extensive
consultations were held with NGOs and individuals covering all aspects of the
water related environment.
8.    Tamil Nadu with a population of 55.6 million has 7% of India's total
population; but only 4% of its land area and only 3% of its water resources,
and is thus water short relative to other states. The state's average
rainfall is 925 rn/annum, but is highly variable, both yearwise and
geographically. Tamil Nadu is affected by two monsoons, the northeast monsoon
(October to December, average 440 mm/annum) and the southwest monsoon (June to
September, 310 mm). Rainfall is highest on the coast, decreasing westwards as
the impact of the northeast monsoon diminishes. Water resources are estimated
to be 23.6 billion cubic meters of surface water and 22.3 billion cubic meters
of groundwater. The construction of tanks and reservoirs over the last 100
years has harnessed the surface water to a large extent and there is little
opportunity for further development of this nature.
9.    The soils are generally fertile and lack of suitable soils is not a
constraint to agriculture which is the principal activity in the State.
Irrigated agriculture is a key activity in the local economy, covering 2.4
million ha under canal, tank and well irrigation or 41% of the state's net
cropped area. Rlice is the main crop grown in irrigated areas, with increasing
development of groundnut, banana, sugarcane, cotton, coconut, vegetables and
other cash crops. Farm holdings are typically small, with about three-
quarters of land holdings less than 1 ha.
10.   The state comprises 17 river basins, all flowing eastwards from the
Western Ghats and the Deccan plateau. Amongst these, the major rivers are the
Cauvery, Palar, Vellar, Vaigai, Bhavani and Tambaraparani. Tamil Nadu either
contains a basin in full or is a downstream riparian. For one river, the
Cauvery, shared with upstream Karnataka, water sharing has not been agreed and
Tamil Nadu has decided to undertake no investment in disputed parts of the



- 117 -
Annex 7
Page 4 of 17
basin. While basin planning in the Cauvery would be part of the project, the
WRCP contains no plans for investment in disputed parts of the Cauvery basin.
Tamil Nadu does not contain any internationally shared rivers.
11.   Approximately 95% of the surface water resources are utilized and 90% of
this for irrigated agriculture. The demand for potable water supply and water
for industry is increasing and is expected to expand significantly in the
future in line with urban and industrial growth. Gross usage will remain
dominated by agriculture, but near urban centers, municipal and industrial
needs will become important to the basins concerned. Basin planning and
allocation on a cross-sectoral basis is needed, including reallocation as
necessary to serve the state's changing needs and growing imbalances.
12.   Madras city's specific water needs are acute.  Serious water shortage
and rising population and industrialisation have necessitated severe water
rationing and recourse to bringing water in by train and lorry to supplement
meager supplies. Low pressure in pipes also results in contamination posing
health risks beyond those posed by shortage of water for washing and other
hygienic purposes, especially for poorer families. The state has two projects
under preparation for possible World Bank financing: the Veeranam Water Supply
project would bring water from Veeranam tank to Madras, and a subsequent
project would provide water supply and sanitation services to Madras using the
Krishna Water Supply Project as a source.
13.   Groundwater pumping has progressively increased and it is now estimated
that 60% of groundwater resources are utilized, mostly for irrigated
agriculture, but with significant withdrawals for urban use near major
centers. Groundwater utilization varies by basin. In a number of locations,
especially around Madras, Coimbatore and Madurai and parts of the Palar and
vaigai basins, withdrawals are causing groundwater levels to fall. In
affected coastal areas, saline intrusion is also occurring.
14.   The State has a wide variety of flora and fauna, mainly in the hilly
regions in the western ghats, and the forest areas are rich in plant and
animal life. The State has a long coastline and marine fisheries are an
important source of income. Most of the coastline is developed but there are
small areas of mangrove and a wildlife sanctuary at Point Calimere covering
1,700 ha of tidal swamps.
15.   Only 1% of Tamil Nadu's population belongs to scheduled tribes, and the
main concentrations of tribal groupa are in the hill country and will not be
affected by the project.
16.   The WRCP is not an isolated project but builds on experience under
previous interventions in the water sector, including assistance from World
Bank and other donors. In particular, the project's main component follows
closely on the experience and succeases gained from the National Water
Management Project (NWMP) funded by the World Bank.
Environmental Assessment
17.   The environmental assessment provides an overview of the water resources
sector of the State and covers issues both within and beyond the scope of the



- 118 -
Annex 7
Page 5 of 17
WRCP. The findings of the environmental assessment have been taken into
account during project preparation through integration of environmental
considerations into the project design. Below is a summary of actual or
potential environmental issues that have been reviewed by the environmental.
assessment, with recommendations as appropriate on actions to be taken.
Environmental Tmpact of Project Investments
18.   The impact of the WRCP's investment components have been examined at
design stage on a scheme-by-scheme basis and with reference to the
environmental status of the existing schemes and experience with previous
similar investments. The Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover progranune is
found to be environmentally beneficial, in line with the positive experience
already gained under the National Water Management Project, where 150,000 ha
have been improved in Tamil Nadu. The rehabilitation and modernisation to
improve water management directly tackles current widespread problems of poor
water distribution. By improving water distribution, problems of excess water
in headreaches, leading to water logging and rising groundwater and potential
salinisation, would be tackled, as would the reverse problem of insufficient
or no water in tail reaches, reducing productivity and potentially causing
groundwater levels to fall, as well as reducing pressure to pump groundwater
by farmers. The project would thus have positive impact on the state's
groundwater situation. Schemes have been subject to careful hydrological
analysis, and the investments, entailing repair and improvements on the
existing distribution systems, would not cause environmental disruptions.
Sustainability of the systems, would be ensured through improved maintenance
and involvement of farmers in water management and maintenance.
19.   The Scheme Completions have each been subject to detailed environmental
analysis from the perspectives of hydrological impact, impact on flora and
fauna, forest impact, soil erosion, siltation, safety of dams and construction
practices, and found environmentally acceptable by state and central
government and the consultants. Completion of small reservoirs averaging 67
ha in size are involved in eight of the nine scheme completions. Loss of
forest land totalling 107 ha for the nine schemes combined is considered
acceptable, particularly as this impact would be greatly outweighed by the
catchment management programme to be funded under the project.  A positive
environmental impact is that the scheme completions, through the additional
storage and link canals, enable resolving present inadequacies in water
management in the commands concerned. The main environmental impact resulting
from the reservoirs and additional distribution canals is due to the need for
land acquisition (600 ha in total). Full land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation provisions have been made, as discussed in Part II.
Other Water seetor Related Tmsues
Summarised below are other environmental issues reviewed in the environmental
assessment with comments where appropriate on impact or actions included under
the WRCP.
20.   Need for Water Planning.  With surface water almost entirely utilized
and groundwater exploitation increasing, improved water resources management
is critical to the future social and economic development of the State. This
is the most important need in an environmental action plan for Tamil Nadu13



- 119 -
Annex 7
Page 6 of 17
water resources, as basin planning is central to all initiatives. A more
holistic approach is needed than hitherto in resolving water resource and
related environmental issues. Sectoral environmental issues need to be taken
into account in the preparation of river basin plans for future development.
As little potential exists for the development of new sources of supply other
than interstate water transfer and desalination, water resources will have to
be well managed and conserved if future demands are to be met. Planning and
allocation, including reallocation as needed, will in particular need to find
solutions to the growing needs of industry and domestic use, and improved
efficiency of use in agriculture. The WRCP's water planning initiatives,
already underway, would provide an important start to improved water resources
management for the state.
21.   Tmnroving Rnvironmnttal CrapAhlity in WRO. Environmental capability is
assessed to need strengthening in WRO. The institution needs capability to
handle environmental matters in all relevant aspects of its work. The EA
recommended creation of environmental units in IWS (WRO's state water planning
agency) and with Chief Engineer, Project Formulation, plus environmental cells
at the levels of the basin managers, and an environmental sub-committee in the
Water Resources Control and Review Council, together with supporting actions
to strengthen surface and groundwater hydrological measurement, laboratories
for water quality testing, supporting research actions and provision of
consultancies, training and equipment to improve technical capability, all
included under the EAP.
22.   Watpr Mana    n.  The important need for improving surface water
deliveries and drainage within existing conuands (improving productivity and
efficiency of water use, reducing pressures on rainfed land as a result of
increased output and employment in irrigated areas and increased biomass for
cattle fodder and fuel, reducing pressures for groundwater pumpir,g as a result
of reliable and equitable water supplies, correcting waterlogging/salinisation
in headreaches and inadequately drained areas) would be specifically addressed
under the project's investment programme.
23.  crri   dwatpr Prntei2n.  Better management of groundwater exploitation
is needed, and will become increasingly important over time. Whilst not
c. .Lcal over the State as a whole, there are increasing areas of over-
e -Atation. The project's investment components are not supporting
g. .;dwater development and would reduce pressures on groundwater and
ele..tricity use for pumping, as a result of more reliable and equitable
surface supplies. More broadly, however, actions were recommended for overall
groundwater sector management and included: groundwater monitoring to be
strengthened through additional measurement facilities and strengthened data
collection, processing and analysis; establishment of a groundwater regulatory
mechanism and supporting legislation to limit licenses and usage in areas of
overexploitation; and price incentives to discourage overpumping.
24.   CatehmAnt orotection.  To date, land and water management of catchments
has been on a piece-meal project by project basis which has not sufficiently
taken into account complex inter-relationships within the catchment.
Vegetative cover ranges from excellent to poor, as for instance, in parts of
the Bhavani basin where soil conservation and forestation measures are needed.
The formation of the River Basin Managers under the WRCP will lead to better
catchment management and coordination at the basin level between the numerous



- 120 -
Annex 7
Page 7 of 17
agencies involved in catchment activities. Basin plans will be drawn up which
will identify degraded catchments and propose suitable conservation measures
and address problems such as eutrophication. Funding of catchment management
actions in critical areas would be provided under the EAP through WRO
provisions to the relevant agencies after detailed plans have been prepared
and reviewed by the WRCRC'3 environmental committee and basin managers.
25.   Water Oual;ty  Water quality is becoming a serious issue in some areas.
For example, in the Palar basin industrial pollution of ground and surface
water by the tanneries needs to be regulated, effluent problems are associated
with most urban centers, and salinisation of groundwater needs monitoring and
remedial actions where appropriate. The EAP would assist in improved water
quality management through the various technical and institutional
strengthening activities: (i) the establishment of the WRCRC as the top level
body to identify problems, prepare strategies and coordinate actions with
other agencies, and the basin managers at local levels; (ii) improved
monitoring, data collection and the establishment of a central data system;
(iii) groundwater actions as discussed above; and (iv) special studies under
the research programme in problem areas.
26.   wjldlifp.  The State has abundant wildlife but not within the command
areas included in the project and there is, therefore, little possibility of
harming or enhancing it. Within the project areas the tanks are the most
beneficial habitats for wildlife, especially bird life. Those tanks which are
identified by the Wildlife Department as having significant bird populations
are designated sanctuaries and under the guidance of the Forestry Department.
WRO retains the right to control the water but enables the trees and birds to
be protected. The project investments will not have any adverse affect on
this arrangement.
27.           _        .mmunity  with rehabilitation, improved maintenance and better
water management, the WRCP is expected to have a beneficial impact on
community health by improving nutrition as a result of the greater food
production and wealth created, and by reducing the incidence of some water-
related diseases through reduction of areas of stagnant water. The
recommendation to include health officials in the WRCRC and review of basin
plans and future sub-project environmental assessment is included in the EAP.
28.    naUrgJyLe.  The WRCP is not financing groundwater development.  Impact
of the project on energy use by rural pumpsets is assessed to be towards less
consumption of energy for this purpose. This would result from the impact of
the project's systems improvement program which would improve the reliability
and equity of water distribution on half of the state's surface irrigated
area. Farmers with pumpsets in irrigated areas would have less need for
groundwater pumping, and energy use is expected to be reduced based on field
observations in already improved comnands.  Quantitative assessment of the
magnitude of this impact is, however, not available, and is a recommended
topic for research under the project's Water Resources Research Fund.

Environmental Action Plan
30.   An Environmental Action Plan has been prepared and is designed to ensure
that environmental considerations form an integral part of the duties of the
new WRO and that all opportunities for environmental enhancement are carried
beyond the planning stage into implementation. The EAP comprises the
following components, which have also been summarised in the attached matrix:
(a) State Water Policy. Environmental management features have been
incorporated in a Tamil Nadu State Water Policy prepared in 1993/94 and
approved by Government of Tamil Nadu in July 1994.  This has been prepared
consistent with the National Water Policy (Government of India, 1987) and with
the overall objectives of the Water Resources Management policy paper issued
by the World Bank in 1993. Tamil Nadu's water policy highlights the water
scarcity faced by Tamil Nadu and the need for management as a unitary
resource; by river basin, conjunctively for surface and groundwater,
integrating environmental management, planning holistically for all uses of
water, prioritizing water between sectors including drinking water and the
growing needs for industrial and urban requirements, and improving the
efficiency of water use in the agricultural sector, the largest user of water.
Specific actions discussed in the policy backstop actions relevant to WRO's
responsibilities included under the WRCP and environmental action plan.
(b)  Tnstitutional Needs. The reorganisation of WRO includes establishment of
a water planning and allocation apparatus and work schedule to plan water by
river basin and on a holistic basis. A Water Resources Control and Review
Council was created in September 1993 as the highest state body for water
planning and allocation decisions. The WRCRC is chaired by the Chief Minister
and comprises membership of all Departments connected with water use, supply
and related environmental issues. WRO's Institute of Water Studies has been
appointed Secretariat of the WRCRC and Nodal Agency for state and basin
planning. Under a revised work schedule for IWS, commenced in 1993 in
preparation for the WRCP, initial basin assessments have been carried out on
16 of Tamil Nadu's 17 river basins, and the remaining basin and a State
Framework Water Plan will be completed by December 1994. More in-depth Basin
Plans and a detailed State Water Plan will be prepared in the following three
years, and subsequently updated on a periodic basis.
Under the EAP an Environmental Committee will be established within the
WRCRC to provide specific high-level attention and expertise to environmental
matters in water planning. An Environmental unit has been established in IWS
to provide staff capability for water planning within IWS, and an
environmental unit also established with WRO's C.E. Project Formulation, which
will have responsibility for carrying out environmental impact assessments for
all future investment proposals and will ensure that all new works conform to
the statutory regulations, both Central and State Government, before work



- 122
Annex 7
Page 9 of 17
commences. WRO's Basin Managers will also have environmental cells
responsible for the functional aspects of environmental activities at field
level. The RMB cells will monitor environmental issues at the basin level and
coordinate with local government and non-government organizations and
individuals ao that environmental concerns are fully addressed by WRO. Work
will include monitoring of water quality and point pollution episodes, and
contributions to the environmental aspects of the river basin plans.
(c)   Measurement and Monitoring Capability. Capability to measure and monitor
all aspects of surface and groundwater hydrology including environmental
aspects such as water quality (salinity, organic and inorganic pollutants) and
groundwater situation will be strengthened under WRO's new Water Resources
Data Center including expanded facilities for field measurement, laboratory
equipment, and computers and other equipment for data storage, retrieval and
dissemination (to be funded under the National Hydrology Project). Under the
WRCP, WRO's Institute of Hydraulics and Hydrology and Irrigation Management
and Training Institute will also be strengthened, including in aspects related
to environmental management.
(d)   rrondwatpr Manag-ement.  The following actions would be taken within
WRO's area of responsibility. Groundwater legislation would be prepared to
establish a framework for regulating new development, development restrictions
in areas where groundwater resources are threatened, licensing and setting
limits on withdrawals. A Groundwater Regulatory Directorate would be
established in WRO responsible for implementing groundwater regulatory
actions. These measures would establish the needed regulatory apparatus in
WRO to improve management of groundwater development and exploitation.
(e) Catchmant Management. The EAP includes a funding provision of Rs 60
million (US$ 2 million) for ecorestoration of impoverished catchments with
high soil erosion and siltation problems. Intensive investigation and
implementation plans would be developed in the first two years of the project
followed by channelling of funds by WRO to the concerned agencies (forest
department, agricultural engineering, agriculture, environment, etc).
(f)   Procaedures and Guidelines.  WRO will prepare procedures and guidelines
for environmental assessment and will check that environmental issues are
properly addressed at the design and implementation stages of any works
undertaken, to ensure GOI and GOTN statutory requirements are met. A code of
good construction practice will be prepared and included as part of future
construction contracts.
(g) Consultancy Services and Training. This will form an important part of
the EAP as most staff will be engineers redeployed from other tasks and will
thus initially have a limited knowledge of environmental problems.
Consultancy services will be used to assist the WRO and provide on-the-job
training, for carrying out any EIA that may be needed and for advice on the
preparation of procedures, guidelines and codes. A consultant will be
appointed as Expert Advisor (part time) to the WRO for the first three years
until the IWS can fulfil this function. Staff training and recruitment for
c6re positions has commenced. Training will include specialist training for
WRO's environmental staff, and awareness training for different staff levels
and functions for other staff. Seminars and workshops involving other
Government departments and members of the public will also be arranged to



- 123 -
Annex 7
Page 10 of 17
encourage general awareness in the state on water related environmental
issues.
(h)   Applied Research Program.  Research topics have been identified as
necessary to better understand and solve some of the sectoral problems
mentioned above. Research would cover groundwater recharge studies, pollution
assessments, wastewater reuse, catchment processes and interlinkages,
sedimentation, health hazards and other topics as appropriate. The research
will be managed by IWS and will be on a collaborative basis with involvement
of other units within WRO such as IHH, and local universities. Modest funds
have also been set aside for a twinning arrangement with an overseas institute
which would contribute technical expertise to the research program, contribute
to training and provide specialist short term consultancy inputs as required.
31.   Equipment, consultancy, training, laboratories, the ecorestoration
program and other expenses related to the above activities have been detailed
and included under the WRCP proposal, amounting in total to Rs 270 million
(USS 8.7 million).
Overall Assessment
32.   The WRCP will introduce substantial institutional changes with the
creation of a new Water Resources Control and Review Council and Water
Resources Organisation. Under this new institutional structure the focus of
activity will shift from development of new works to water resources
management. Environmental awareness and capability will be integrated into
mainstream activities through the Environmental Action Plan included under the
project. The physical works included in the project are low impact activities
which are substantially confined to the improvement of existing irrigated
command areas and will lead to more efficient and sustainable water use. The
project's limited land acquisition and economic rehabilitation needs will be
handled comprehensively, including creation of institutional capability and
comprehensive economic rehabilitation based on detailed socio-economic surveys
and action plans (refer Part II).
33.   The project is in line with the principles set forth at the Dublin
Conference on Water and the Environment for improved management of water
resources. No significant negative impacts have been identified resulting
from the project, and, if implemented according to design, the project will
lead to environmental enhancement. The Environmental Action Plan and Land
Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation program form an integral part of the
project and are aimed at ensuring this enhancement.
