Annex 7
Page 11 of 17
INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJZECT
SECTORAL ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT SUMMARY
PART I:. - SOCIAL ISSUES
1.    The WRCP integrates in project design the objective expressed in the
Tamil Nadu State Water Policy (1994) that sustainable planning, construction,
management and maintenance of water infrastructure must involve the public and
in particular the users. By so doing, results more responsive to needs can be
obtained, the energies and talents of the non-government sector can be better
utilized, and a sense of 'ownership" develops, leading to creative use of
decision forums and better care of infrastructure.
2.    Farmer Participation.  The Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover
Programme, the project's principal component, integrates farmer participation
in the rehabilitation and modernization process, and in subsequent operations
and maintenance. Farmers have been involved from the outset of project
planning activities, and considerable interest in participation has been
evidenced. This has been fostered by WRO staff, consultants and NGOs through
a series of field based and more formal meetings and workshops. Training of
WRO staff and field extension efforts is now in a more intensive phase, in
preparation for the major effort required for this progranmne. On several
commands, farmers have already mobilized to form informal councils, and
collected funds from their members. Investment will be phased and directly
linked to the degree of farmer organization achieved. The final phase in
particular would require substantial cormitment from farmers, and would
involve turnover of operations and maintenance of distributaries to farmer
councils, duly organized as registered societies under acceptable by-laws, and
under a memorandum of understanding with WRO spelling out the rights and
obligations of each party. Prior to investment, farmers would contribute Rs
250/ha to capitalize their maintenance account, and would contribute 20%
against the investment costs of distributary linings, and handle themselves
the bulk of the construction work for watercourses and field channels.
Implementation of this programme is recognised by WRO as its major challenge,
and has already been the subject of a substantial staff familiarization and
training effort, and assistance from consultants.
3.    Public par-tiipation anA NrnO-.  Basin planning, while primarily
implemented by WRO, will involve a multi-sectoral decision forum through the
WRCRC created for this purpose, and local advisory committees at basin level
including non-governmental participation. Other components such as the
research fund, consultancy arrangements and training make extensive use of
resources available in Tamnil Nadu's universities, in India and overseas,
including a particular focus on technology transfer, necessary to WRO's
objectives to create specialization and state-of-the-art approaches to the
increasing complexities of water management in a resource constrained
situation. WRO will also sponsor seminars of various kinds to develop public
understanding of water management and environmental issues, and to tap ideas
available from the general public, academia and business sectors. NGOs will
be used as the primary party for field implementation of economic



- 125 -                      Annex 7
Page 12 of 17
rehabilitation (see below), and as community organizers assisting WRO 3taff
for farmer participation and turnover.
4.    Women and Scheduled Castes.  The interests of women and scheduled castes
have been targeted for specific care in implementation of, in particular,
farmer organization and turnover, and land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation. Features to handle this have been incorporated in both
components, in the latter through the special socio-economic surveys and
resultant tailoring of action plans answering to the specific needs of
individuals as well as cormmunities, and in both components through use of
trained community organizers, preferably from the local community.  In the
case of farmer participation, an effort is being made to staff each two-person
NGO team with a man and a woman, so that outreach will stand the best chance
of fully integrating women's interests.
5.    Poverty Alleviation.  The project is directly supportive of Tamil Nadu's
focus on poverty alleviation. Most farms in Tamil Nadu are small. The
average holding size is 1 ha, with 71% of holdings less than 1 ha, and 88%
less than 2 ha. The project would improve the incomes and living standards of
small (less than 1 ha for irrigated lands) and marginal (less than 0.5 ha)
farmers - estimated between them to form some 70% of beneficiaries - through
improved and equitable distribution of water, resulting in higher yields,
increased food production and diversification to higher value crops. About
50% (650,000 ha) of Tamil Nadu's surface irrigated area would benefit from the
project. Some 600,000 families (about 3.6 million persons) would be direct
beneficiaries, with spin-off benefits to a larger population (landless, urban
poor) through employment opportunities from more intensive agriculture and
increased and lower price food production. Employment opportunities for
landless families in the command areas would be substantially increased, as
labor needs are closely linked to crop intensification and yields.  For the
urban poor, the project's water planning component would yield benefits
through planning of future bulk water supply needs.
6     The remainder of this social assessment summarises the land acquisition
and economic rehabilitation programmue to be implemented under the project. In
preparation of this component the aims have been two-fold: first to ensure
that the individual needs of persons affected are met; and secondly, to create
capability for handling land acquisition and economic rehabilitation in the
future. The latter has involved creating institutional capability and
adjusting policies and procedures. In turn, this is expected to substantially
affect the chances for success of the first and primary objective.
Land Acquisl$-{n  -a Innnom1c RohAhilsaAtinn
7.    Tamil Nadu intends to ensure that persons affected by land acquisition
activities under the WRCP are provided with full compensation and economic
rehabilitation to enable all such families to retain or surpass their previous
standards of living. This would be achieved through implementation of land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation action plans at each site concerned.
Opportunity has also been taken, based on the recommendationa of a review of
state policy, procedures and experience, undertaken by consultants and
reviewed by the World Bank, to create a permanent institutional capability
within WRO to handle land acquisition and economic rehabilitation under WRO's



- 126 -
Annex 7
Page 13 of 17
responsibility, and to adjust policies and procedures to resolve weaknesses or
constraints in WRO's current practices.
8.    A Baseline consultants survey has reviewed all project sites to
determine the extent of land acquisition involved and the nature of
compensation and economic rehabilitation required. The survey included both
land acquisition for eight small reservoirs to be constructed, and land
acquisition and persons affected by new canals and other distribution
infrastructure. In total, about 600 ha of lands would need to be acquired for
the WRCP, distributed over nine sites as presented below. Households involved
are estimated at 603 comprising 1247 "project affected persons". About 70% of
the 600 families involved would have less than 50% of their land holding
acquired.   No village sites require resettlement and only 12 dwellings would
be affected. The survey found that actions required would be able to be
accommodated without major individual or community disruptions, as the
predominant situation involved agricultural lands only, and often only part of
the holding, and possibilities to acquire alternative lands or other income
earning opportunities were present in the local community.
9.    The baseline survey and preliminary rehabilitation plan assessments are
being followed up by preparation, by ORG consultants in association with WRO
and local administrations, of detailed socio-economic surveys and Land
Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation Action Plans (LAERAPs), following
guidelines provided by the World Bank. On two locations involving one family
each (Rajathopekanar and Kodumudiyar), LAERAPs were found not needed as the
land holdings are large and compensation procedures alone would apply. On the
other seven locations, two LAERAPs are finalized, three ongoing and expected
ready by September 1994, and two are commencing and expected ready in early
1995.

Land Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation Action Programme
10.   The programme would be implemented on the basis of the individual socio-
economic surveys and Land Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation Action Plans
above, including strengthened implementation capability and adjustments in
policies, procedures, allowances and benefits as summarised below.

11.   Tmprovements in Tnstitutional Capahility.  To implement the WRCP's Land
Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation Action Prograrmne, and to establish
permanent capability for future state needs in the water sector, a Land
Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation Office (LAERO) has been established in
WRO, reporting to the Secretary, PWD (G.O. Ms.No.138, Feb 15, 94) and headed
by a Special Officer with experience in rural administration. The LAERO will
be directly responsible for planning, supervising and monitoring the LAERAPs,
and will also serve as the Secretariat for a State level Coordinating
Committee comprising the Secretary of PWD (Chairman), Land Administration
Commissioner, Secretaries of Rural Development, Agriculture, Livestock and
Fisheries, WRCP Director and CE/EIC WRO, and the district Collectors where
LAER is involved. At district levels, Negotiation Committees and Economic
Rehabilitation and Grievance Committees, chaired by the district Collector,
will ensure local coordination, including channelling all relevant district
development progranmes to assist with economic rehabilitation. For each LAER
site, an NGO with suitable hands-on experience in rural development and
community organisation will be contracted by LAERO to undertake primary
responsibility for implementing the economic rehabilitation programme and to
represent the interests of PAPs (rights and entitlements), organise community
participation and handle women's needs, and facilitate access to ongoing
Government development schemes. A full-time WRO official will also be
delegated at each site to provide WRO assistance. The WRCP will fund the
technical assistance and training required by the institutional changes and
activities included under the LAER and economic rehabilitation action
programme. The above arrangements follow the recommendations of the
consultants and the World Bank and have been designed to provide the
implementation capacity to effectively implement the programme.
12.   Tmrovements in Prnnedurem and Econnmin Rehabilitation.  Land
acquisition and compensation in Tamil Nadu is based on the Land Acquisition
Act of 1894 as amended in 1984, and as applied and implemented by relevant
Government Orders. Weaknesses in the present procedures cause delays in
settling land acquisition, cases of inadequate compensation, and incomplete
follow-up to ensure full economic rehabilitation. The procedures and benefits
have been amended for the WRCP.  First, land valuation by negotiated
mettlemant will be an alternative available for PAPs to the present lengthy
methods. It will enable more speed and flexibility in determining
compensation levels based on full market value and transaction costs for
purchase of fully equivalent agricultural land, thus giving more opportunity
for enhanced benefits. A Government Order (G.O.M3.No. 128; Feb 11, 1994) to
put this amendment into effect has been issued. Second, the definition of
PAPs will be adjusted, following recommendations of the World Bank, and will
include landless PAPa (encroachera, sharecroppers and tenant farmers). Third,
arrangements for land prnvis;in will be adjusted. A first option available to
marginal farmers with incomes below the poverty line would be provision of
productively comparable land-for-land with purchase assisted by the NGO. The
land would be identified by the PAPa within a well-defined geographic area
near the village or in the command area. The terms of replacement would be on
an equal basis of one acre of dryland lost to one acre of replacement dryland,
or one-half acre of replacement irrigated land. Compensation paid for the
land lost would be paid to the cost of the replacement land, with any
difference being borne by GOTN. As a second option, a farmer may elect to
receive Rs 14,000/ha of dryland lost (or ?s. 28,000/ha for irrigated land



- 128 -
Annex 7
Page 15 of 17
lost) as Rehahi1itation Grant Fund (RGF) up to a maximum of six acres of
unirrigated or three acres of irrigated land lost. The amount would be
deposited by the NGO in a joint bank account with withdrawal against signature
of the PAP, the NGO and the LAERO. Withdrawals would be linked to purchase of
productive assets such as farm animals or implements, or for self-employment,
or other income-generating activities. Fourth, both the land-for-land option
and the RGF option would be supplemented by provision of an Asset Maintenance
Grant (AMG) of Rs 500/month/family applied from date of takeover of land by
WRO when the landowner loses his right to cultivate the land, and ends with
either possession of replacement land or one year after establishment of the
joint bank account. The AMG would be to ensure that the PAPs are not obliged
to sell existing family assets pending restoration of earning ability.
Finally, through the district economic rehabilitation committees and assisted
by the NGOs, existing rural development programmes would be specifically
targeted to land acquisition sites (many prograimmes exist; eg IRDP, the
sericulture project, horticulture, dairy cooperative development, fisheries,
NABARD credit facilities, programme for development of women and children in
rural areas and various vocational training schemes). The objective would be
to create a record of tangible gain in welfare and earning capabilities for
PAPs. The remaining Government Orders relevant to the above are targeted for
issuance by September, 1994.
13.   The LAER would be supported by IDA funding of equipment, vehicles,
training and consultancies. IDA would also finance LAERO's contracts with
NGOs, including, within the contracts, the NGO's provision of rehabilitation
grant fund and aaset maintenance grant fund. LAER monitoring and special
auditing procedures would apply to ensure the RGF and AMG was used for
creation of productive assets and safeguarding of existing assets. The
consultancy assistance would include an annual review by an independent
consultant of the status of PAPs at all sites. In addition to the ongoing
work to finalize the LAERAPs, staff training is underway and an initial set of
equipment has been purchased to provide LAERO and other staff involved with
the necessary skills and facilitiea to do their work.

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Water Resources Research Fund
1.    Tamil Nadu, through its Water Resources Organization (WRO) and a number of
related agencies will undertake new efforts in water resources planning and
management by basins, taking into account competing uses of water, irrigation
management improvements and intensive farmer participation. A large effort is
required to update knowledge and skills of WRO staff, identify problems at field
level and provide solutions through applied research. The research units within
PWD have been focused on technical aspects and need support to broaden their
research mandate to other areas like agriculture, economics, environment,water
management, and social sciences. Universities and other research institutions
offer excellent research facilities and a broad expertise in these areas, but
academic research has been often not sufficiently operations oriented.
2.    The policy, institutional and technological reforms needed for meeting the
new challenges are complex and need careful analysis, adaptive and applied
research and experimentation. The Water Resources Research Fund (WRRF) under the
WRCP would support a practical program of research to assist in the formulation
and implementation of the substantial reforms to be implemented in the water
sector.
3.    Water Resources Research Fund (WRRF): The Water Resources Research Fund
(WRRF) will fulfill an essential need under Tamil Nadu's program to modernize
management and planning of its water and land resources under WRCP. In total 3
million US$ will be earmarked to fund high quality applied research relevant to
Tamil Nadu's present and future water sector. The WRRF objectives will be to:
i)    Promote the culture of applied research among water and irrigation
professionals in the field;
ii)   Identify and support applied and problem solving research,
specifically addressed to land and water production and
sustainability in the water and irrigation sector, especially at the
river basin level;
iii) Promote research on social issues, such as farmer's and women's and
disadvantaged groups participation in field level activities and
decision making for better management; and
iv)   Promote cooperation between WRO and research institutions from Tamil
Nadu, India, and overseas.
4.    Oraanizational Framework:  The WRRF would be administered by a project
officer in the office of the Engineer in Chief (EIC). The project officer would
convene quarterly meetings, in consultation with the EIC and the Research
Advisory Committee (RAC). He will prepare invitations, and circulate proposals
and the agenda to the committee members.
5.    The Research Advisory Committee (RAC) would be established to ensure the
quality and the applied nature of the research, and to advise the Engineer in



- 132 -               Annex 8
Page 2 of 3
Chief (EIC) on research projects. The RAC would not have administrative
responsibilities but provide scientific and professional advice to WRO on
research matters.  The RAC would screen the research proposals for their quality
and relevance, assess the capability of each applicant to carry out the research,
advise on modifications to the proposal as needed, review the proposed cost
submitted by the applicant, monitor the research progress and suggest mid term
corrections if necessary. Based on the number of received proposals, the RAC
would recommend a priority list of proposals to the EIC (WRO) who would make the
final decision on selection, priority and funding. The RAC would also take a
proactive role in identifying and promoting problem solving research and
technology transfers in the water sector of the state.
6.    The RAC would consist of not more than five senior experts, in addition to
the EIC (or his delegate) as Member Secretary. These experts would have
outstanding capabilities in the following areas of specialty: (i) water
resource/hydraulic engineering, (ii) agricultural science, (iii) environmental
science, (iv) economics and social science, and (v) other areas of relevance.
The members of the RAC would present a mixture of those with practical experience
and those with academic experience and at least one member with experience in the
urban and industrial sector. Members should not include active government staff.
In any situation where a RAC member is actively involved with an applicant
institution, the member would exclude himself from the committee's deliberations
on that proposal. Members would be appointed by the EIC.
7.    Methodoloav:  The RAC would have the following responsibilities:
i)    Organize workshops at Madras and especially at river basin level with
participation from reputed universities, including agricultural
universities, individual researchers, as well as water users , and
identify problems relevant to Tamil Nadu's water use and to specific
river basins;
ii)   Identify problem areas based on their experience and critical review
of water and river basin issues;
iii) Short-list research proposals and identify researchers and
institutions for support;
iv)   Publicly advertise and invite research proposals from within the
state as well as conjunctive research of state institutions with
either Indian or overseas institutions;
v)    Review the research and special study program of WRO under the
responsibility of CE Plan Formulation, IHH, IWS, and IMTI; and
vi)   Submit its recommendations to the EIC, WRO.
8.    Research proposals emanating from different streams suggested above would
be considered by the RAC, and discussed in detail with the applicants. The
proposed research should normally be not longer than 2 years, with a possible
extension to a third year. In exceptional cases the research could continue for
a fourth and final year. Each proposal should provide full justification of
estimated costs (e.g. laboratory equipment, salary of research scholars, research
assistants, enumerators etc., recurring costs for chemicals, glassware etc.,



- 133 -                   Anex 8
Page 3 of 3
costs of demonstrations and piloting, travel costs including national and
international travel but excluding the purchase of vehicles, participation in
seminars/workshops, and contingencies).
9.    The WRRF suggests priority research topics in Tamil Nadu such as:
i)    Cross-sectoral (municipal, industrial, agricultural etc.) issues in
water management and planning;
ii)   Cross-sectoral linkages between agriculture, irrigation, forestry and
land use;
iii)  Hydrology, system design and engineering;
iv)   Water resource allocation, water markets and water prices;
v)    Ground water regulation and pricing (incl. variable cost pricing and
metering);
vi)   Basin management and basin planing;
vii)  Farmer and local participation in water management and system
turnover;
viii) Role of women, marginal farmers, and other target groups in water
management and system turnover;
ix)   Water efficient irrigation systems and new technologies; and
x)    Environmental impacts of irrigation, drainage, urban and industrial
use.
10.   Research proposals submitted to the WRRF have to oriain from research and
related institutions located in Tamil Nadu. However. the WRRF actively
encouraaes research proposals jointly prepared by research institutions from
Tamil Nadu with Indian or overseas institutions. Such conjunctive research
facilitates the transfer of technologies and expertise from abroad to further
improve the water sector. An example for proposed research collaboration is a
proposal by the Tamil Nadu Agricultural University, Coimbatore and International
Food and Policy Research Institute (IFPRI), Washington on "Water Resource
Allocation: Productivity and Environmental Impacts in Tamil Nadu, India".
11.   Financina:  IDA funding of the WRRF under the WRCP would cover 100% of the
grants awarded by the EIC and 100% of honoraria of the RAC and other reasonable
RAC costs. Disbursement would be against expenditures on contracts or against
certificates of expenditures under such contracts. To enable the progressive
build-up of a permanent fund, GOTN would provide matching grants into an interest
earning account for the WRRF with contributions to the account made quarterly
equivalent to one-third of IDA disbursements in the preceding quarter.

TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
LAND ACOUISITION AND ECONOMIC REHABILITATION (LAER) PLAN
A. INTRODUCTION
1.    The Land Acouisition and Economic Rehabilitation Action Plan (LAERAP),
to be implemented and funded under the WRCP and described below, is a
comprehensive action plan to fully address the needs of all project affected
persons (PAPs) under the WRCP. It includes: establishment of a new
institutional structure in PWD/WRO to handle land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation (LAER); revised policies and procedures for LAER including use
of negotiated purchase procedures to provide higher and speedier compensation;
revised definitions of PAPs, including landless and major children; additional
entitlements for PAPs including introduction of economic rehabilitation and
maintenance grants in addition to compensation for land acquisition; targeting
of existing government development programs for specific benefit of PAPs,
including project funding of a fisheries income-generating scheme; and
implementation, with assistance of NGOs, of site-specific land acquisition and
economic rehabilitation action plans. The program is also supported with
consultancy and training assistance, underway since early 1994, and includes
special monitoring and independent consultant evaluation procedures. While
the land acquisition needs for the WRCP are fairly small (para. 6), the
objective of the WRCP's LAERAP is to provide not only a record of tangible and
sustainable gain for the WRCP's PAPs, but to establish permanent capability
and state-wide experience for handling all future LAER in Tamil Nadu's water
sector.
2.    The paragraphs below and supporting attachments provide summary
background of LAER in Tamil Nadu, description of LAER needs, component
objectives, description of institutional strengthening measures, definitions
of PAPs, entitlements, roles of NGOs, training, project support, and
financing. A Matrix, summarizing LAER Obiectives and Actions. is at
Attachment 1. The Implementation Schedule is at Attachment 2. The new
Institutional Structure for LAER is at Attachment 3. Attachments 4 and 5
provide summary data on extent of land acauisition and proiect affected
persons. Finally, Attachment 6 summarizes the fisheries income-aenerating
scheme, also to be funded under the project. The site-specific socio-economic
surveys and LAERAPs, providing details by site and by family/PAP are in
separate supporting documentation. All policy, procedural, entitlement,
institutional (including staffing and initial training), and other measures
described in the action plan below were officially announced by Government
(Government Orders) or implemented during project preparation so as to ensure
a complete and already established basis for the implementation phase of the
LAERAP.



- 135 -
Annex 9
Page 2 of 9
B. BACKGROUND
3.    Because Tamil Nadu has limited water resources, it has not had a history
of substantial resettlement due to the development of large scale reservoirs.
It is also a state which has almost completed the development of its
irrigation potential which means that there is limited scope for new
irrigation and that future emphasis will be on rehabilitation and
modernization of existing irrigation networks. The state uses the Land
Acqusition Act of 1984 (LAA) as a basis for determining land compensation,
though actual procedures may be modified by relevant Government Orders. Given
the limitation on future irrigation expansion, land acquisition needs in Tamil
Nadu are not large, but improved compensation and economic rehabilitation are
needed for all such cases. The project has provided an opportunity to
introduce policy, institutional, and procedural improvements to ensure full
compensation to and sustainable livelihoods of project affected persons
(PAPs). The improvements introduced would be of benefit both to the land
acquisition during the WRCP and for all future land acquisition cases in the
water sector.
4.    The main problem that faces project affected persons in Tamil Nadu has
been their restoration to their former living standards prior to land
acquisition. This is attributable mainly to low level of compensation
provided under the LAA and to delays in processing the land acquisition. For
small and marginal farmers, the gap between possession of the land and payment
of compensation has often resulted in a substantial loss of income and
increasing indebtedness.
5.    Under the WRCP, Tamil Nadu is committed to implementing corrective
measures to address this through: (i) creation of a permanant institutional
capability within WRO to handle land acquisition and economic rehabilitation
in the water resources sector; (ii) adjustment of its policies, procedures and
entitlements to resolve weaknesses or constraints in PWD/WRO's current
practices; and (iii) implementation, with assistance of NGOs and consultants,
of site-specific land acquisition and economic rehabilitation action plans.
C. DESCRIPTION OF LAND ACOUISITION
6.    A Baseline Survey by consultants has reviewed all project sites to
determine the extent of land acquisition involved and the nature of
compensation and economic rehabilitation required. The survey included both
land acquisition for eight small reservoirs to be constructed, and land
acquisition and persons affected by new canals and other distribution
infrastructure. In total, about 570 ha of private lands would need to be
acquired for the WRCP, plus 280 ha of government lands, primarily unused
wastelands, and 110 ha of forest lands (neither of them have any affected
persons), distributed over nine sites as presented below (Attachment 4).
Households involved are estimated at about 710 comprising 1460 PAPs
(Attachment 5). About 70% of the 711 families involved would have less than
50% of their land holding acquired. No village residential sites require
resettlement and, statewide, only 12 dwellings would be displaced by the WRCP



- 136 -
Annex 9
Page 3 of 9
investments.  A number of families are losing more than 75% of their land but
with the Rehabilitation Grants and Asset Maintenance Grants being provided
under the project, it is expected that they will be able to restore their
standard of living without needing to relocate.  The survey found that actions
required could be accommodated without major individual or community
disruptions, as (except for 12 dwellings noted above) land acquisition
involved agricultural lands only, and usually only a small part of the
holding, and possibilities to acquire alternative lands or other income
earning opportunities were present in the local community.
7.    The baseline survey and preliminary rehabilitation plan assessments were
followed by preparation by consultants in association with PWD/WRO and local
administrations, of detailed socio-economic surveys and Land Acquisition and
Economic Rehabilitation Action Plans (LAERAPs). On two locations involving
one family each (Rajathopekanar and Kodumudiyar), LAERAPs were found not
needed as the land holdings are large and compensation procedures alone would
apply. On the other seven locations, socio-economic surveys and LAERAPs have
been completed, with one (Vadakku Pachayar) undergoing final revisions.

D. COMPONENT OBJECTIVES
8.    The objective of this component is to ensure that persons affected by
land acquisition under the Project are left as well off or better off than
their previous situation. To meet this objective, the following measures are
being adopted:
(a) strengthening of the institutional capacity to expedite LAER
through the establishment of a special cell for LAER within the PWD/WRO
and district level negotiations and economic rehabilitation and
grievance committees;



- 137 -
Annex 9
Page 4 of 9
(b) assistance to PAPs provided by NGOs for purchase of
productive assets, for self-employment, or for other income-
generating activities including associated funding through
economic rehabilitation and asset maintenance grants;
(c) assistance to PAPs to facilitate their access, through NGO's
assistance, to existing government poverty alleviation programs and
income-generating schemes.
E. INSTITUTIONAL STRENGTHENING
9.    To strengthen PWD/WRO's capacity to deal with LAER matters, a special
cell for the land acquisition and economic rehabilitation program (LAERP) has
been established in the PWD/WRO, headed by a Special Land Acquisition and
Economic Rehabilitation Officer (LAERO) reporting directly to the Secretary,
PWD/WRO. He will be directly responsible for planning, coordinating and
monitoring land acquisition and economic rehabilitation under the project. He
will also be responsible for contracting with NGOs to carry out site-specific
Economic Rehabilitation Action Plans (ERAPs) for each subproject and to
oversee NGOs' functions and for setting up and providing approval for the
joint accounts established under the ERAPs. Under the special LAER officer
(at senior rank, i.e. IAS), the Cell is staffed with an Assistant Executive
Engineer, two Assistant Engineers and supporting technical and clerical staff,
plus a full- time Assistant Engineer (LAER) at each field site. A consulting
firm experienced in LAER has been assisting the LAER Cell in undertaking
socio-economic surveys, preparing land acquisition and economic rehabilitation
action plans, and training PWD/WRO staff over the past year. A consulting
firm with experience in preparing and implementing resettlement and
rehabilitation action plans would be employed during the WRCP period on a long
term contract to assist the Cell in implementing the LAER component.
10.   The LAERP Cell would serve as the secretariat for a state level
Coordinating Committee for LAER under WRCP. This Committee is chaired by the
Secretary, WRO, and has the following members: Commisssioner, Land
Administration, Secretaries of Finance and Rural Development, Development
Commissioner, WRCP Director, Engineer-in-Chief, PWD/WRO, the District
Collectors where project sites are situated, and the Special Land Acquisition
and Economic Rehabilitation Officer (member secretary).
11.   At the district level, the LAERP Cell would be supported by the
Negotiations Committee and the Economic Rehabilitation and Grievance
Committee. The Negotiations Committee, whose members consist of the District
Collector as Chairman, the District Revenue Officer, and the Assistant
Executive Engineer, LAERO, will have the authority to conduct negotiations
with landowners and/or their NGO representative to establish a full and fair
valuation of the land. The Economic Rehabilitation and Grievance Committee
would be responsible for local coordination, and monitoring the implementation
of the Rehabilitation Action Plans, including channelling all relevant
district development programs to assist with economic rehabilitation. It
would be chaired by the District Collector and would have the following



- 138 -
Annex 9
Page 5 of 9
membership: Project Officer DRDA, District Revenue Officer, relevant
representatives from ongoing development programs in the district, the
Commands' Executive/Assistant Engineers, and the NGOs assisting with the
implementation of the RAPs. The Committee would also be charged with
providing a grievance mechanism to hear and determine fairly and in good
faith, and after taking into account the LAER criteria, all complaints raised
in relation to land acquisition and compensation. Attached is the
organization chart (Attachment 3).
12.   For each LAER site, an NGO with suitable hands-on experience in rural
development and community organization will be contracted by LAERO to
undertake primary responsibility for implementation of the economic
rehabilitation program and to represent the interests of PAPs (rights and
entitlements), organize community participation and handle women's needs, and
facilitate access to ongoing Government development schemes. A full-time WRO
official has been delegated at each site to provide assistance. The WRCP will
fund the technical assistance and training required by the institutional
changes and activities included under the LAER program, and fund incremental
staff needs. The above arrangements have been designed to provide the
implementation capacity to effectively implement the WRCP program and to
create a permanent capacity in Tamil Nadu for future LAER needs in the water
sector.
13.   Definition of Project Affected Person (PAP): Based on the results of the
socio-economic surveys, five categories of PAPs have been identified (the
definitions of small and marginal farmers and the poverty line are the
standard definitions used in India):
Categorv 1: Small farmers (less than 2 hectares of dry land or 1 hectare of
irrigated land) and marginal farmers (less than 1 hectare of dry land or 0.5
hectares of irrigated land)
Cateaorv 2: Landowners with incomes below the poverty line of Rs. 11,000 per
year
Cateaorv 3: Landowners losing more than 50% of their land who are not
included in Categories 1 and 2 or those landowners who have lost complete
access to their lands due to the construction of the reservoir or canals and
are unable to cultivate their lands
Cateaorv 4: Landless PAPs such as sharecroppers, encroachers and tenant
farmers
Cateaorv 5: Major children (18 years or older) of all Categories above with
the cut off date as the date of commencement of construction.



- 139 -
Annex 9
Page 6 of 9
F. ENTITLEMENTS
14.   PAPs whose land or part thereof has been acquired for the purpose of the
Project are entitled to compensation for such land acquired. Moreover, this
category of PAPs, together with the other categories, are entitled to two new
provisions under the project: Rehabilitation Grant (RG) and Asset Maintenance
Grant (AMG).
15.   ComDensation for Land Accuisition.  Under the LAA, district level
Negotiating Committees have been established for the WRCP which has the
authority to conduct negotiations with the landowners to establish a full and
fair valuation of the land. If the negotiating process does not result in
agreement ("consent award"), then land acquisition proceedings continue using
the normal procedures established under the Act. Negotiated settlement is,
however, expected to be the more commonly chosen option of PAPs as experience
using negotiated settlement has resulted in higher and faster compensation
than the standard land acquisition and compensation procedures.
16.   Additionally, two further entitlements would be provided to assist in
economic rehabilitation. The Rehabilitation Grant (RG) would assist PAPs in
acquisition of productive assets for sustainable economic rehabilitation
(para. 17). Also, for all project affected families, an Asset Maintenance
Grant (AMG) would be provided from the date of takeover of land by PWD/WRO
when the landowner loses his right to cultivate the land and ends with either
possession of replacement land or one year after establishment of the joint
bank account for the RG (para. 19).
17.   Rehabilitation Grant (RG): There are two options under the
Rehabilitation Grant. The first option available to small and marginal
farmers with incomes below the poverty line would be provision of productively
comparable land-for-land with assistance by NGO. The land would be identified
by the PAPs within a well-defined geographic area near the village or in the
command area within a specific time frame. The terms of replacement would be
on the basis of one acre of dryland lost to one acre of replacement dryland,
or one half acre of replacement irrigated land, up to a maximum of six acres
of unirrigated land or three acres of irrigated land.   Compensation paid for
the land lost would be applied to the cost of the replacement land. The
government would provide the difference between the amount compensated and the
sale price of the replacement land. The sale price would be negotiated
between a willing buyer and willing seller.
18.   As a second option, a farmer may elect to  receive, in addition to
compensation for land acquisition, a rehabilitation grant of Rs.14,000/ha of
dryland lost (or Rs.28,000/ha for irrigated land lost) up to a maximum of six
acres of unirrigated or three acres of irrigated land lost.   The amount would
be deposited by the NGO in a joint bank account with withdrawal against
signatures of the PAP, the NGO, and the LAERO. Withdrawals would be linked to
purchase of productive assets, excluding land, such as farm animals or



- 140 -
Annex 9
Page 7 of 9
agricultural implements, or for self-employment (e.g. equipment for
establishing a small business) or other income-generating activities.
19.   Asset Maintenance Grant (AMG): For all orolect affected families (PAFs)
(defined in terms of one family represented by one kitchen) choosing either
the land-for-land option or the RGF option, a supplemental Asset Maintenance
Grant (AMG) of Rs. 500 per month would be provided. Payments would start from
the date that the PWD/WRO takes over the land for construction, when the
landowner loses his right to cultivate the land, and ends with either the
possession of replacement land or one year after the opening of the joint
account for the RGF. The purpose of the AMG would be to ensure that the PAPs
are not obliged to sell existing family assets pending restoration of earning
ability. The LAERP Cell would be responsible for making these payments
through the NGOs and would include retroactive payments due.
G. ROLE OF NON-GOVERNMENTAL AGENCIES (NGOS)
20.   NGOs have an important role to play in the implementation of the land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation and action plans (LAERAPs). The
LAERAPs for each subproject have been prepared by consultants who are
assisting the LAERP Cell in identifying one NGO per subproject to assist in
its implementation. For each LAER site, an NGO with suitable hands-on
experience in rural development community organization will be contracted by
LAERO to undertake primary responsibility for implementing the economic
rehabilitation programme and to represent the interest of PAPs (rights and
entitlements), organize community participation and handle women's needs.
Criteria for NGO selection are at Annex 18 of the Staff Appraisal Report.
21.   The NGOs would also work with the PAPs and the ERGC to assist the PAPs
in obtaining benefits from ongoing government poverty alleviation schemes.
The Department of Rural Development has indicated that PAPs under WRCP would
be given priority treatment under these ongoing schemes in their district.
22.   A fisheries income-generating scheme to be conducted by a Fisheries
Officer from the Fisheries Department deputed to WRO is also included
(Attachment 6). This scheme would provide freshwater fisheries and
aquaculture employment opportunities for project affected households in the
vicinity of eight small reservoirs suitable for fisheries enhancement. In
addition to the direct beneficiaries taking up fish production activities, it
is anticipated that others conducting support activities (hatchery operators,
feed suppliers, fish traders, ice suppliers, etc.) in the project area would
also be indirectly benefitted.
23.   Other ongoing government poverty alleviation schemes which have been
identified for targeting PAPs in the WRCP project area include the Integrated
Rural Development Progam (IRDP), the Development of Women and Children in
Rural Areas (DWCRA), special access to NABARD credit for target groups,
Training of Women in Agriculture (TANWA) and ongoing World Bank-supported
projects, such as the Agriculture Development Program-Tamil Nadu, the National



- 141 -
Annex 9
Page 8 of 9
Sericulture Project, and the dairy cooperative program financed by NDDB under
the Second National Dairy Project.
24.   Among the other functions of the NGOs would be:
Creating awareness amoung the PAPs of their rights and entitlements
under the WRCP, the grievance procedures, and options for economic
rehabilitation under existing government programs through meetings and
printed material;
Facilitating women's development to ensure that they are not adversely
affected by the loss of land and that they are able to take full
advantage of the economic rehabilitation program offered by the
government;
Organizing community groups to facilitate community participation in the
economic rehabilitation program, including the identification and
training of community organizers within the village.
I. TRAINING
25.   The object of training for LAER would be to provide the staff of the
subprojects in WRCP with increased knowledge and skills to deal with the
social and economic issues related to LAER. The subjects of training would be
concepts of economic rehabilitation, participatory rural appraisal, women's
development, linkage with other government schemes, community organization and
participation, and the role of NGOs. Training would also include the
preparation of model rehabilitation action plans and setting up indicators for
monitoring and evaluation. The methodology for training would be seminar
presentation, and experience-based learning through group discussions and
group work. Training has already commenced with three R&R training courses
for PWD/WRO staff completed, participatory rural appraisal techniques, and
hands-on operational training, in association with consultants undertaking the
socio-economic surveys and economic rehabilitation action plans.
J. PROJECT SUPPORT
26.   The LAER Component would be supported by IDA funding of equipment,
vehicles, training and consultancies. IDA would also finance LAERO's
contracts with NGOs, including, within the contracts, the NGO's provision of
rehabilitation grant fund. Government would finance land acquisition and
other compensation measures as well as the Asset Maintenance Grants. LAER
monitoring and special auditing procedures would apply to ensure the RGF and
AMG are used for creation of productive assets and safeguarding of existing
assets. The consultancy assistance would include an annual review by an
independent consultant of the status of PAPs at all sites. Staff training is
underway and an initial set of equipment has been purchased to provide LAERO
and other staff involved with the necessary skills and faciities to do their
work.



- 142 -
Annex 9
Page 9 of 9

-     Ensure that project affected persons (PAPs) are as well off or better
off than their previous situation.
-     Under WRCP, an additional objective is to establish an institutional and
procedural capability for any future land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation (LAER) in the water resources sector, thus providing benefits
beyond the specific and fairly limited requirements for land acquisition
during the WRCP period.

and Economic Rehabilitation (LAER) in the Water Resources Sector. (completed).

training, and technical assistance, coordination mechanisms, and
implementation support through NGOs.

fair comDensation and economic rehabilitation for sustainable welfare and
livelihoods. (all actions complete/under finalization).

including channelling all relevant

(e)   Legal Framework                     The State uses the Land Acquisition

addition, to strengthen WRO's
capacity to deal with LAER matters,
Government Orders (GOs) were issued

Special Land Acquisition Economic
Rehabilitation Cell within WRO, (ii)
optional use by PAPs of negotiated
settlement and establishment of
District Negotiations Committees.
Agreement has been obtained (Letter

(i) establishment of District

for PAPs; and (iii) rehabilitation
grants and asset maintenance grants
for eligible PAPs.
(f)   Valuation of and Compensation       PAPs (personally or through an NGO
for Lost Assets and Entitlements          representing the PAPs) will have the
option to request negotiated
settlement . If it does not result in
a "consent award", then normal land
acquisition procedures are followed.
The WRCP would also provide for
Rehabilitation Grants (RG).   One
option would be provision of
productively comoarable land-for-land
identified by the PAPs within a well-
defined geographic area near the
village or in the command. The terms



- 147 -
Annex 9
Attachment 1
Page 5 of 7
of replacement would be on an equal
basis of one acre of dryland lost to
one acre of replacement dryland, or
one-half acre for replacement
irrigated land. Compensation paid for
the land lost would be applied to the
cost of replacement land and the
difference to be borne by GOTN. As a
second option a farmer may elect to
receive Rs. 14,000 of dryland lost
(or Rs. 28,000/ha for irrigated land
lost) as a rehabilitation grant, in
addition to compensation for land
acquisition, up to a maximum of six
acres of unirrigated or three acres
of irrigated land lost. The amount
would be deposited by the NGO in a
joint bank account with withdrawal
against signature of the PAP, the NGO
and the LAERO. Withdrawals would be
linked to purchase of productive
assets or for self-employment or
other income generating activities.
Both the land-for-land option and the
rehabilitation grant option would be
supplemented by an Asset Maintenance
Grant (AMG) of Rs. 500/month/family
applied from date of takeover of the
land by WRO when the landowner loses
his right to cultivate and ends with
either possession of replacement land
or one year after establishment of
joint bank account.

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
LAER - Fisheries Income Generating Schemel
obiective
1.    The objective of the fisheries income generating scheme would be to
develop freshwater fisheries and aquaculture employment opportunities for some
project affected households in the vicinity of 8 small reservoirs suitable for
fisheries enhancement.   The total water area for the 8 reservoirs that would
be developed by the project is 605 ha (Table 1). Also, there are 21 tanks
directly linked and below Rajathope Kanar (5 Q 130 ha), Mordhana (13 Q 2,640
ha), and Sothuparai (3 @170 ha) Reservoirs which could possibly also be
developed for fish production by the project; however, surveys remain to be
done to determine their suitability for inclusion. The income-generating
scheme as presently designed and costed is only for the 605 ha reservoir area.
In addition to the direct beneficiaries taking up fish production activities,
it is anticipated that others conducting support activities (hatchery
operators, feed suppliers, fish traders, ice suppliers, etc.) in the project
area would also be indirectly benefitted. The fisheries sub-component would
be implemented by the Water Resources Organization (WRO).
Table 1. Water Areas Proposed for Development
Reservoir           Tank        Project Affected
Proiect Area                 Area (ha)         Area(ha)      Households (no.)
Northeast Monsoon Group
Rajathope Kanar                25               130                 1
Mordhana                      110             2,640                 66
Sothuparai                     15               170                35
Southwest Monsoon Group
Adavinai Nakoil                35                                 104
Vadakku Pachiyar              185
Kodumudiyar                    50                                    1
Narmbiyar                     155                                 144
Poigaiyar                      30                                 210
Total                           605            2,940                561
Fish Production Technology
2.    The fish production strategy would involve two stages.  The first stage
would concern the culture of suitably sized fingerlings (12.5 - 15 cm) for
stocking the reservoirs and the second would involve the release and grow-out
of fingerlings to market size (0.5 - 1.5 kg each) fish of several species
stocked in a proportion that would optimally exploit the natural feeds
available in the reservoirs. The approximate stocking ratio of fish species
1The information provided in this attachment is derived from a
consultant's report "Scope for Fisheries Development in Tamil Nadu -
Resources Consolidation Project" by Dr. V. D. Singh and a World Bank
Office Memorandum dated June 19, 1994, from Ronald Zweig (ASTEN).



- 155 -
Annex 9
Attachment 6
Page 2 of 4
include catla (Catla catla) stocked at 40%, rohu (Labeo rohita) and mrigal
(Cirrhinus mrigala) stocked at 25% each, and grass carp (Ctenopharyngodon
idella) stocked at 10%. The catla, rohu, and mrigal are endemic to India and
are known as the Indian Major Carp and the grass carp is an exotic species
native to China. The grass carp consumes land and aquatic vegetation. It is
presently propagated and cultured in India, since there is no indigenous
species with the same feeding habits. All of these species are riverine and
do not successfully spawn in still waters which thus requires annual
restocking of fingerlings produced in rearing facilities with the seed derived
from hatchery produced hatchlings or fry.
3.    Fingerling Rearing.  There are three options that could be used for the
culture of fingerlings -- pond, pen, or floating cage culture. If suitable
ponds (in good condition or needing minor improvements) are available in the
vicinity of the reservoirs, it would be best to use these for the culture of
fingerlings followed by pens and then cages. If suitable areas (seasonal water
depth and appropriate soil conditions) are available, pens would be the next
best option. Successful pen culture has been demonstrated in Orissa. If
ponds are not available and pens are not feasible, floating cages could be
used. Effective cage culture techniques for fingerling production have been
developed by the Central Institute for Freshwater Aquaculture (CIFA) facility
in Bangalore. For the project, calculations have been made for the pen
culture option from which the investment and operating cost provision could be
used for pond improvement (if available) or cage culture (if necessary). The
cost for pen construction is about Rs. 44,000/ha divided into 0.25 ha units
and operation is about Rs. 110,000/ha/crop, including all inputs as well as
labor. Fry (3-4 cm) of all species (para. 2) are stocked at a density of
about 250,000/ha with an estimated survival rate of 50% to 12.5-15 cm size.
Approximately 5.5 ha of pens would be required for the 605 ha reservoir area.
4.    Reservoir Fisheries. Two production models for reservoir fisheries would
be followed. The first, or Model A, would be for reservoirs 50 ha and below
(with a depth of 1-2 m) for a total area of 155 ha, and the second, Model B,
for those above 50 ha with a depth of 2-3 m with a total area of 450 ha. From
Table 1, 5 reservoirs would be managed under Model A and 3 reservoirs under
Model B. For Model A reservoirs, fingerlings would be stocked at a density of
1,500 per ha with a production target of 400 kg/ha and for Model B, stocking
would be 1,000 fingerlings per ha with a production target of 300 kg/ha/yr
with a survival rate of about 30% of the number stocked each year for both
Models. The number of fingerlings required for Model A reservoirs would be
about 233,000 and for Model B reservoirs 450,000 for a total of 683,000 (27.5
t), and the total incremental fish production at full development would amount
to about 200 t/yr at a value of approximately Rs. 3.2 million. Growth would be
the result of the fingerlings consuming natural feeds available in the
reservoirs. The types and quantities of fishing gears to be used would be
specified by the WRO, and a closed fishing season would be enacted for two
months following the stocking of fingerlings. Each year's production output,
marketed via fish traders at each site, would be evaluated to determine the
following year's stocking proportion.



- 156 -
Annex 9
Attachment 6
Page 3 of 4
Number of Potential Beneficiaries
5.    Approximately 1 boat (coracle) would be required per 7.5 ha of water
area in the Model A reservoirs and 1 coracle per 10 ha of Model B reservoirs
at a cost of about Rs. 1,500/coracle. This would amount to a total of 66
boats. If the boats, which usually have two occupants, are shared,
approximately 132 households could benefit. In addition, the 5.5 ha
fingerling rearing pens divided into 0.25 ha units, would provide employment
for 22 more households with each provided 1 coracle for pen management. The
total number of households that could be employed by the sub-component would
be 154 or between 25-30% of the estimated 561 PAHs. The average net income
would be about Rs. 14,000 per fishing household per year and about Rs. 2,600
per pen culture household for about 3 months labor. If it is not feasible for
2 PAHs to share 1 coracle, then the number of beneficiaries would be reduced
to 88 households. If the 2,940 ha of tanks were also developed, the number of
beneficiaries could be increased nearly 6 fold.
Organization and Management
6.    The implementation of the fisheries sub-component (reservoirs and pens)
would be conducted by TN Fisheries Officers deputed to the WRO. The
reservoirs, with fishing rights transferred to the WRO, would be leased for a
period of 10 years to groups of PAHs who would pay an annual lease fee. -The
annual lease fees should be set high enough to cover government management
costs, including (a) those for technical extension, management, and monitoring
of the reservoirs by district DOF officers; and (b) a fee to the TN Irrigation
Department at the rate of Rs. 2.5/ha/yr for use of the reservoirs. All fees
would be collected as a fixed, comprehensive fishing licence fee paid by each
fisher that would be paid on a quarterly basis, beginning in each reservoir's
fifth year of operation. The PAH groups would be responsible for rearing and
stocking fingerlings, harvesting fish, and clearing floating vegetation from
the reservoirs. The WRO deputed Fisheries Officers would monitor overall
fisheries management including fingerling stocking, fish landings, and weed
removal.
7.    The WRO should also identify and recruit a suitable non-governmental
organization (NGO) working in the area for a period of 4 years to assist with
the identification and organization of groups of PAHs that would lease and
manage the fishery in the reservoirs. The NGO would also assist the
beneficiaries with establishing savings accounts to cover the closed fishing
season, expenditures required for fishing licence fees, replacement costs for
coracles, nets, pens, and for fingerling production costs. Fees paid for NGO
services would be derived from "NGO Support and Beneficiary Training" (Table
2) which includes Rs. 1,000/PAH/yr for the first 4 years at each reservoir.
Technical Assistance
8.    In addition to the training provided to the beneficiaries by the
Fisheries Department and the NGO under the direction of the WRO, domestic
training (i.e. by CIFA) for the Fisheries Officers deputed to the WRO and for
local consultants from other state Fisheries Departments or private sector
enterprises in which expertize would exist for reservoir fisheries management

Attachment 6

and fingerling rearing. A total lump sum of Rs. 930,000 has been allocated
for this TA during the first 2 years of the sub-component.
Phasing
9.    It is anticipated fisheries activities at each reservoir would be
initiated upon completion of their construction once filled with water after
the next fish spawning season. Table 2 has been organized on the basis of all
fisheries activities beginning in the same year.
Project Cost and Financing
10.   Table 2 provides investment, operating costs, and financing requirements
for the sub-component. All costs for the first 4 years would be financed by a
grant from the Project for a total of Rs. 5.4 million (US$173,000) with
estimated physical (10%) but without price contingencies for the 605 ha
reservoir area.

TNnTA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOTRCFS CONSOLIDATTON PROJECT
Terms of Reference for the Baseline Survey
1.    The objectives of the baseline survey are twofold: (i) to provide the
necessary information to assess the current situation before field implementation
of the project and (ii) to establish development performance indicators to
monitor and evaluate project success. Based on the assessment of the current
situation the baseline survey would provide simple and low cost performance
indicators to monitor project success continuously for all project areas.
Performance indicators form part of the performance review of a particular
activity to be measured against specified objectives. To gain further experience
in monitoring project success of this and other Water Resources Consolidation
Projects in India more detailed baseline surveys followed by a more detailed
monitoring program will be carried out in selected commands. The baseline survey
and the development of the performance indicators will be an interdisciplinary
product jointly produced by irrigation engineers, agricultural
scientists/economists, remote sensing specialists, and water resource managers.
2.    The following list provides guidance of possible performance indicators for
specific project activities. The necessary information for the indicators should
be obtained through the baseline survey in order to assess the current situation.
The list of indicators may not be complete, nor do all of the suggested
indicators have to be used for the monitoring process. It is essential to keep
the monitoring process as simple as possible in order to reduce the workload on
field staff.
(a)   Engineering indicators for operational performance:
i)    Actual dates of irrigation versus planned dates of irrigation;
ii)   Variations in the flow at main canal and distributary heads as a
ratio of their planned discharges;
iii) Variations in the flow at main canal and distributary tail clusters
as a ratio of their planned discharges; and
iv)   Volume of the water received at the distributary head as a ratio of
water discharged at the main canal head.
(b)   Engineering indicators for maintenance performance:
i)    Actual capacity of main canal and distributaries as a ratio of their
design capacities;
ii)   Maintenance cost per kilometer of canal/drain system maintenance
broken up in 3-4 categories of different ranges of canal sizes;
iii) Maintenance cost per hectare of command area;
iv)   Actual maintenance expenditures versus budget targets; and
v)    Actual area irrigated as a percentage of planned area.



- 160 -
Annex 10
Page 2 of 3
(c)   Agricultural production indicators for performance of scheme completions,
rehabilitation and modernization investments:
i)    Area receiving full, partial and no canal water supplies as a ratio
of total command;
ii)   Production of three principal irrigated crops;
iii)  Changes of crop yields in relation to the previous year;
iv)   Changes in the cropping pattern in relation to the previous year;
v)    Changes of farm income in relation to the previous year;
vi)   Amount and value of the three principal crops as a ratio of water
deliveries; and
vii)  Amount of water received per crop (irrigation and rainfall) as a
ratio of crop water requirements.
(d)   Remote sensing to indicate performance of scheme completions,
rehabilitation and modernization investments at command level:
i)    Total area under irrigation; and
ii)   Changes in the cropping pattern.
(e)   Performance indicators for the institutional restructuring of WRO:
i)    Reorganizational benchmarks achieved;
ii)   Benchmarks of environmental action plan achieved;
iii)  Progress on basin/state water resource planning;
iv)   Programming and budgeting plans formulated and submitted on schedule;
v)    Annual training program for WRO personnel approved on schedule by
EIC; and
vi)   Number of senior engineers who received management training.
(f)   Performance indicators for farmer turnover program:
i)    Number of farmer councils at distributary level established and
participating:
a) in planning distributory works,
b) in joint management with WRO, and
c) and taking responsibility for O&M;
ii)   Amount of funds collected by farmer councils for maintenance work at
distributary;
iii)  Number of farmer councils requesting and involved in stage 2
improvements;



- 161 -
Page 3 of 3
iv)   Number of apex committees established and working; and
v)    Number of farmer councils having problems.
3.    The more detailed baseline surveys should cover 2 to 4 major schemes under
rehabilitation/modernization in stage 1 (for example Lower Bhavani, Periyar
Vaigai) and 2 to 4 schemes in stage 2 (for example Kodayar, Amaravathy, Sathanur,
Cumbum). This survey would involve field visits, a stratified farm survey and
intensive remote sensing support with field verification. For the detailed
baseline survey in these areas, WRO could be supported by appropriately
experienced universities and/or research institutions; for example Tamil Nadu
Agricultural University, the remote sensing center at Anna University, and other
Tamil Nadu, India or overseas institutions.
