INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Imnlementation and Supervision Plan
1.    Introduction.  This Annex covers the following material.  In Part A
Project Objectives are summarized as a basis for the implementation and
supervision guide. Part B - Imr,lementation Plan - covers: Project Management
and Implementation; Training and Technical Assistance; Implementation
Schedule; Costs, Financing and Disbursements; Procurement Implementation, and
Economic Analysis. Part C - Manaaement and Supervision Plan - covers:
Management and Supervision Tools; GOTN/GOI Supervision; IDA Supervision;
Monitoring and Evaluation; Programming and Budgeting; Development Indicators;
and Reporting (Baseline Survey, Quarterly Reports, Annual Review Action Plan
and Budget, Interim Review of Farmer Organization and Turnover Program, Mid-
Term Review, and Implementation Completion Report).
2.    Reference is made to the main text and other Annexes.  Attachments to
this Annex comprise:
Attachment 1      Implementation Schedule
Attachment 2      Implementation responsibilities
Attachment 3      IDA Supervision Schedule
Attachment 4      Development Indicators
Attachment 5      Procurement Schedule Format
Attachment 6      Quarterly and ARAPB Report Content Guides
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES AND DESCRIPTION
3.    Pro-ect Objectives. The project's primary objectives would be to:  (a)
introduce water resources planning by river basins across all uses of water;
(b) improve agricultural productivity through modernization and completion of
irrigation systems, upgraded water management and farmer participation; (c)
assure sustainability of water infrastructure and the environment; and (d)
improve institutional and technical capability for managing the state's water
resources.
4.    Proiect Description.  The project would be a sector investment loan
implemented over seven years and financing an agreed state-wide program to
improve the productivity and sustainability of Tamil Nadu's irrigation sector,
to introduce multi-sectoral water planning, to integrate farmers in irrigation
management, and to strengthen the state's institutional and technical capacity
in water development, management and planning. Project components are: (a)
System Improvement and Farmer Turnover; (b) Scheme Crim1ttiJona; (c) Water
Plannina. Environmental Manaaement and Research; {d) In;titutional UDaradina;
and (e) land acauisition and economic rehabilitation.



- 216 -
Annex 13
Page 2 of 9
5.    The investment program would be supported by a number of policy and
institutional reforms including: actions consistent with the objectives under
the recently formulated "Tamil Nadu State Water Policy"; the creation and
strengthening of the state "Water Resources Organization"; implementation of
an Environmental Action Plan; prioritization of state expenditures in the
irrigation sector emphasizing maintenance and institutional strengthening as
first priority, followed by the system improvement and farmer turnover
program, followed by viable scheme completions, and last (and only as needed
within Tamil Nadu's state water planning requirements, particularly for
metropolitan needs), viable new investment; full programming and funding of
maintenance; measures to rationalize water charges, review cost recovery
annually and hand-over O&M responsibilities and funding of distributaries to
established farmer councils; measures to introduce groundwater legislation and
regulatory actions for groundwater pumping; and establishment of an
institutional apparatus, improved procedures and implementation of individual
land acquisition and economic rehabilitation action plans for persons affected
under the project (Refer chapter II of main report and relevant Annexes).
B. IMPLEMENTATION PLAN
6.    Project Management and Implementation.  The project would be implemented
by Tamil Nadu's Water Resources Organization (WRO). Project management would
be under the responsibility of the Engineer-in-Chief, WRO, under the overall
guidance of the Secretary, WRO (PWD). The EIC would have overall
responsibility for implementation and coordination of all activities,
supported by CEs responsible for their respective management units: CE Plan
Formulation, CE Design, Research and Construction, CE O&M, CE State Water
Resources and Data Center, CE IWS and CE IMTI, plus, for field management, the
four CE Basin Managers. The EIC's office would be supported by a joint CE,
units for Administration, Program and Budgeting, Monitoring and Evaluation,
and Project Officers for special assignments. WRO would also contain a
Regulatory Directorate and a Land Acquisition and Economic Rehabilitation
Office. Environmental cells would be in IWS and with the CE Plan Formulation,
plus smaller environmental units at basin manager level. The institutional
structure. orcaaniarams and position descriptions are at Annex 2.
Implementation responsibilities are at Attachment 2 of this Annex.
7.    For water planning (refer Annex 3) the Water Resources Control and
Review Council (WRCRC), chaired by the Chief Minister and with Ministers PWD
(WRO), Municipal Administration and Rural Development as Vice Chairmen, and
including representation from all Government Departments associated with water
use and quality, would be the highest state body for coordinating basin and
state water planning and allocation across sectors. The IWS would be the
Secretariat of the WRCRC and Nodal Agency for state water planning. For
coordination of the Environmental Action Plan (refer Annex 7), the WRCRC would
contain an Environmental Sub-Committee. Specific management and implementation
details for the System Imnrovement and Farmer Turnover proaram are at Annex 4.
The institutional and management arrangements for the Land Acauisition and
Economic Rehabilitation program are at Annex 9. Management arrangements for
the Water Resources Research Fund are at Annex 8.



- 217 -
Annex 13
Page 3 of 9
8.    Training and Technical Assistance.  The extensive reorganization and
institutional strengthening to be undertaken under the project, and the major
initiatives in the areas of water planning, farmer participation and turnover,
systems improvement, environmental management and business skills such as
management information systems and programming and budgeting, would require
substantial training and technical assistance throughout the WRO. Training
and consultancy needs have been prepared by WRO and the Bank for each
management unit and are detailed in their respective cost tables at Annex 11.
Each CE would be responsible for managing the specific training and
consultancy relevant to his management unit, but would receive administrative
support from a Training and Technical Assistance Officer in the EIC's office.
Overall coordination and identification of training opportunities would be
provided by the CE, IMTI. The EIC would provide general guidance and balance
needs between WRO units. Training would focus on strengthening skills in all
engineering areas including operations and maintenance, water planning,
environmental management, design and construction, and supporting skill
development associated with technologies or management techniques relatively
new to WRO such as financial management skills, programming and budgeting,
computerization, computer aided design, remote sensing and management
information systems. For consultancies, in order that know-how is transferred
to WRO staff, consultant assistance would be linked in all cases to staff
training through association of staff with consultant assignments and
interlinked formal training as practicable. National and international study
tours, training courses and temporary assignments with experienced water
agencies would also be used to provide first-hand exposure to new technologies
and management techniques.
9.    Consultancy arrangements would include small consultancies with
individuals or firms as needed, but would be backstopped throughout the
project period with one main consultancy contract comprising a "twinning
arrangement" with an experienced consultancy group. All consultancy selection
would follow Bank guidelines. The twinning arrangement would be with a
consortium of several firms or specialist institutions, likely including one
international firm and one domestic firm, plus other firms/institutes as may
be considered appropriate in the offers by consortia and as may be accepted in
final selection of the most qualified offer. The central skill areas for an
acceptable consortium, seldom found in one firm or agency, would comprise all
aspects of irrigation engineering, water planning and modelling, environmental
management, and hands-on irrigation management, particularly in O&M, general
and corporate management techniques, and beneficiary participation, plus
capacity to provide or arrange training in all of the above areas. One of the
firms or agencies of the consortium would be the lead partner and have overall
responsibility for mobilizing the consultancy and training support required.
The contract would provide a basic expertise and training input annually,
including some core senior staff providing continuity of visits, and access to
other specializations and training on an "as needed" basis, with flexibility
retained and provided for in the consultancy budget of the contract.
Selection would be through an initial invitation and subsequent
prequalification of interested firms (in their prequalification offers the
firms would advise of any intended partners), followed by short listing by WRO



- 218 -
Annex 13
Page 4 of 9
of about 4 (range 3 to 5) firms or groups of firms for submission of detailed
offers. A contract over the full project period would be agreed at the outset,
with flexibility for annual variations based on an annual review of
consultancy and related training needs between WRO and the consortium in
consultation with GOTN's Department of Finance and IDA. The above procedures
would provide the expertise required while limiting constraints frequently
found in India of obtaining individual clearances, especially for consultants
and training requiring foreign exchange. Consultancy needs are contained in
the detailed project cost tables by project component and WRO management units
at Annex 11.
10.   Additional to the above, WRO would: (i) contract a senior institutional
advisor, institutional advisors and community organizers (NGOs) for the farmer
participation and turnover program (already under implementation) (Annex 4);
(ii) continue to hire the services of a national firm with expertise in land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation for assisting the LAERO with the land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation program over at least the first three
years of the project; (iii) contract NGOs for implementation of the LAERAPs
and administration of the associated RGF and AMG asset creation funds (Annex
9); (iv) contract a national fisheries specialist to advise on the fisheries
component of the LAER program (Annex 9); contract a supervision consultancy
contract to assist WRO and GOTN's "Supervision Committee" (para 17); (v) under
advice of the Research Advisory Committee, contract Tamil Nadu, other Indian
and overseas research institutes for the research and technology transfer
program under the project's Water Resources Research Fund (Annex 8), and (vi)
contract for other needs as may be determined by WRO and IDA during project
implementation.
11.   Staff Continuity and Specialization. WRO will ensure organizational and
functional effectiveness by establishing various categories of specialists.
These specialists will represent the broad range of responsibilities of WRO
from design to maintenance to programming and budget. Specialists will be
selected and provided additional training, either by IMTI or by outside
training institutes or consultants. Study tours will also be arranged where
appropriate to further enhance the expertise and awareness of technologies and
management skills of the specialists. Consistent with the expenditure of time
and funding to provide specialized training, posting policies will be modified
to take advantage of this additional training by retaining these employees in
their specialized disciplines. In many cases, transfers can still be
accommodated but should be within the same discipline. In other cases the
specialization is so specific and limited that few if more than one location
will be able to utilize that speciality. For example, an earth dam designer
can only be usefully posted with the Chief Engineer, Design, Research and
Construction. In such instances the posting policy will allow an extended
stay in the same location, with due consideration to the preferences of the
employee. Promotions would actively favor employees staying in a post for more
than three years and employees staying in a particular speciality.
12.   Implementation Schedule.   A Summary Implementation Schedule for the
project is at Attachment 1. Details by project component are in their
respective annexes.



- 219 -
Annex 13
Page 5 of 9
13.  Costs. Financing and Disbursements.  Detailed oroject costs by component
and year are at Annex 11 and summarized in Chapter III of the main report. The
financing olan is at para 3.04. Disbursement arrangements are described at
para 3.13 and a Disbursement Schedule is at Annex 12. Arrangements for
retroactive financing, the special account and accounting and auditing are at
paras 3.15 and 3.16.
14.   Procurement Implementation and Schedule. Procurement arrangements are
described at paras 3.05 to 3.12 of the main report. They include special
management features (para 3.11) to ensure efficient and timely procurement.
Detailed procurement schedules with contract packages, timing and estimated
costs have been prepared for all field sites/components. An example of such a
schedule is at Attachment 5 of this Annex.
15.   Economic Analysis.  The detailed economic analysis for the project is at
Annex 14 and summarized with risk assessments and description of non-
quantifiable benefits in chapter 5 of the main report. The overall economic
rate of return for the project is estimated at 17%. Sensitivity tests and
risks analysis are in Annex 14.
C. MANAGEMENT AND SUPERVISION PLAN
16.   Management and Supervision Tools.  A project of this nature requires
close management, monitoring and supervision for its success. Sustainable
success depends on creating a management and monitoring structure and
capability integral to the entirety of WRO's operations and continuous after
the duration of the WRCP. For this reason, management information systems
(MIS), monitoring and evaluation (M&E) capability, programming and budgeting
(P&B), use of development indicators including the results of a baseline
survey, and a system of quarterly reports and a closely followed Annual
Review, Action Plan and Budget, supplemented by special reports on certain
components and a mid-term review, have been integrated in project management.
These will form the basic tools for management, supervision and actions to
redress problem areas and exploit evolving opportunities for faster or
modified implementation. They will in themselves significantly raise the
management capabilities of WRO, which were constrained within the former PWD
due to lack of basic management tools. PWD did not have, or had only in very
limited form, capabilities in MIS, M&E and P&B. As important in terms of
limiting the facility of management, PWD's organizational structure was overly
centralized with inadequate delegation of authority. Further, management
units were only partially organized around functional specializations limiting
staff specialization which was further exacerbated by frequent staff
transfers. The new organization (Annex 2) around functional specialties,
divested of non-water related activities, and with decentralized control
nearer to field sites, will - once fully established - greatly improve the
task of management. It will enable WRO's evolution, associated with intensive
training and consultancy assistance, to a modern water agency. The management
tools above are described in the paragraphs below and relevant sections of the
main report and its annexes.



- 220 -
Annex 13
Page 6 of 9
17.   GOTN and GOI Supervision. In view of the institutional and technical
challenges to be undertaken under the project, GOTN will implement special
measures to provide additional supervision support from government. A WRCP
Supervision Committee has been formed by GOTN chaired by the Secretary WRO
with the Secretary, Finance Department as Vice Chairman and EIC, WRO as
Secretary and including the Member (Irrigation) of the state Planning
Commission, Land Development Commissioner, plus two experienced invited
members (former WRO, other government department or business community/NGO
with proven management ability). In central government, MOWR, DEA, Advisor
(Irrigation) Planning Commission (and other ministries such as environment
where issues addressed are relevant) will also be copied minutes and have
standing invitations to attend as possible. As relevant for particular
issues, other GOITN representatives will be invited to participate (eg
Agriculture Department, AED, Environment, District Collectors). Meetings will
be at least quarterly to discuss the quarterly reports and the ARAPB, and more
often as needed. Additionally, a Supervision Consultancy will be arranged and
funded under the WRCP with a national consultancy firm. This would provide
backstopping consultancy assistance to the WRCP Supervision Committee and site
visits. The consultants would make two visits per year, timed to overlap with
IDA supervision missions, and additional assistance as needed. Consultant
reports would be made to the committee and copied to IDA and GOI.
18.   IDA Supervision. An indicative IDA Supervision Schedule is at Attachment
3, and would be adjusted as needed during project implementation. The overall
plan comprises in a typical year two formal visits, supplemented by
correspondence, clearances and visits of WRO staff to the Delhi office as
needed. One of the visits would be short and primarily of an expediting
nature: checking progress, helping resolve interim constraints, dealing with
consultancy, procurement, disbursement and accounting matters arising. One
main mission a year would be timed to coincide with the review of the draft
ARAPB in the December/January period and would make selective visits to the
field, go through all aspects of the project and help determine the
forthcoming year's program and review the program budget. GOTN's
"Supervision" consultants (para 17) would, inter alia, include in their
program missions of their consultants at the same time as both the IDA visits.
Visits of the core staff of the main consultancy contract would also be timed
where possible to overlap with the IDA missions, and as needed, similar
arrangements would be made for specialists, especially where decisions are
required on particular aspects of the project. Special meetings of GOTN's
"supervision Committee" (para 17) would be convened by GOTN at the time of the
IDA visits for discussion with IDA staff. These arrangements would enable
harnessing of the required specializations to enable effective multi-
disciplinary supervision and close interaction between WRO and its
consultants, GOTN and IDA, while keeping IDA resource requirements within
reasonable dimensions. The IDA Supervision Plan at Attachment 3 also allows
for: (i) more intensive supervision at project start-up, including in
procurement and disbursement, institutional reorganization, for the systems
improvement and farmer turnover program, for the LAER program and for basin
planning; (ii) the interim review of the farmer turnover program in PY2; and
(iii) the mid-term review in PY 3.



- 221 -
Annex 13
Page 7 of 9
19.   Monitoring. Evaluation. Programming and BudQeting.  With the emphasis of
WRO on improving overall management of water resources in Tamil Nadu, there is
a need to consolidate and report the activities of WRO in a format which will
assist the EIC and the Government in assessing program progress and
performance, and for programming and budgeting to maximize effectiveness.
There will be two staff units, reporting directly to the EIC: the Monitoring
and Evaluation Unit, and the Program Budgeting and Accounting Unit.
Consultancy assistance will be used to guide the establishment and initial
operation of both units, and as needed thereafter.
20.   Monitoring and Evaluation.  The new Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) Unit
is an independent entity which will: (i) monitor expenditures on programs
relative to total estimated costs; (ii) conduct post-project evaluations to
compare benefits with original planning estimates; and (iii) perform
evaluations of organizational effectiveness and performance of units within
WRO for compliance with the goals and objectives of WRO. The M&E Unit would
utilize the program budgeting process for much of its data to evaluate
progress of projects and programs. At the request of any organizational unit
or as directed by the EIC, the M&E unit would collect data, conduct site
visits, initiate discussions with water users and the public, and evaluate
reports to determine any deficiencies or problems and provide a report to the
requestor with recommendations for action.
21.   The Monitoring and Evaluation unit will also monitor and evaluate
development effects on crop yields, total crop production, crop
diversification, farm income levels, water distribution efficiencies and
project affected persons. The current situation will be assessed based on
personal visits, discussions with water users, sample surveys, questionnaires,
and review of official publications. The evaluation will determine these
effects by comparing the situation of a given time with the Baseline Survey
(Annex 10). Annual reports will be prepared for dissemination to the State,
Government of India, the World Bank, universities and others. Also, the unit
will assess the effectiveness of the Farmer Organization and turnover Program
and its impact on water delivery efficiencies and other factors. The unit
will utilize the services of consultants or local or other universities for
special surveys and analyses, as appropriate. The development effects could
be used in the future to assist the Government in setting agricultural
production targets by matching crop water demands with available water
supplies by region or command.
22.   Program. Budgeting and Accounting.  WRO's new Program Budgeting and
Accounting Unit (PBA) will establish, maintain, and administer the program
budget and accounting system. The system will be used to develop annual
budgets by consolidating budget requests from all WRO organizational elements
and ensure that the final budget request is consistent with the policies and
instructions from the Government. The system will provide the mechanism for
tracking program progress and to assist the EIC in making recommendations on
transfer of funds from one program to another. the unit will provide periodic
management reports for use by the EIC, CEs and other managers for decision-
making purposes. In addition and to support all activities in WRO, the unit
will develop and maintain a Management Information System (MIS).



- 222 -
Annex 13
Page 8 of 9
23.   Development Indicators.  Development Indicators (Attachment 4)
corresponding to the project objectives at para 3 have been chosen to help
track the project's impact on the project objectives. Attachment 4 provides a
matrix of monitorable indicators against each project objective and comments
on how such indicators may be monitored. Indicators include physical
indicators for the field components and qualitative and progress indicators
for other components. The indicators include: implementation of the
institutional restructuring and strengthening and its effectiveness,
agricultural growth, productivity and crop diversification, increased delivery
of water and improved services, implementation and effectiveness of the farmer
participation and turnover program, and improved planning, programming and
budgeting.
24.   Baseline Survey.  To provide a basis for monitoring the development
impact of the project, and further refining the development indicators above,
a baseline survey to be conducted by consultants would be carried out in 1995
(TOR for the Baseline Survey is at Annex 10). The survey would comprise an
overall survey supplemented by more detailed surveys at the level of sample
commands, and remote sensing for assessment of effective irrigation coverage
and cropping patterns, and would cover the following aspects: engineering
indicators for operational and maintenance performance; agricultural
production indicators; performance indicators for the institutional
restructuring and strengthening; and indicators for the farmer participation
and turnover program.
25.   Ouarterly Revorts.  The quarterly reports would be issued within two
weeks of the end of each quarter and would be issued by the Programming and
Budget unit after clearance from the EIC. Each quarterly report would contain
a listing of standard tracking data on physical progress, financing,
procurement and disbursement, a sub-set of relevant qualitative data from the
baseline survey determined parameters, brief submissions from each
implementation unit in WRO which would quantify and compare progress with the
SAR and the previous ARAPB and highlight problems, constraints and recommended
solutions, and an overview section from the EIC's office summarizing decisions
or recommendations based on the overview and each management unit's
submission. This would include commentary on status of decisions/actions
outstanding from the last quarterly report and review of any decisions/actions
still required related to recommendations of the GOTN Supervision Committee,
GOI or IDA. The reports would be presented to the Secretary WRO, and copied
to the Minister, Supervision Committee and relevant state officials, GOI and
IDA. Proforma guidance for the report is in the project file, with a single
component example at Attachment 6. The Supervision Committee would meet to
discuss and act/advise after each quarterly report, and IDA would provide
advice by written or mission communication.
26.   Annual Review. Action Plan and Budaet (ARAPB).  The ARAPB would be the
main vehicle for annually taking stock of project performance and future
needs, for adjusting WRO's program as needed, for budgeting by GOTN of WRO's
Plan and Non-Plan expenditures, and for annual major review and supervision by
GOTN, GOI and IDA. For WRO it would provide annual opportunity to assess its



- 223 -
Annex 13
Page 9 of 9
program, to propose adjustments as needed, and to make its consolidated plan
and justification for financing needs for the forthcoming year.  The ARAPB
would review for each _omponent and responsible unit the past year's
achievements in terms of development indicators, physical targets and
expenditures, and propose a justified work program and yroposed expenditure
budaet for the forthcoming fiscal year. It would also include: (i)
description of each year's works, schedule of activities, staffing
requirements and distribution of responsibilities; (ii) proposed next year
expenditures compared witlh previous full year's expenditures and budgeted and
expected expenditures in current year; (iii) details on major civil works
contracts exceeding US$ 200,000 and for ICB for goods; and (iv) financing plan
and sources of funds. Special sectioons would cover: (v) review of
implementation progress related to the institutional strengthening and policy
reforms under the project; (vi) detailed review and proposal of maintenance
needs (command-by-command) and corresponding non-Plan expenditure
requirements; (vii) the farmer participation and turnover program; and (viii)
progress of state water planning under WRCRC and IWS.
27.   Interim Review of Farmer Organization and Turnover Program.  Due to the
importance and innovatory nature of the Systems Improvement and Farmer
Turnover program, in particular the farmer participation and turnover aspects,
a Special Evaluation would he carried oUt of this program in PY2 (FY 1996/97)
by consultants with expertise in this area. This would enable feedback and
recommendations sufficiently early in project implementation to enable timely
adjustments.
28.   Mid-Term Review.  By December 31, 1997 (mid "PY3"), GOTN/WRO would carry
out, jointly with IDA and (G,OT, a Mid-Term Review of project progress and
possible need for adjustments to components, implementation arrangements and
policy reforms, and thereafter implement its recommendations.
29.   Implementation CompLetion Reoort   An Implementation Completion Report
would be prepared by GOTN orn the basis of IDA's guidelines, not later than six
months from the closing date of the Credit.

TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLTDATION PRO.TECT
Economic Analysis
1.    OveXview:  The Tamil Nadu Water Resources Consolidation Project would
assist Tamil Nadu State to significantly refocus its program in the water and
irrigation sector. The State's water resources agency would be modernized, and
complemented with supporting policy and institutional mechanisms to plan and
manage water from a multi-sectoral and environmentally-responsive perspective.
The project would introduce mechanisms for private sector participation in water
management activities, including the turnover of irrigation facility operations
and maintenance to farmer organizations. Irrigation sector expenditure
priorities would be revised to ensure first priority to full funding of
maintenance activities, second priority to rehabilitation and modernization of
existing irrigation schemes, and third priority to completion of existing
schemes. In addition, the project introduces to the water resources agency the
use of rigorous criteria for future project selection. These actions together
would serve to improve the productivity and sustainability of irrigation services
and ensure the timely provision of water to all user.
2.    Besides the irrigation benefits, the project would also generate long-term
benefits which are more difficult to quantify. Transforming the irrigation
branch of the Public Works Department (PWD) from a construction-oriented agency
into a specialized Water Resource Organization (WRO) would improve the management
of the State's water resources to include a multi-sector and environmentally-
sustainable perspective. Groundwater protection would be enhanced by improving
groundwater monitoring, regulations and enforcement. Allocation of water
resources between agricultural and non-agricultural users would be improved
through more holistic water planning. Bulk water supplies for industrial and
domestic users would be assured by multi-use basin and state water planning
wherein they would enjoy high priority. Revision of irrigation sector
expenditure priorities, in addition to improved project selection criteria, would
yield efficiency gains for the State, allowing it to avoid costly and unnecessary
projects and to invest in the most effective projects upfront. Lack of
quantitative data prevents the explicit incorporation of these benefits in the
analysis, but their significance is large.
Analysis and Methodologfy
3.    The analysis concentrates on quantifying the impact of project activities
on irrigation and agricultural production. The non-quantifiable benefits will be
described briefly though they will not figure into the determination of project
viability. In concert with the project's structure which is comprised of two
main components, the analysis is divided into two parts: first, the economic
analysis for the scheme completions, then a separate analysis for the
modernization and rehabilitation component of the project. This division is
chosen because it provides better opportunities for monitoring project progress
after implementation.
4.    Water is a scarce good in Tamil Nadu.  Domestic and industrial users
compete with agriculture for water resources and demand will continue to
increase. Project activities would increase the surface water supply
availability for all sectors. Currently more than 90% of the surface water



- 240 -
Annex 14
Page 2 of 11
supplies are used in the agriculture sector, so most of the increases in surface
water supplies through project activities are likely to be added to irrigation
and rural water supply. The economic benefits of increasing surface water
supplies through project activities could theoretically be calculated by using
bulk water prices for industrial, domestic, or agricultural use. The current
water rates in Tamil Nadu (industry Rs. 500/'000m3 , domestic Rs. 200/'000m3,
agriculture Rs. 14-26/'000m3) belong to the highest in India.  However, these
prices are more an indication of political will than actual efficiency prices for
bulk water and can therefore not be used for the analysis. To cautiously value
the benefits of the project, the increase in surface water supplies is evaluated
in terms of the marginal benefits of water supplied to irrigation which is
generally the least attractive alternative.
5.    Economic import and export parity prices are calculated based on the World
Bank Revision of Primary Commodity Price Forecasts, July 1994 (Table 1) using
year 2000 projections expressed in constant March 1994 prices. Export parity
prices are used for cotton and sugarcane, import parity prices calculated for
paddy and for fertilizer. Rice is treated as an import substitute as Indian
demand and supply analysis for rice indicates the need for substantial expansion
of domestic production to avoid imports. Although India is essentially
self-sufficient in rice production, there will be a need to increase paddy
productivity in order to keep pace with population growth and positive income
effects due to economic growth. Sugar and cotton are valued at export parity
prices. Groundnuts and coconuts are treated as non-tradeable since they are
consumed as fresh products, and do not compare with internationally traded copra
or vegetable oil commodities. The project's incremental production of these
crops is fairly small and would be absorbable in the local fresh market. The
financial prices, adjusted with a standard conversion factor (SCF) of 0.9, are
comparable to export parity prices for copra and slightly higher for groundnut
oil. All non-internationally-traded products have been adjusted with the
standard conversion factor (SCF) for India of 0.9. The economic prices for
seeds, labor, draught power, and machinery are based on financial prices, net of
taxes, adjusted by the SCF.
Scheme Completions
6.    This component involves the construction works required to complete
irrigation schemes which are currently being undertaken. From an earlier list of
twenty candidate sub-projects, thirteen candidates were selected for detailed
analysis during project preparation on the basis of preliminary screening,
including assessment of water availability within Tamil Nadu's overall water
planning and development objectives, and assessment of likely economic viability.
Of the thirteen schemes, five have construction activities at a fairly advanced
stage. Construction on the remaining eight candidates is either in the early
stages or proposed to start, with land acquisition and tender procedures under
preparation. On the basis of the analysis below, nine of the thirteen candidates
have been selected for inclusion in the WRO's work program over the WRCP period.
7.    While worldwide and Indian experience suggests that new irrigation schemes
typically have low economic rates of return, higher rates of return can generally
be expected from investments to complete the substantial backlog of on-going
irrigation projects. However, ERRs above the opportunity cost of capital, even
for scheme completions, are by no means automatic and the selection of candidates
for finance has to be done rigorously based on economic, technical and social
criteria.



- 241 -
Annex 14
Page 3 of 11
8.    Benefits  The Scheme Completions component has the direct objective of
increasing the quantity of, and improving the reliability of water supplies for
agricultural and domestic purposes. The direct benefits which would accrue as a
result of the investments are as follows:
1. Increases in irrigated command areas;
2. Improved yields resulting purely from reduction in moisture stress;
3. Improved yields resulting from optimum input practices adopted by
farmers in the light of improved reliability of water supply;
4. Agricultural intensification and changes in cropping patterns to higher
value crops; and
5. Provision of domestic water supplies.
9.    For each of the thirteen scheme completions appraised, the direct
agricultural benefits have been quantified in detail and form the basis of the
economic evaluation of the sub-component. However, secondary economic benefits
(and costs) which could be available, but have not been valued and included in
the analysis, would include:
1. Future options for bulk water supply to municipal and industrial users;
2. The net value of any fish production resulting from building
reservoirs;
3. Improved drinking water supplies caused through groundwater recharge;
4. Any flood control due to the construction of civil works;
5. The byproduct values associated with the various crops to be grown;
and
6. Social forestry schemes, and thus fuelwood output, planted in new
tanks.
10.   Crop budgets in economic terms have been derived from the financial crop
budget and are set out in Table 2. These data were compiled from joint WRO and
FAO/CP field visits to each scheme area. The value of the electricity consumed
in supplementary irrigation from wells is extremely difficult to quantify because
disaggregated records of power consumption are not available, and the use of well
irrigation can vary significantly dependent on seasonal climatic conditions.
Supplementary well irrigation would be reduced as a result of the project because
groundwater and associated power consumption would be partly substituted by the
increased and more reliable surface water supplies. For the purposes of the
analysis, however, it is assumed that power consumption would not change with the
project.
11.   The investment costs in the economic analysis are derived from the detailed
cost tables, adjusted to economic costs, and include physical contingencies.
Annual operation and maintenance (O&M) costs have been estimated at Rs. 298/ha
subsequently adjusted by the SCF, and applied from 1 April, 1994 onwards. This is
based on a detailed estimate of requirement for full maintenance provided by WRO
and reviewed during appraisal. These costs include current costs of staff.



- 242 -
Annex 14
Page 4 of 11
12.   Methodology  For each of the thirteen scheme completions, the economic rate
of return was calculated to ascertain the economic viability of the scheme, and
assess the merits for its financing. The economic rate of return calculation
(ERR) is based on the following assumptions: (i) all costs are in constant March
1994 prices; (ii) project benefits and costs are calculated for 30 years; (iii)
quantifiable net returns are from incremental crop production; (iv) O&M
expenditures would remain constant over 30 years; and (v) the opportunity cost
of capital is 12%. Thus, schemes are automatically included for financing if the
ERR is at least 12%.
13.   In order to estimate the incremental benefit and cost streams for each
scheme, the irrigated areas were divided into areas defined by the source of
irrigation water, which is set out graphically in Table 3. The future situation
without the project is assumed to approximate the present situation without the
project. For ongoing schemes, it has been assumed that all costs incurred before
1 April, 1994, will be treated as sunk.
14.   In the base case scenario, scheme completions have been costed using
standard Government construction rates, increased by 50%. The analysis also
presents results using Government rates increased by 25%, and Government rates
only. The use of Government rates plus 50% is based on analysis of actual past
and present implementation experience, compared with unit rates quoted by the
Government. This base case has been adopted for both the ERR analysis and
project costing, given the importance of the economic success of each completion
scheme in the overall program and adequate funding based on implementation
experience. The costs further include physical contingencies of 10% for all
civil works and vehicles, and 15% for all equipment. The approach taken during
appraisal was to incorporate sufficient safeguards in the analysis and selection
process of completion schemes to ensure as far as possible the economic viability
of each scheme.
15. Results. The table below gives a summary of the economic rates of return
and benefit/cost ratios for the thirteen candidate completion schemes, including
sensitivity analysis for cost overruns. For details refer to Table 4. The
results indicate that eight of the scheme completions, Adavinainarkoil,
Gridhammal II, Kodumudiyar, Mordhana, Nambiyar, Poigayar, Rajathopekanar, and
Vadakku Pachayar, are acceptable investment projects in economic terms since they
would be expected to provide economic rates of return at or in excess of the
opportunity cost of capital (OCC) for India of 12% p.a. Four schemes (shown at
bottom of the table) are not viable and have been excluded from the project.
16.   One scheme, Sothuparai, has an ERR of 11.2%, and under normal circumstances
would not be considered for financing. A calculation of the switching value for
benefits, however, indicates that benefits for Sothuparai would have to increase
by only 6.5% for the ERR to be equal to 12%. This is equivalent to an additional
NPV of Rs. 16 million (US$0.5 million), or Rs. 2 million (US$60,000) per year.
The NPV of additional benefits amounts to an average of Rs. 9900/ha (US$300/ha),
or Rs. 1200/ha per year (US$37/ha per year). These additional benefits would
likely be achieved through the secondary benefits that would accrue as a result
of the project, such as byproduct values from additional crop production (e.g.
increased straw biomass for fodder), health benefits from improved drinking
water, and new fisheries production. For example, it is estimated that net
income from fisheries alone would be Rs. 14,000 (US$440) per fishing household
per year (Annex 9, Attachment 5), which amounts to an estimated Rs. 1 million



- 243 -
Annex 14
Page 5 of 11
(US$31,000) per year for the Sothuparai scheme sitel. On this basis, the
Sothuparai scheme is considered an acceptable investment, and is included in the
project package.

1/ Base case: Economic parameters as at October 1994 - updated SCF for India (May 1994),
updated commodity prices (July 1994);  Project costs based on Government rates plus 50%,
plus physical contingencies.
2/ Case 2: Project costs based on Government rates plus 25%, plus physical contingencies.
3/ Case 3: Project costs based on Government rates only, plus physical contingencies.
4/ The benefit/cost ratio is included here since it has been in the past the basis on which
WRO (formerly the Public Works Department) evaluated its projects.  Projects were
considered acceptable by PWD if the ratio was greater than 1.5 (or 1 if the project
was located in drought-prone areas). It is calculated as a ratio of annual benefits to
annualized investment plus operating costs using base case cost assumptions: i.e.
(Net benefit at full capacity)/((10% of total investment costs) + (2% depreciation) + O&M).
17.   The analysis shows the benefits of careful control of construction costs.
Of the nine schemes above, six (Adavinainarkoil, Nambiyar, Poigayar,
Rajathopekanar, Sothuparai and Vadakku Pachayar) have economic rates of return at
or near the 12% level. The economic rate of return of each of these six schemes
would move to the 15-16% range if construction costs equal to Government rates
plus 25% were achieved (case 2). In order to avoid realization of low economic
rates of return, the project's procurement and contractor supervision and
monitoring arrangements are specifically designed to achieve maximum economies in
construction costs.
18.   As indicated above, four of the schemes reviewed, Shanmughanadhi, Periyar
Vaigai Tirrupattur, Periyar Vaigai Maravangamalam and Irrukangudi, are not
acceptable in economic terms based on present design, cost, water availability
and resulting cropping pattern. Each of these four would provide economic
returns significantly less than the required 12% OCC. Therefore, only the nine
schemes mentioned previously will be included in WRO's program for support by the
project. The nine scheme completions together have an overall economic rate of
return of 15% and a net present value of Rs. 338 million. The major benefits are
an incremental production of 4,700 t of paddy, 5,500 t of groundnut and an
additional 76,000 t of sugarcane (Table 5).
19.   The State's program in the water sector, which is financed under the WRCP,
will include detailed water planning for future water needs. When the basin and
state water plans are developed, it is likely that new projects would be

identified by WRO in the course of the next years, in particular in relation to
estimated urban and industrial bulk water needs. To enable the WRCP to
accommodate the inclusion of further completion schemes under the overall
investment program of WRO, a small provision (at 10% of base costs of the nine
accepted scheme completions) would be made in the project costs for the
construction and completion of presently unidentified schemes. The inclusion of
any further projects would be dependent on completing and satisfying a set of
rigorous criteria as outlined in Annex 15, including the criterion of an ERR of
12% or more.
Systems Imorovement and Farmer Turnover (Modernization and Rehabilitation)
20.   Background:  The productivity of irrigation in Tamil Nadu is well below
potential. On the majority of irrigation schemes, suboptimal distribution of
water results in low yields and cropping intensity and reduced opportunities for
diversifying agriculture. The existing infrastructure of the conveyance system
has been deteriorating which further aggravates the suboptimal irrigation
efficiency. The project's modernization, rehabilitation and farmer turnover
activities are aimed at improving the productivity and efficiency of the
irrigation system. Modernization and rehabilitation include activities like
desilting of and repairs to the canals, repairs to structures, selected lining of
canals and distributaries, on-farm development works, and improved operations
management of the reservoirs and canals. Integral to the rehabilitation and
modernization program will be a major thrust to hand over the responsibility of
operation and maintenance below the distributary level to Water Users
Associations.
21.   Investments of this nature are generally considered to have high potential
returns both in India and in other countries, and available analysis from a
variety of sources confirms this (India, Irrigation Sector Review, Report No.
9518-IN). In Tamil Nadu, investments in modernization and rehabilitation are
currently being financed under the National Water Management Project (NWMP) which
commenced in the late eighties in seven of the State's schemes. First monitoring
reports allow to tentatively assess the benefits in selected schemes. Although
the monitoring process has not been completed because in most schemes the work is
still ongoing, for two completed schemes, Sathanur and Cumbum Valley, WRO has
reported impressive increases in both expansion of effectively irrigated area and
in yield (increases in irrigated area of around 100%, and yield increases in the
order of 13 - 80% for paddy and groundnut). Most of the other schemes show a
positive development in expansion of irrigated area (Table 6).
22.   In total more than 660,000 ha of cultural command area (CCA) would be
rehabilitated and modernized under WRCP. This includes 17 major and medium (more
than 2,000 ha per scheme) and 40 minor schemes. The major schemes are further
divided into stage 1 and stage 2 works. Stage 1 includes the most immediate
repairs to the major canal system and is limited to expenditures averaging Rs.
5,300 per hectare. Investments falling under stage 2 include lining of
distributaries and on farm development works. Stage 2 investments are limited to
expenditures of not more than Rs. 10,000 (avg. Rs. 7,900) per hectare.
23.   Benefits:  The major benefits to be expected for agriculture are:  (i)
increases in cultivable area receiving full and more reliable water supplies
which allow farmers to increase production intensity, (ii) improved yields
resulting from reducing moisture stress and by using higher inputs, and (iii)
intensification and diversification of the cropping patterns to higher value
crops. Benefits to the non-agricultural sectors comprise more reliable bulk



- 245 -
Annex 14
Page 7 of 11
water supplies to domestic and industrial users. Water saved by increasing the
efficiency of irrigated agricultural production could also be passed on to the
other sectors in the future. The economic analysis nevertheless evaluates
increases in surface water supplies in terms of the marginal benefits of water
supplied to irrigation. Benefits will thus tend to be under-estimated, as
agricultural water usage usually has lower returns than for industry.
24.   In nearly all schemes, the actually irrigated Cultural Command Area (CCA)
is considerably smaller than the originally designed CCA. Moreover, not all of
the actually irrigated CCA receive sufficient irrigation supplies to fully
irrigate the crops according to crop water requirements. To give a realistic
assessment of the situation in Tamil Nadu in the economic analysis, irrigation
benefits will be divided into benefits resulting from increasing:
i)    The irrigated area at the tail-end, enabling farmers to switch from
rainfed to irrigated crops (reducing the gap); and
ii)   Water deliveries to partially irrigated land, enabling the farmers to
derive incremental irrigated production (stabilization);
25.   Methodology:  A detailed economic analysis has been prepared for 16 of the
17 major schemes covering a designed CCA of 530,000 ha or 80% of the total area
under consideration. The analysis follows a disaggregated approach looking at
the agricultural cropping patterns, crop budgets, input use and production for
each of the 16 major schemes separately. For three of the schemes, information
on the existing agricultural structure has been prepared in detail jointly by WRO
and FAO/CP. These three were selected as being representative of the three major
types of irrigation water supply - canal only, tank only and canal/tank systems.
The other 13 major schemes have been analyzed using crop budgets provided by the
Tamil Nadu Agricultural University (TNAU). The results of the economic analysis
are extrapolated to a medium scheme and the minor schemes which have not been
separately analyzed because they are scattered all over the State.
26.   A detailed analysis of the benefits of the three selected model schemes
(Kodayar, Vaigai Old Ayacut, Lower Bhavani) was carried out by identifying
presently unirrigated areas, or areas of unreliable irrigation, that would be
made reliable irrigation areas as a result of the modernization/rehabilitation
works. Details of the cropping pattern and crop budgets were obtained during
joint WRO - FAO/CP field visits to each site (Table 7). The analysis of the
model schemes serves to confirm the economic viability, based on data from WRO
and TNAU, of the other thirteen modernization schemes.
27.   The TNAU crop budgets (Table 8) summarize information on inputs, outputs
and prices and are based on an annual monitoring procedure of farm performance in
all districts of the State based on a stratified sampling procedure. This farm
management data base is the only statewide statistical source using actual
performance information for the 1992/93 farming season. The without project
yields for the major crops on partially irrigated land are 3.2-5.9 t/ha for
paddy, 1.2-1.3 t/ha for groundnut, and around 100 t/ha for sugarcane, based on
observed present yields in each of the different schemes. The wide yield range
for paddy reflects the differences in yield levels from scheme to scheme (Table
9). Cropping patterns are established for each scheme based on information
provided by WRO (Table 10). At full development it is assumed that:
(i)   The cropping pattern does not change when farmers fully irrigate
their previously only partially irrigated land. Even though a change



- 246 -
Annex 14
Page 8 of 11
to higher value crops could be expected, this aspect has not been
included;
(ii) Rainfed crops are grown on 50% of the non-irrigated land, the
remainder being left fallow (the gap). This assumption is based on
national statistics and field observations;
(iii) Irrigation benefits in stage 1 schemes consist of both a reduction of
the gap and a shift from partial to full irrigation. Irrigation
benefits in stage 2 schemes only consist of an increase from partial
to full irrigation. This division was selected to account for the
previous investments under the NWMP in stage 2 schemes;
(iv) In all schemes, about 30% of the presently irrigated CCA moves from
partial to full irrigation. This estimate has been given by WRO
engineers and can be described as cautious compared with the first
monitoring results of NWMP; and
iv)   Yields, hired labor and fertilizer use increase by 10% with full
irrigation as compared to partial irrigation. The yield increases
are based on realistic estimates of TNAU economists.
28.   The economic analysis for the SIFT component is based on realistic
assumptions on the incremental irrigation benefits of the project. Especially,
the division of the irrigation benefits into two groups, partial to full
irrigation and gap to full irrigation gives a detailed and realistic assessment
of the actual benefits.
29.    esaults;  Modernization and rehabilitation of the analyzed 16 major schemes
would allow to increase irrigation from partial to full supplies on an area of
107,000 ha, and an additional 34,000 ha presently not receiving any water would
receive irrigation. After project implementation, annual irrigation benefits
would amount to Rs. 1,040 million at March 1994 prices. At full development
total foodgrain production of paddy, and groundnut would increase by 170,000 t
per year (Table 10).
30.   The ERR calculation is based on the same economic assumptions as the scheme
completions. The implementation period for most of the schemes is seven years
and incremental benefits are phased in, beginning with year 4. Determination of
construction costs is based on actual costing experience under NWMP which sets
limits on rehabilitation and modernization costs per hectare. A similar approach
has accordingly been used with the addition of 10% for physical contingencies on
civil works and vehicles, and 15% for all equipment. The type of works necessary
for modernization/rehabilitation does not require large amounts of civil works
and earthworks, nor major civil works which are subject to considerable cost
variations. Base cost for civil work average Rs. 5,300/ha for stage 1 and Rs.
7,900/ha for stage 2 investments. Project costs for this component consist of
the direct investment costs for the schemes, the farmer turnover component, the
Irrigation Management & Training Institute, operation and maintenance costs of
irrigation facilities (budgeted at Rs. 265/ha), and costs for maintenance of
equipment.
31.   The overall ERR for this component is 19% and reflects the expected high
returns from increasing efficiency of already existing schemes. The detailed
analysis for Kodayar, Vaigai Old Ayacut, and Lower Bhavani gives an ERR of 38%,
40%, and 24%, respectively. The component is especially robust to cost overruns



- 247 -
Annex 14
Page 9 of 11
as the sensitivity analysis for this component reveals. Nevertheless, as with
the scheme completions, construction costs should be monitored carefully.
Summary Results of the Economic Analysis
32.   The overall Economic Rate of Return (ERR) is based on the same economic
assumptions as the individual ERR's for the above described components. The
economic cash flows are presented in Table 11. Total quantified benefits only
consist of irrigation benefits of which at full development 25% and 75% can be
attributed to the scheme completions and modernization/rehabilitation components,
respectively.
33.   The overall ERR for the project is estimated at 17% and the net present
value in March 1994 prices at Rs. 1,990 million. The overall ERR is calculated
as the combined ERR for the project's two investment components, scheme
completions and modernization/rehabilitation/farmer turnover, which together
account for 84% of the project's base costs. Costs and benefits have not been
included for the project's water planning and environmental action plan as well
as institutional improvement expenditures not directly related to the two
production components. The expected benefits of the iatter components are
substantial but are difficult to quantify. If the costs for these items are
included, but without ascribing benefits, the overall ERR of the total project
would fall to 13%, and the net present value would be R3. 587 million. At full
development the project would achieve an incremental foodgrain production of
paddy and groundnuts of 180,000 t per year.
Sensitivity and Risks Analysis
34.   The table below presents the results of the sensitivity and risks analysis
covering increases in costs, decreases in benefits, and the switching values in
costs and benefits that would reduce cash flow to break even levels. For example
if costs increased by 20% the ERR for the overall project would fall from 17% to
14%. As expected, the SIFT component has higher returns than the scheme
completions component, which are reflected in both the base case and the various
scenarios. The switching values illustrate that benefits would have to fall by
30% or costs to increase by 43% for the ERR to fall to 12%. It is, however,
unlikely for implementation shortfalls of this magnitude to occur, for several
reasons:  (i)  estimates of costs are realistic (the SIFT component uses actual
costing experience of the NWMP and the scheme completion costs are based on
actual experience with on-going contracts); (ii) estimation of benefits was
similarly realistic and detailed, using actual farm performance information from
a statewide farm performance database (SIFT) or data compiled from WRO-FAO/CP
field visits (completions), and obtaining disaggregated estimates for
subcomponents of the project components; (iii) GOTN has achieved successful
piloting of the modernization and rehabilitation activities on a large scale
(153,000 ha) under NWMP, on which the extension to the larger WRCP program is
based; (iv) GOTN has demonstrated its commitment to the project's implementation
and sustainability through successful institutional and fiscal reorganization;
and (v) WRO has proven capability and successful experience in implementing the
kind of physical activities called for in the project.
35.   The table also includes results of risks analysis of three other scenarios,
i.e. (i) reduced implementation and realization of benefits, (ii) inadequate
maintenance beyond the project period, and (iii) shortfalls in the Systems
Improvement and Farmer Turnover component, which investigate their impact on the
economic rate of return.



- 248 -
Annex 14
Page 10 of 11
36.   Reduced imDlementation and realization of benefits.  While the risk of
shortfalls in implementation has been reduced through a variety of monitoring and
incentive mechanisms built into the project to help ensure this, it is
instructive to investigate the consequences of a delay in the implementation
process. Under this scenario, where it is assumed that benefits are lagged by 2
years, the ERR of the overall project would fall from 17% to 13%, the ERR of the
SIFT component would fall from 19% to 15%, and the ERR of the scheme completions
component would fall from 15% to 12%.
37.   Inadequate maintenance beyond the project period.  The table also tests for
a scenario that would develop if the WRCP's objectives to sustain full
maintenance (supported by improved cost recovery and farmer turnover) were not
continued beyond the project period, i.e. a historically relevant non-
sustainability scenario. This scenario is possible if funds for 0 & M fall short
of expectations after the project implementation period, resulting in
significantly reduced long term benefits. The sensitivity test therefore assumes
a scenario of gradually decreasing benefits, where: (i) the incremental benefits
of the modernization/rehabilitation component cannot be sustained after year 10
and progressively fall to zero in year 20 producing no incremental benefits
thereafter, and (ii) the incremental net benefits of the scheme completions would
drop from 100% in year 10 to 50% in year 30. Under these assumptions the ERR
would fall from 17% to 13% and the NPV would fall from Rs. 1,990 million to Rs.
350 million. This test illustrates the importance and the high pay-off of full
funding of adequate maintenance requirements, hence the attention paid to full
funding and vigorous monitoring of maintenance under the WRCP. GOTN funding of
maintenance was brought to full requirements in the April 1994 budget, and GOTN
has spelled out in its Letter of Development Policy its intentions for sustained
funding for maintenance functions.
38.   Shortfalls in the Systems Improvement & Farmer Turnover component.  The
potential exists for less than satisfactory completion of the SIFT component.
SIFT incorporates an innovative public-private partnership-that calls for new
kinds of skills and markedly different attitudes on the part of both government
personnel and farmers. In particular, management of farmer organizations are
intended to be autonomous and must no longer be subjected to bureaucratic or
political pressures. The inherent risk is that the bureaucracy may not easily
undergo the necessary transformation and may continue to exert influence on
farmer organizations. A scenario that tests for this possibility of incomplete
implementation of SIFT assumes: (i) a 2 year lag in benefits, (ii) a 30%
shortfall in benefits, and (iii) a 20% increase in costs. Under these
assumptions the ERR would drop from 19% to 13% for SIFT and from i7% to 14% for
the overall project. The NPV would fall from Rs. 1,652 million to Rs. 186
million for SIFT, and from Rs. 1,990 million to Rs. 524 million for the overall
project.

39.    The sensitivity and risks analysis above demonstrates the robustness of
both components in addition to the overall project, and confirms the soundness of
the proposed investment. The analysis further emphasizes the importance of
strict adherence to the project's implementation, monitoring and maintenance
guidelines. Close monitoring of implementation activities, continuation of
budgetary allocations for maintenance functions beyond project completion, and an
emphasis on improving the interaction between public and private institutions,
would ensure that the project yields the expected benefits.

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Criteria For Preparation and Selection of Investment
1.   Introduction.  This annex deals with the criteria to be utilized by WRO
in the preparation of future investment schemes that may be included in
the WRO's work program within the seven-year implementation period of
the WRCP. The WRCP investments are directed to rehabilitation and
modernization of irrigation schemes and completion of civil works
related to other schemes. The latter focus on improving the source of
supply of water to existing traditional irrigation infrastructure. On
the policy side, WRCP focusses on setting up and strengthening of
institutions dealing with irrigation and improving the water resource
utilization planning capabilities of GOTN. As discussed in other
sections of the SAR, Tamil Nadu is a water deficit state with most of
the current utilization of surface and ground water being in the
agriculture sector. With the growth in the industrial sector and the
need to provide the population with clean drinking water at a higher per
capita consumption rate, competing demands on this limited resource will
increase. Future schemes to meet these requirements in other sectors
would involve transfer of water from agricultural use. In order to meet
and maintain agricultural water requirements, additional schemes similar
to those supported under this WRCP may be required. These needs may
indeed arise over the next seven-year period as better basin plans are
prepared and water utilization projects are identified and executed in
other sectors. As a case in point, GOTN is currently preparing a water
supply project to be implemented by TWAD Board' to provide potable water
to the city of Madras which might involve transfer of water from
agricultural use to municipal use. In the future, WRO would be
responsible for providing bulk water supplies to municipal and
industrial sectors2. There are also other schemes in planning stages
similar to the nine scheme completions supported under this WRCP. in
order to allow for investment assistance from IDA for such developments,
an unidentified category has been established under the scheme
completions component. Approval by IDA and the inclusion of any such
future schemes under the WRCP would be dependant upon their satisfying
the set of criteria outlined below.
1/   Tamil Nadu Water Supply and Drainage Board.
2/   These schemes would deliver untreated water at bulk rates to the
municipal or industrial unit and therefore would involve only the
development of a source be it surface water or subsurface water or
both. Agencies such as TWAD Board and METROWATER will continue their
role in urban water supply and drainage.



- 276 -
Annex 15
Page 2 of 4
2.   Preparation and Selection Criteria.  These criteria include the
essential requirements that the schemes are: (a) prepared in the context
of a river basin development considering all water use needs; (b) well
prepared from all engineering, agricultural and other technical
perspectives; (c) economically viable; (d) in full compliance with
environmental and social requirements; and (e) identified and prepared
in full consultation with beneficiaries. The criteria for investment
scheme preparation and selection are:
I.     Basin Plan:  The scheme should be prepared in the context of a
river basin plan taking into account probable competing demands on the
water source to be developed; it should fit in an overall riverbasin or
subbasin development plan prepared by WRO.
II.    Water Demand:  Estimates of water demands should be prepared
taking into account the economic life of the proposed scheme. In the
case of municipal and industrial sectors such estimates would be
provided to the WRO by the responsible entities. WRO should ensure that
these estimates cover a projected service period and specify the degree
of reliability required. In the case of irrigation schemes, crop water
requirements should be prepared on the basis of climatic data using
standard methodology3 taking into account the contribution of effective
rainfall. In estimating these demands, soils and land suitability for
potential crops to be grown under the scheme should be established based
on data obtained from the agricultural department and future crops,
cropping patterns and crop calendars should be established in
consultation with farmer and agricultural department officials.
III.   Water Availability:  Detailed hydrological studies and reservoir
yield analysis using standard methodology for supplies at the degree
required. Quality of water supplied from the scheme must meet prevalent
standards for raw water supply to municipalities or water boards,
industries and agriculture as appropriate. In the case of groundwater
exploitation4 , analysis of aquifer capacity, dynamic recharge potential
for various probabilities, drawdown rates, pump capacities, availability
of power to operate the prime movers and cost of such power should be
estimated. In the case of irrigation schemes, wells in the scheme
command must be identified and studied to establish conjunctive use
which must be reflected in water balance computations. Analysis of
existing irrigation infrastructure to clearly identify possible measures
such as improving existing storages through dredging and improving
3/   Modified Penman-Monteith method is currently used by FAOCP to estimate
potential evapotranspiration rates.
4/   This possibility is included for any such future eventuality.  None of
the appraised WRCP investments involves investment in groundwater
explotation.



- 277 -
Annex 15
Page 3 of 4
efficiency of delivery systems should be prepared and the necessity to
build new storages should be clearly established.
IV.    Engineering:  Detailed engineering designs and implementation
schedules, based on WRO capability and optimal contract packages, should
be prepared.
V.     Operation and Maintenance Plan:  Detailed 0 & M plan for the
scheme should be prepared clearly spelling out the role of the end user
in these and the financial and technical inputs required on a recurrent
basis with an indication of how these requirements would be met.
VI.    Environmental Assessment:  Necessary environmental impact studies
should be prepared and mitigating measures if required should be
identified and an action plan to implement such measures prepared. If
the scheme involves building dams higher that 10m, designs would have to
be presented to State DSRP and their concurrence obtained. Riparian
rights should be assessed as well as impact on downstream users. In the
event of a downstream user being affected, a counterimpact scheme should
be prepared or suitable remedial measures be identified and included in
the development of the scheme.
VII.   Resettlement and Rehabilitation:  Detailed action plan for the
economic rehabilitaion of scheme-affected persons should be prepared
together with an implementation schedule and costed. Baseline
socioeconomic surveys should be conducted to assist in this process. In
the preparation of above, both reservoir area and pipeline or other
delivery mechanism route should be included. Land acquisition and
economic rehabilitaion plans together with realistic schedules for such
acquisition and economic rehabilitation should be prepared.
VIII. Costs and Benefits: Detailed costs for the engineering works
should be prepared taking into account prevailing contract rates and
optimal contract packages. The total costs should include LAER costs
arising from the environmental impact assessment. Annual recurrent
costs based on the 0 & M plan should also be included. In the case of
irrigation schemes an estimate of annual net agricultural benefits
should be made taking into account existing agricultural support
services and an analysis of the markets for the agricultural produce .5
A detailed farming systems study should be carried out and the financial
impact of the scheme on the beneficiary farmers be estimated.
IX.    Economic viability:  Detailed economic analysis should be carried
out using standard methodology (refer FAOCP reports for nine schemes).
The economic rate of return should be 12% or greater. Social factors
such as poverty alleviation, regional development etc., which could
5/   Unquanitified benefits (e.g. environmental, or health benefits from
domestic water supply schemes) may also be documented, and where
feasible, benefits ascribed.



- 278 -
Annex 15
Page 4 of 4
influence choice where ranking of different investment options are
similar, may also be documented.
X.     Cost Recovery:  For bulk water supply schemes, establishment of
cost of water for each scheme and agreement with the recipient on
financial terms of supply. Legal agreement as needed should be
prepared. For irrigation schemes, terms of cost recovery and farmer
responsibilities should be agreed through memorandum of understanding.

Annex l
Page 1 of 4
INDTA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJTECT
Maintenance. Water Charges and Cost Recovery
1.    As a result of recent initiatives, Tamil Nadu has substantially improved
its situation as regards funding of maintenance and actual and prospective
cost recovery. Most important, GOTN is now fully funding maintenance
expenditure needs. Second, water charges have been increased and now cover
maintenance expenditure and the bulk of other recurrent costs. GOTN is also
examining additional ways of improving water sector financing provisions and
revenues, and the project would further assist in this process.
2.    Maintenance Funding.  In the past, Tamil Nadu has chronically
underfunded irrigation maintenance, resulting in progressive deterioration of
infrastructure. Recognising the need - pervasive in almost every Indian state
- to substantially increase funding for maintenance works, GOTN has
progressively increased its allocations for operations and maintenance over
the last three years including an informal committment to IDA made in 1993 at
the beginning of project preparation that maintenance funding would be at full
required levels by project commencement and in all future years. From a
funding level for operations and maintenance averaging only Rs 103/ha in
1989/90, GOTN increased O&M funding to Rs 120/ha in 1992/93, Rs 200/ha in
1993/94 and Rs 265/ha for the 1994/95 fiscal year. The 1994/95 allocation
comprises a separate allocation to "maintenance works" (civil works,
maintenance contracts and materials) of Rs 150/ha (the remaining Rs 115/ha
being for staffing and other non-works recurrent costs). This follows the
recommendations of PWD/WRO engineers and the Bank, and represents the full
estimated requirement for maintenance works (94/95 prices) for the 1994/95
season.
3.    An important detail in Tamil Nadu's Non-Plan funding provisions to WRO
is that funding is disaggregated and earmarked. "Maintenance Works" have
separate budget heads from establishment and other recurrent costs, and funds
for "works" go into separate bank accounts, earmarked for works contracts and
materials, with the relevant local branches of the State Bank of India. This
desirable situation is being increasingly adopted by Indian states and will
continue in Tamil Nadu. A common situation, applicable in Tamil Nadu also
until recently, is that overall Non-Plan allocations are barely enough to
cover staff salaries. As in such situations staff salaries have always had
first call on funds, little has been left for actual maintenance. As staff
salaries can be assumed payed, protecting the maintenance works budget is the
critical need for sustaining water service infrastructure. The other needs
are farmer pa-rticipation (non-breakages of structures, farmer turnover for the
critical and most usually neglected lower levels of irrigation systems) and
better planning, management and technical capability for O&M directorates of
irrigation departments.
4.    Water Charges.  Tamil Nadu's water charges for irrigation are made up of
a "basic irrigation rate', an "irrigation cess" which is calculated as a
