Annex 16
Page 2 of 4
multiple of the basic rate and an "additional irrigation cess", also a
multiple of the basic rate. The basic rate, and the resultant overall water
charge through the cess multipliers, is calculated by crop, soil type and
season and presently has over a hundred rates. Through adjustments of the
cesses, GOTN has progressively increased charges for irrigation, the most
recent adjustment being in 1991. The typical rate now amounts to between Rs
200-210/ha, amongst the highest water charges in India. The present water
charges more than cover expenditure needs for maintenance works (Rs 150/ha in
1994/95), and cover about 80% of all recurrent expenditure costs of WRO
including establishment (both staff employed on O&M and other staff).
5.    GOTN has also applied a policy of freezing all recruitment to PWD, and
envisages continuing this for WRO until staff numbers are balanced to work
needs. This will bring establishment costs down further, and the farmer
turnover program under the project will allow continued decline in O&M staff
requirements as farmers assume greater responsibilities.
6.    For commercial and industrial bulk supplies from WRO, rates have also
been increased. From very low previous levels, both industrial and
commercial, rates were increased in 1986, and then subjected to a further
increase of eightfold in 1991: from Rs 60/1000 cubic meters to Rs 500/1000
c.m. A temporary court order injunction has since resulted in rates being
brought down to Rs200/1000 c.m. This is still below service costs but is well
above typical rates in other Indian states. Supplies for drinking water are
supplied free of charge. Major drainage and flood protection works are also
supplied free.
7.    Further initiatives are expected from the work of GOTN's "Water Rates
Rationalization Committee". This is examining agricultural rates. Based on
the interim conclusions of the committee, likely recommendations will be a
significant simplification of the present array of rates; from over 100 to
less than 20. The committee is also examining ways of equitably increasing
overall revenues. Final recommendations are expected in 1995.
8.    The WRCP would further build on these initiatives.  Full and timely
provision of maintenance works funds by GOTN, a matter already agreed (and
implemented for the 1994/95 year, see above) would be confirmed at
negotiations and be the subject of a closely monitored assurance. "GOTN
would: (a) provide annually to WRO the full funds required for maintenance to
sustain the infrastructure in full operating order; (b) funds would continue
to be allocated to a separate and protected maintenance works budget exempt
from withdrawals for salaries and wages; (c) the maintenance works budget
would be available from the beginning of the fiscal year so as not to impede
maintenance activities; and (d) WRO would submit its annual budget estimate
for maintenance to GOTN and IDA based on detailed command-by-command estimates
following an agreed reporting, programming and budgeting and monitoring and
evaluation process" (Annex 4).
9.    on the revenues side, an important need is to have a permanent
institutional apparatus for review of the costs of water services and the



- 281 -
Annex 16
Page 3 of 4
revenues received, and for annual review of status and possible adjustments.
In Tamil Nadu's case, industrial, commercial and agricultural rates have all
been the subject of recent increases, and intent for further change in
agricultural rates is evidenced by the present work of the Water Rates
Rationalization Committee. This, however, is not a permanent body and is
restricted in mandate to agricultural rates. The fact of recent price changes
and the work of the committee is nevertheless welcome relative to situations
in many states where rates have not been changed for many years, in some cases
for over a decade. While political constraints are present, an important
contributing factor is the absence of any mechanism and responsible party to
annually review and make recommendations on service costs and fees. As change
is unpopular, there is little incentive for any party to propose adjustments,
and understanding of the disaggregated costs and revenues of water service
provision is also often incomplete. GOTN, thus intends to establish a
permanent "Water Service Costs and Revenues Committee". This would be chaired
by the Secretary, PWD/WRO with membership comprising Secretaries Finance,
Revenues Department, Land Administration, Agriculture, Municipal
Administration, TWAD, Metrowater and WRO's EIC and Basin Managers, and a co-
opted Consultant economist. The consultant would, under the direction of the
committee, prepare an annual status report covering the service costs
(recurrent and taking account of capital recovery) of all water services
(irrigation, drainage, flood control, rural drinking water, municipal,
industrial and commercial), fees applied and revenues, with regional
disaggregation as appropriate (for instance by basin/major water source and
major urban centers), and disaggregated and overall net revenues-costs
situation. The annual report and any recommendations forthcoming from the
committee would be distributed annually for consideration by state authorities
(Cabinet and Bureaucracy). WRCP funds would cover the costs of Tamil Nadu's
consultant over the life of the project, and the costs of an additional short-
term consultant employed in PY1 to help establish a computerised and
updateable multi-sectoral model.
10.   The WRCP's Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover Program will offer an
opportunity to further improve on the financial situation of service provision
for agricultural use. Cost recovery for irrigation has been impeded India-
wide by the frequently low quality of the service. Poor irrigation and lack
of involvement of farmers in irrigation operational plans, water management
decisions and management and maintenance of irrigation leaves a disaffected
farming community, lack of a sense of "ownership" in the irrigation system and
resistance to water charge increases, usually seen as a tax unrelated to
irrigation and with no correspondence to service quality.
11.   The WRCP's Systems Improvement and Farmer Turnover program will include
the following actions impacting on service costs and revenues. On the
investment side, for "On-Farm-Development", large savings will accrue from
state withdrawal to the role of topographic survey and extension, with
construction limited to provision of outlet and profile wall structures, and
with farmers rather than WRO or AED now handling water channels, field
channels and field drains. State investment costs at this level would come
down from Rs 3500/ha to Rs 500/ha. For distributaries lining, investmet would



- 282 -
Annex 16
Page 4 of 4
now involve a 20% contribution from farmers. Lining has usually been the most
expensive item under modernisation programs for the secondary distribution
system, and has often been excessively applied, especially where groundwater
recharge would be beneficial. By establishing farmer contributions for this
item, generally less essential than the water control structures, cost savings
would result both from the contributions from farmers and from reduction in
the demand for lining, which will be substantially reduced to reaches where
farmers consider it to have significant benefits. On the recurrent
expenditure side, members of farmer councils would make an up-front
contribution of Rs 250/ha to their bank account and would take over
maintenance and operations of their distributaries under the Memorandum of
Understanding established with WRO. This would reduce WRO's notional
maintenance costs by about 30%, though its overall effect would be gradual in
line with progress under the turnover program.
12.   Measures beyond the above would also be considered by the Water Service
Costs and Revenues Committee. First, a service fee increase (conceptually
like a betterment levy) could be considered for stage II investments. Second,
the current practice of all rural revenues being collected by the Revenues
Department could adjust to collection by Farmer Councils. A limited objective
could be for Farmer Councils being allowed to collect, still pass on to the
Revenues Department, but retain a percentage as collection reward, with higher
percentage retention against higher collection rates (actual collections
divided by dues). A relatively easy adaptation would be for Farmer Councils
to be charged volumetrically (measuring devices are part of the package for
monitoring of water flows and the physical means would thus already be
present). This is a highly likely evolution as experience is gained under the
program. More fundamentally, GOTN should look towards going beyond
internalising O&M costs and revenues at distributary levels to achieving the
same objective at the levels of entire commands and at state level; ie
progression towards a "utility" approach, with funds collected and used
directly by the service provider. Funds could be collected by Councils,
agreed proportions passed on to the command level Apex Committees, and
proportions handed on to the WRO bank account for the command concerned, with
part handed on to WRO overhead expenses, with revenue-sharing related to costs
of each entity. As regards management of O&M, farmer responsibility reaching
beyond the level of distributary head, and perhaps to whole command (below the
headworks) is a possible evolution, particularly for medium commands where a
tradition of farmer organization is already strong. An objective under the
project will be to encourage and facilitate any such initiatives as
opportunities are identified. It is expected that at least some commands
would be ready for further initiatives within several years. In the immediate
time frame, however, the key need is to strongly establish the system
improvement and farmer turnover program and gain the confidence and
satisfaction of the farmers. On this basis, larger steps such as the above
would have a good foundation for their success.

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
WATER AND AGRICULTURE IN TAMIL NADU ECONOMY
Tamil Nadu is the southernmost state in the Indian Union
located between 6805' and 1334' north latitutdes and 76b14'
and S021' east longitudes and covering an area of 1.:0 lakh
Sq.Kms. which is about 4 percent of the total area of the
country. The state borders Andhrapradesh on the north,
Karnataka an the northwest and Kerala on the west, and in
the east by the Bay of Bengal and the Indian Ocean on the
south.
PHYSIOGRAPHY
Varied topographical features ranging from coastal
plains to high mountains are found in Tamil Nadu. The State
is divided into four physiogrpahic regions. The coastal
plain stretches about 1000 Kms., comprising of northern
plain (comprising Chungleput District and parts of South
Arcot, North Arcot and Tiruchi Districts), the Cauvery delta
(Thaniavur District and parts of Trichy, South Arcot and
Pudukkottai Districts) and the southern coastal plain
(shared by Ramnad, Tirunelveli and Kanyakumari Districts).
The eastern ghats comprise of hills, the Javadis,
Shervaroys, Kalrayans, Pachamalais and Kollimalais between
Palar and Cauvery river%.
The central plateau undulate between eastern and western
ghats 150--160 m in elevation with the Palghat Gap betwewn
This annex vas prepared by Institute for Niter Studies, Water Resources Organisation, Sovernsent of
Tasil Nadu.

Nilgiris and the Anamalai Hills on the west. The western
ghats comprise the higheut mountains of the Peninsula. The
Nilgiris located on the northern side of Palghat gap form a
compact area with a Summit 10Oevl of 1830 to 2440 m. To the
south of Palghat gap is the Anamalais zone of the highest
peaks of the Peninsula. Palani hills overlooking river
vaigai and Cardamom hills on the continuation of western
ghats.
The major rivwrs of the state' are Cauvery, vaigai
Tambraparani, Ponnaiar and Palar.
POPULATION
The state's overall population increased from 30.12
million in 1951 to 55.64 million in 19912. The annual
increase between 1981 to 19912 was 1.49/. As per 1991
census report the literacy rate is 54.61/. The density of
population per Sq.Km. is 428 against the National Level
of 257.  Of the total population of 55. 4 million in 1991,
36.61 million are in rural areas and 19.03 million in urban
areas synchronising with farm and industrial activities
respectively.

EMPLOYMENT
The occupational structure of population is an important
component of human resource assessment and also is an
indicator for development planning. In agricultural
operations, human labour contributes to the major unit of
operational cost.
The total principal workers under different occupation
constitute 22.97 million as per 1991 census reports. Of
this total, 5.73 million are cultivators and 7.B5 million
form agricultural labourers. Of the total working group of
22.97 million the cultivators alone constitute 24.9% (5.73
million) and the agricultural labourwr form 34.1% (7.85
million), indicating the maJor working group dependent on
agriculture.

capita income of Tamil Nadu at constant prices was lower
than the National level of 1721 and 1967 during the same
period of sixth and seventh plans respectively.

RAINFALL -
The rainfall in the state is highly variable. The state
receives rainfall both during the southwest monsoon and the
northeast monsoon. The amount of rainfall received during
the northeast monsoon is significantly higher than that of
southwest monsoon. The rainfall received during southwest
monsoon indicates a decreasing trend from north to south.



- 287 -
Annex 17
Page 5 of 19

depressions from the Bay of Bengal, the rainfall is highest
on the coast and decreases westwards. The following table 2

S0 I LS"
The soils of Tamil Nadu broadly fall into four major
groups (i.e.), ti) black uoila, (ii) rod %oils, (iii)
alluvial soils and (iv) laterites. Black soils cover an
area of 27.112 lakh hectares covering all the districts in
the state. These black soils are either shallow or deep
Lupto 5 meters. As these soils contain 30 to 50% clay they
crack in summer due to shrinkage on drying. These soils are
poor in organic matter, nitrogen and phospherous but rich in
potash and lime.
The red soils are the maJor type covering 57.460 L.Ha.
with five broad groups as red loam, red inf@rtile soil, red
sandy soil, thin red soil and deep red soil.  The red loam
occurs in the districts of Chingleput, North Arcot, SoLuth
Arcot, Salem and parts of Trichi, having medium depth with
good drainage. Red infertile soil occurs in the districts
of Trichi, Madurai and Ramnad having shallow, light gravelly
and stony nature. Rod sandy soil occurs in Madurai, Ramnad
and Trichi districts, with sandy loam texture, shallow in
depth, good drainage.
Thin red soils are found in Coimbatore and Madurai
districts and are shallow in depth. Deep red soils are
-found in parts of Coimbatore, Madurai, Ramnad, Tirunelveli
and Kanyakumari districts and these soils are deep and loamy
in texture.  Alluvial soils occupy an area of 2.159 L.Ha.
This is classified as saline coastal alluvium and river
allu)vium. The saline coastal alluvium appears along the
coast as a narrow belt in the districts of Chingleput, South
Arcot, ThanJavur, Ramnad, Tirunelvwli and Kanyakumari.  The
texture of these soils varies from sandy loam to sand.
The rivwr alluvium are the transported soils found in
deltaic areas of Tirunulveli districts. The major portion
of Cauvwry alluvium lies in Thanjavur district.  These soils
__ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _- -_ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _

havw texture varying from mandy loam to clay loam, laterite
soils constitute 2.114 L.Ha. and mainly found in Nilgiris
and othur hilly areau of huavy rainfall and vary in depth
depending on topography.
IRRIGATED AREA
As per 1990-91 statistics the total cropped area is
66,31,761 Ha. Out of this 29,93,880 Ha are irrigated.
The contribution of Irrigation towards the AgricLtlture
in Tamil Nadu is 44%.
Among the various sources of irrigation, surface water
through canals and tanks irrigate 52.6% of the cultivated
area while the ground water supply through well% and other
sources cover 47.4%.  The canal irrigation is important in
Cauvwry, Palar, Ponnaiyar, Amaravathi, Vaigai and
Tambaraparani commands. Tank irrigation is the major source
in the districts of Ramnad, TirLunelveli, Kanyakumari,
Chingleput and North Arcot. Well irrigation covers major
area on tho western part of the state. The following tables

DRAINAGE, WATER LOGGING AND SALINITY-
The rivers in this state generally flow from west to
east.. Cauvery systwm is the major one that drains more than
one third of the state. The area to the north of Cauvary
basin is drained by the Palar, the Ponnaiyar and the Vollar
rivers. The area to the south of the Cauvery basin is
drained by the Vaigai, the Gundar, the Vaippar and
Tambaraparani. The drainage over greater part of Tamil Nadu
follows the pattern of branching of a tree. The total
drainage area in the state is 1.30 lakh Sq.Km. of which
Cauvery basin accounts for 0.4B7 lakh Sq.Km. followed by
Ponnaiyar (0.12 lakh Sq.Km.) and Palar (0.10 Lakh Sq.Km.).
Tamil Nadu is highly vulnerable to cyclonic depressions
and flooding. The depressions and cyclones are frequent
during the months of Octobwr, November and December (i.e.,
northeast monsoon) along the coast from Madras to Ramnad.
As these- depressions move along from east to west, extensive
flooding in the low lying areas af the rivwr basins occur.

SALINITY AND ALKALINITY t0
The growth and produLCtivity of crops are affected by
soils with high salt content. The soils containing wxcess
neutral soluble salts of chlorides and sulphates of calcium,
spdium and magnesium are saline soils and those soils
containing excess exchangeable sodium are alkali or sodic
soils. Saline soils are widely distributed in arid and
semi-arid and in seasonally dry coastal areas. Saline soils
occLur due to high salt content in the profile, saline ground
water and high water table, seepage from canals and higher
adjacent irrigated areas, poor surface and subsurface
drainage, irrigation with water of high salt content and
intrusion of sea water, occurrence of salt layer in the %oil
crust and rise of water table.
Alkaline soils have higher pH content (more than 6.0).
Acid soils have pH lass than 6.0.  These acid soils are
charctmristic of high rainfall areas and are duo to
laterization, intense leaching and marshy conditions.
In this state, saline and alkaline soils are spread over
in 4.115 Lakh Ha. while acidic soils occur in 1.231 Lakh Ha.
Districtwise distribution of these soils is given in

INDIA
TAMIL NADU WATER RESOURCES CONSOLIDATION PROJECT
Criteria for the Selection of Non-Government Organizations (NGOs)
The project would seek NGO participation in various activities related
to farmer organizations and beneficiary participation in management, operation
and maintenance of irrigation systems, and to land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation of project affected persons. Tamil Nadu's Water Resources
Organisation (WRO) would invite from interested NGOs detailed proposals for
collaboration. WRO would be responsible for reviewing the proposals and
agreeing on appropriate mechanisms to involve NGOs in the project. The
selection of NGOs would be guided by the following criteria:
(a) As part of legal requirements, the NGO should be a registered body
under the relevant State Law. It should regularly maintain its accounting
records and have properly audited annual statements of income and expenditure.
(b) Since the program involves working primarily with small and
marginal farmers, it is essential that the NGO has a proven track record in
community development in the agrarian setting. To avoid creating any biases
and misconceptions in the minds of the people, the NGO should be secular and
non-political in the conduct of its field activities.
(c) As the project requires NGO support for farmer organization
activities over many sites, and for land acquisition and economic
rehabilitation in seven completion sub-projects, NGO's should have adequate
numbers of field level staff conversant with the local dialect and customs,
and having a clear understanding of the socio-economic and political situation
of the area or areas in which they will operate. They should also possess
excellent communication skills and good rapport with the community so as to
involve them in their own farmer organization or economic rehabilitation.
(d) A prior understanding of the concept of Water Resources
Consolidation Project and experience in areas relevant to the NGO's interest
in project participation (for instance, in community development, farmer
organization, and land acquisition and economic rehabilitation) would be
desirable. Owing to the nature and extent of the physical coverage of the
program, each selected NGO will be part of the community organisation or land
acquisition and economic rehabilitation process. Hence, the NGO should be
able to interact smoothly with, and be a part of, a team.
(e) Finally, the NGO should have internal stability so as to assure
long-term support to the project.
